# Cairn — Design Brief

**Status:** Working draft
**Author:** [Author]
**Last updated:** 2026-05-28
**Document version:** 0.5

---

## About this document

This brief describes the architecture, scope, and operational plan for a secure-communications project aimed at users facing adversarial network environments and serious personal-safety risks. It is intended as the basis for external review, partnership conversations, and funding discussions. A full technical specification will follow, informed by feedback on this document.

The brief is structured to be readable by technical reviewers (cryptographers, security researchers), operational reviewers (NGO security trainers, journalists' digital security staff), and potential funders. It is roughly 15-20 pages when complete.

---

## 1. Executive Summary

_Purpose: a scannable orientation that lets a busy reader decide whether to engage with the rest of the brief, and points different reader audiences at the sections they specifically need to evaluate._

### 1.1 What Cairn is

Cairn is a secure-messaging system for people whose adversaries are willing to spend tens of thousands to millions of dollars per target to read their messages. The threat tier includes the audiences targeted by mercenary spyware (Pegasus, Predator, and similar commercial offerings), forensic-extraction operators (Cellebrite, MSAB, Magnet AXIOM, GrayKey), and traditional state intelligence services with legal and extralegal authority over telecom and platform operators (Section 2.1, Section 3).

The product is an integration of existing cryptographic primitives — SimpleX's identifier-less queue protocol; Briar's peer-to-peer-over-Tor design (joining in v1.5, subject to Briar's continued upstream maintenance); social recovery via Shamir Secret Sharing; a transparency-log-anchored trust graph — with substantial original cryptographic engineering at the integration boundary (capability-token construction, trust-graph operation envelope with cascade quarantine semantics, share format, recovery-flow memory hygiene per [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md) and [D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md)). The brief is the basis for external review, partnership conversations, and funding discussions; a full technical specification will follow.

### 1.2 The threat tier and audience

Three questions about audience are conflated in most product descriptions; the brief separates them (Section 2.2) so each can be evaluated independently.

- **Architecture-designed-against threat tier (broad).** Journalists, lawyers, organizers, dual-use technology workers, civil-society researchers, dissidents, undocumented organizers, abuse survivors with severed networks, queer and religious minorities in criminalizing jurisdictions, and others whose adversaries treat their communications as worth substantial resources to compromise.
- **Long-term addressable population.** The subset of the threat tier whose conditions match the architecture's commitments: GrapheneOS-on-Pixel hardware baseline, hardware-backed operational keypair, viable peer-recovery network, facilitator-supported provisioning.
- **v1 pilot audience.** A 10–15 user cohort in one or two local groups already known to the developer (Section 6.3). The four-precondition v1 intersection is a working estimate of low hundreds globally pending partner-outreach refinement (Q17); v1.5/v1.6 (Briar tier, non-peer recovery candidates per [D0014](decisions/D0014-non-peer-recovery.md)) and v2 (iOS, USB) lift the addressable audience materially.

Subsets of the threat-tier population the architecture does not serve well at v1, named explicitly:

- users whose primary adversary is co-located (the GrapheneOS-Pixel choice and provisioning ceremony are themselves visible signals to a co-located adversary);
- users without an available peer-recovery network — sex workers under criminalization whose peers face co-prosecution, abuse survivors with severed networks, isolated dissidents, undocumented organizers, queer people in criminalizing jurisdictions whose peers face the same prosecution (v1.x/v2 candidate paths per D0014);
- non-English-language populations until localization ships (Q19);
- users in jurisdictions where GrapheneOS-Pixel hardware is not available through standard commercial channels.

### 1.3 Architectural commitments

The product is structured as three layers — endpoint (GrapheneOS-on-Pixel running a Rust core plus a Kotlin UI shell per [D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md)); transport (Tor with pluggable transports tracking upstream); communications (SimpleX in v1; Briar joins in v1.5 subject to upstream maintenance per Section 7.1). Above the protocol layer, Cairn integrates four architectural commitments:

1. **Three-tier identity model** (master → operational → device-scoped capability tokens) that makes compromise bounded in scope and time during routine device operation. The reconstruction-window exposure (provisioning, recovery, rotation) and the undefended evil-maid case are named as residual surfaces in Section 5.1 rather than denied.

2. **Cryptographic trust graph** with five operation types (attestation, attestation withdrawal, key-compromise revocation, introduction, key rotation per [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md)). The withdrawal/compromise split addresses the cascade-laundering attack identified in the Section 5 adversarial review. Operations are anchored as commitment-only entries (hashes, not content) in Sigsum so issuer/subject/context stay out of public view.

3. **Social recovery without centralized trustees** via Shamir-among-peers (3-of-5 default) with pre-shared peer challenges plus a 48-hour delay-and-confirm window. The combination raises the cost of the unlock → peer enumeration → impersonation chain (per [D0005](decisions/D0005-peer-verification.md)); the recovery network surface in Section 3.3 is the acknowledged cost of refusing centralized trustees.

4. **Release-security posture aligned with comparable open-source security tools at v1, distinct from Signal Foundation's posture; v1.5 adds multi-party verification (per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)).** v1 ships developer Sigstore identity-based signing + Rekor transparency log + Sigsum-anchored release log with witness cosignatures + multi-channel distribution through F-Droid (primary), Accrescent, and direct download. The long-lived APK signing key persists as a residual single-credential surface per Section 5.5 (multi-release recovery via APK Signature Scheme v3 if compromised). The OIDC provider's jurisdictional posture (U.S.-based in v1 per Section 5.5 and Section 3.4) is retained as a trust placement rather than removed. The v1 stack is _not_ distinct from GrapheneOS's or Briar's application-layer posture; v1.5 adds reproducible builds + a recruited 5+/3-of-5 reviewer pool that together deliver multi-party binary-equivalence verification distinct from Briar's posture.

Several supporting components have substantial prior art (transparency-logged signing per Sigstore; reproducible builds per Briar and GrapheneOS; social attestation patterns from CONIKS and Keybase). Cairn's distinct contribution is the integration plus the original construction at the integration boundary plus operational discipline calibrated to this specific audience (Section 4.3).

### 1.4 v1 scope and roadmap

v1 ships SimpleX-only messaging on GrapheneOS-Pixel with the cryptographic engineering surface above, single-profile UI, source-code release security (with the source-vs-binary v1 supply-chain gap acknowledged in Section 5.5), and documentation-form post-coercion guidance. v1 is a closed pilot, not a public release.

**v1 ship-conditions** (all must hold; engineering-only sequencing is not sufficient):

- engineering scope completion (~9–12 person-months at "as available" volunteer-baseline cadence per [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md));
- pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md);
- partner-mediated consent and exit protocol per [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md);
- developer source review of v1 release candidates completes (per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md); the recruited 5+/3-of-5 reviewer pool defers to v1.5 alongside reproducible builds).

Subsequent releases (v1.5 architecture completeness; v1.6 deferred UX; v2 USB and iOS; v3 mesh-radio; v4+ established-organization tier) are described with phase-gate language consistent with Section 10.1's "as available" volunteer-baseline cadence and Section 10.8's "what unlocks what, not when" framing. The brief does not commit to calendar dates. v1.5 broader-than-pilot release is structurally gated on Phase C funding (pre-beta full audit; optionally reviewer-honoraria operating model per Section 8.2 — if honoraria funding does not close, v1.5 ships with whatever volunteer-attestation reviewer pool has formed through Q5 outreach per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)) and on completion of the foundation-incorporation trigger evaluation per [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) (the evaluation outcome may be to incorporate per [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md), to defer further, or to commit to fiscal-sponsor operation indefinitely — the decision is on the record at v1.5 broader-release planning rather than scheduled for a specific timeline). v2 iOS is a meaningfully different audience than v1's (no Briar tier; different release-security stack; App Store policy upstream dependency) per Section 7.1.

### 1.5 Operational and funding posture

Section 8 establishes operational commitments; Section 10 establishes the phase-gated funding model. The register is conditional by design:

- **Unilateral commitments** (what the project controls): documentation discipline (Section 9.4); Apache 2.0 license (Section 8.3); release-mechanism architecture (Sections 5.5, 8.2); disclosure policy (Sections 8.5, 9.4); source-from-day-one for successor recoverability (Section 9.4); dead-man's-switch operations per [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md).
- **Intent subject to conditions** for items dependent on funding, partner outreach, or external grant cycles. v1 implementation is solo — with the Section 8.1 specialist roles (cryptographic consulting, UX engineering, documentation/community) absorbed into the developer's working set at the volunteer baseline per Section 6.1, the concentration Section 9.1 names as the project's most concentrated risk.
- **Reviewer-pool recruitment** is conditional on Q5 (NGO partner outreach) at the volunteer-attestation baseline per Section 8.2; the volunteer-to-honoraria transition is conditional on Q3 (grant funding closes). Witness-pool composition and partner-organization arrangements are similarly Q5-conditional per Section 8.6.
- **Foundation incorporation** is deferred from v1 / Phase C scope with a v1.5 broader-release evaluation trigger per [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md). The [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md) jurisdictional analysis and legal-consultation framework persist as the substrate the project acts on _if_ the trigger activates. The default operational structure for the duration of the deferral is fiscal-sponsor arrangement (NLnet grantee-sponsorship preferred where mission fit allows; otherwise routing through Software Freedom Conservancy, Open Collective Foundation, or equivalent at 5–15% fee). Researcher Safe Harbor per [D0012](decisions/D0012-researcher-safe-harbor.md) is the project's published intent indefinitely under this framing, with formalization _if_ the trigger activates. Until then, Safe Harbor is unenforceable against future personal action, successors, or coercion-induced exceptions per Section 8.5. [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) partner advisory authority becomes the permanent governance scaffold under the deferral rather than a pre-incorporation transition state.

**The phase-gated funding model** (figures stated against the subsidy and rate-card landscape as of brief publication; gross of fiscal-sponsor fees of 5–15% during the pre-incorporation window per Section 10.8):

| Phase   | Range                                    | What it unlocks                                                                                                                                                                                                     |
| ------- | ---------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| Phase A | ~$1,500–3,500 absorbed                   | Volunteer baseline; v1 engineering proceeds "as available"                                                                                                                                                          |
| Phase B | ~$20,000–60,000 addressable              | Pilot readiness (pre-pilot audit; legal consultation; infrastructure)                                                                                                                                               |
| Phase C | ~$80,000–340,000                         | Broader-than-pilot release (pre-beta audit; optional foundation incorporation if [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) trigger activates; optional UX engineer; optional reviewer honoraria) |
| Phase D | ~$90,000–250,000/year ex-maintainer-comp | Steady-state operations; finite sustainability horizon per Section 10.4                                                                                                                                             |

Maintainer compensation at security-engineering market rates remains aspirational; the funding configuration that supports it has not closed (Section 10.4).

### 1.6 What this brief is and is not

The brief is the project's working map of architecture, scope, and operational plan for review by funders, partners, and technical reviewers. It is not a fundraising application, partnership agreement, or technical specification; it is the document those subsequent artifacts reference. The brief does not promise:

- Specific dollar amounts of funding will close, or specific timelines tied to funding events.
- That the project reaches Phase C or D; that named subsidy programs will be open or operating at the rate tiers cited when Cairn applies (the program landscape shifts on multi-year horizons; figures require re-baselining per Section 10.8).
- That the Phase A–D dollar ranges in §1.5 are net to the project — they are gross of fiscal-sponsor fees during the pre-incorporation window per Section 10.8.
- That v2 iOS or v3 mesh-radio extensions will materialize on any timeline, or that audience populations the architecture does not serve at v1 will be served at v1.x or v2.
- That GrapheneOS-Pixel hardware is available through standard commercial channels in every candidate-user jurisdiction; the BYOD posture per Section 10.1 depends on channel availability the project does not control, and the project's small loaner pool does not address jurisdictional channel closures.
- That the differentiation argument relative to existing portfolio peers (Briar, SimpleX, Tails, SecureDrop, CalyxOS) rests on validated field-interview evidence at this draft — the argument is currently structural; field-interview validation is Q18, named as the central grant-case question.
- A self-funding runway figure in calendar terms. Section 9.1 committed Section 10 to deliver this figure as "the financial floor of all v1 funding-related risk discussions"; this draft does not deliver it, and Section 10.7's Phase B funding-failure analysis is therefore bounded against an unstated horizon. Funders evaluating Phase A duration risk should request the runway figure directly from the developer.

### 1.7 Where to read next

The brief is organized so different reader audiences can navigate to the sections they specifically need to evaluate.

- **Funders evaluating fit.** Read Section 10 (phase-gated cost model and current pursuit posture in §10.9), Section 8.5 (audit budget per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md)), Section 2.3 (differentiation argument with the Q18 evidence caveat), Section 9 (risks). Engage at Q3 (honoraria funding), Q18 (differentiation evidence), Q21 (subsidy-program sequencing).
- **Partner organizations evaluating facilitation.** Read Section 2.2 (audience and named exclusions), Section 8.6 (partnership approach), Section 6.3 (pilot deployment), [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md). Engage at Q5 — partner outreach is open and the project is actively seeking these conversations.
- **Technical reviewers and cryptographers.** Read Section 3 (threat model), Section 4.3 (differentiation at the integration boundary), Section 5 (architecture detail), [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md) for envelope construction, Section 8.5 (audit scope). The most load-bearing decisions for technical review are D0006, [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md), [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md), and [D0014](decisions/D0014-non-peer-recovery.md); Appendix A.2 organizes the full register thematically.
- **Pilot candidates and facilitators.** Read Section 2.2 (audience and exclusions), Section 6.3 (pilot plan), Section 8.6 (facilitator model), [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md) (consent and exit protocol).

**No concrete fundraising ask is attached to this draft.** The brief is a basis for engagement before any specific application. Open questions Q1–Q26 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md) track unresolved items; Q5, Q17, Q18, and Q20 (self-funding runway disclosure) are the items where partner or funder input would be most directly useful. Engagement inquiries should reference the brief version (v0.2, May 2026); the changelog records substantive revisions across drafts.

---

## 2. Problem Statement and Audience

### 2.1 The problem

The problem this product addresses is a gap between consumer secure messaging and the operational reality of users whose adversaries are state-or-near-state-capable. Consumer messengers — Signal, WhatsApp, iMessage — are designed for casual privacy at scale: they raise the cost of bulk surveillance materially, they protect against opportunistic interception, and they assume an adversary whose interest in any single user is shallow. The user populations this product targets face adversaries whose interest in specific users is deep — adversaries who deploy mercenary spyware (NSO Group's Pegasus, Intellexa's Predator, the Cytrox / Saito Tech and Sourgum / Candiru lines, Memento Labs spyware, and similar offerings documented by Citizen Lab and Amnesty International Security Lab against journalists, lawyers, activists, and political opponents in Hungary, Greece, Poland, Spain, Saudi Arabia, Mexico, India, El Salvador, the United Arab Emirates, Thailand, and others), forensic-extraction operators (Cellebrite UFED and Premium, MSAB XRY, Magnet AXIOM, GrayKey) deployed at border crossings and detention facilities, and traditional state intelligence services with legal and extralegal authority over telecom and platform operators in their jurisdictions. These users live below the floor that consumer messengers were designed against; they live above the floor that tools built for adversaries paying tens of thousands to millions of dollars per target can recover from without operational discipline matching that investment.

The tools they need exist conceptually — secure messaging is a solved problem cryptographically — but the integrated tools they have available are not calibrated to the threat tier they actually face, and the partial tools they assemble themselves out of Signal-plus-OPSEC-training routinely lose to documented compromise patterns. The product addresses this gap: an integration of existing cryptographic primitives plus original construction at the integration boundary (capability tokens, trust-graph operation envelope, share format, recovery-flow memory hygiene — substantial original cryptographic engineering per Sections 8.1 and 9.3), shipped with operational discipline calibrated to the audience's actual operating conditions.

**Categorical failure modes of the existing landscape.** Consumer messengers' shortfalls cluster into three categories that recur regardless of which specific product is considered:

- _Identifier-as-vulnerability._ Most consumer messengers anchor identity in a phone number or email address. The identifier is the primary thing an adversary needs to compel telecom or platform cooperation against, to enumerate the user's contact graph, to correlate communication patterns across services. Section 3.3's Identity surface and Metadata surface together describe the consequence: an adversary who has the identifier has a footprint on the user's life that bounds what the secure-protocol layer can protect. Cairn inherits SimpleX's identifier-less queue model; the contribution above that inheritance is the integrated identity model, trust graph, and recovery flow that build on the identifier-less substrate.
- _Centralized service dependency._ Even when end-to-end encryption protects content, the service provider sees the timing, frequency, and recipient pattern of messages, and is subject to legal process in its operating jurisdiction. The threat model in Section 3.2 explicitly assumes adversaries with that legal reach.
- _Structural support for ongoing security operations absent from the design._ The tools are built for users who do not need to think about the threat. The threat tier this product addresses includes users whose work continuously interacts with the threat — what to send, what to delete, when to verify a new contact, how to recover if compromised. The honest framing of this gap is that meeting the threat tier carries an irreducible operational cost in the user's lived experience (cognitive load, communication latency, coordination work with recovery peers, periodic verification); the product's contribution is structural support that reduces the cost where it can (calibrated UX defaults; facilitator-supported provisioning; structured recovery flow), with the residual cost named honestly as something users absorb in exchange for the threat tier rather than positioned as user accountability for sustaining the architecture. Section 5.6 catalogs the structural-support components; the residual user-borne cost is named in Section 2.2's audience description and in [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md).

**What's at stake.** The consequences of communication compromise vary sharply by jurisdiction and circumstance. For some users in some contexts the realistic worst case is a fine, professional sanction, or job loss. For journalists covering corruption in jurisdictions where press freedom is contested (documented Pegasus targets in EU member states — Hungary, Poland, Spain, Greece — and in Saudi Arabia, Mexico, El Salvador, India), for activists whose coordination activity is itself the subject of state interest (documented Predator targets among Greek and Egyptian dissidents; documented Cellebrite extraction against environmental and Indigenous organizers in multiple jurisdictions), and for human-rights lawyers documenting torture cases (documented Pegasus targets among lawyers representing Saudi dissidents and Mexican human-rights defenders), the realistic worst case is arrest, imprisonment for years, and in some deployment environments torture or extrajudicial killing. The casework record of Citizen Lab, Amnesty International Security Lab, Access Now Digital Security Helpline, Front Line Defenders, and EFF Threat Lab documents the range of outcomes; Section 3.3's Stakes paragraph captures the upper end. The design's commitment is to the upper end of this range. The product is not appropriate for casual-privacy use cases where its operational discipline would be friction without justifying threat; it is appropriate where the threat tier matches the architectural commitments it makes.

The product is not an attempt to compete with Signal for mass-market secure messaging. It is an attempt to make available, to users at this specific threat tier, an integration of cryptographic primitives that already exist — SimpleX's identifier-less protocol, Briar's peer-to-peer-over-Tor design (v1.5 per [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md)), social recovery via Shamir, transparency-log-anchored trust graph — combined with substantial original cryptographic engineering at the integration boundary and operational discipline that makes the integration usable in the audience's actual operating conditions.

### 2.2 The audience

Three distinct questions are conflated in most product-audience descriptions: who is the architecture designed against; who is the long-term addressable population; who specifically can use v1 in pilot. The brief separates them explicitly so funders, partners, and pilot candidates can evaluate each one independently rather than reading product-marketing breadth claims that v1 cannot deliver.

**The threat tier the architecture is designed against (broad).** Cairn's design is calibrated to users whose adversaries deploy mercenary spyware (Pegasus, Predator, and similar commercial offerings), forensic-extraction operators (Cellebrite, MSAB, Magnet AXIOM, GrayKey), and traditional state intelligence services with legal/extralegal authority over telecom and platform operators. The named populations who face this threat tier across jurisdictions are documented in Section 2.1 and Section 3 and include — non-exhaustively — investigative journalists, human-rights lawyers, civil-society organizers and movement coordinators, dual-use technology workers, civil-society researchers, dissidents and their families, undocumented organizers, sex workers in jurisdictions criminalizing their work, abuse survivors with severed networks, queer people in jurisdictions criminalizing their identity, religious minorities under family or state surveillance, prisoners' families, low-income tenant organizers, and political opposition figures and their staffs. The threat tier itself is jurisdiction-agnostic — Pegasus targets in EU member states (Hungary, Poland, Spain, Greece) demonstrate that the threat is not confined to "authoritarian contexts" elsewhere; the architecture is designed against the threat, not against any specific national-political-system category.

**The long-term addressable population (scoped by the architectural commitments).** The architecture's commitments — GrapheneOS-on-Pixel hardware baseline; tier-separated identity with hardware-backed operational keypair; social recovery requiring a peer network; in-person facilitator provisioning at v1 (broader-deployment facilitation models in v1.5+ pending partner-organization arrangements per Section 8.6) — scope the population for whom Cairn is the right tool. Within the threat-tier population above, the architecture serves users who satisfy the architectural commitments. Significant subsets of the named threat-tier population face conditions the architecture does not serve well:

- _Users whose primary adversary is co-located_ (family, household, employer, partner): The GrapheneOS-Pixel posture, in-person provisioning ceremony, and recovery-peer designation conversation are visible to a co-located adversary. v1 is appropriate for users whose primary adversary is remote; co-located-adversary cases (a casework-dominant pattern documented at Front Line Defenders, Access Now Helpline, Tactical Tech) are not addressed by v1's posture and depend on out-of-band operational accommodations the brief does not promise.
- _Users without an available peer-recovery network_ (sex workers under criminalization whose peers face co-prosecution; abuse survivors with severed or hostile networks; isolated dissidents; undocumented organizers; queer people in criminalizing jurisdictions whose peers face the same prosecution; religious minorities under family surveillance whose peers are themselves the family network; prisoners' families): The architecture's response to peer compromise is a model that requires the existence of a non-compromised peer network — exactly what these populations lack. v1 Cairn is an inappropriate tool for these users until non-peer recovery ships, per [D0014](decisions/D0014-non-peer-recovery.md), which commits to v1.x/v2 candidate paths (paper shares; time-locked self-recovery; single-trustee with attorney-client privilege; explicit no-recovery option) for the populations v1 excludes. The architecture does not pretend to serve them at v1; the brief names the exclusion so the populations and their facilitators can make an informed decision.
- _Users without English fluency in the v1 deployment_: v1 ships in English. The architecture supports localization via the v1 i18n slot per Section 5.7, but v1 user-facing surfaces and facilitator documentation are English-only. The audience is therefore limited to English-comprehending users in the threat tier at v1; non-English-language populations (Tigrinya, Dari, Uyghur, Mandarin among diasporas; Arabic among MENA populations; and many others) are in scope for the threat tier and architectural-commitments population but out of scope for v1 pilot. Localization is a v1.5+ commitment tracked as Q6 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md).
- _Users who do not have or cannot acquire GrapheneOS-capable Pixel hardware_: v1 ships BYOD per Section 10.1 — pilot users source their own GrapheneOS-capable Pixel devices through standard commercial channels. Users without access to such channels in their jurisdiction are out of scope until v1.5+ hardware partnerships (deferred indefinitely under current funding posture per Section 10.5) or v2's USB form-factor reach them.

**The v1 pilot audience (specifically).** Beyond the architectural-commitments scope above, v1's pilot specifically can onboard 10–15 users in one or two local groups already known to the developer (per Section 6.3). The structural narrowing comes from facilitator capacity: at the volunteer baseline per [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md) and the per-user time commitment described in Section 6.3, one facilitator cannot sustain in-person provisioning ceremonies at higher throughput. The specific groups are pending Q4 conversations with candidate communities; pilot enrollment is gated on the partner-mediated consent and exit protocol per [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md) and on the pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit per Section 8.5 and [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md). _Estimated population size for the four-precondition v1 intersection (threat-tier; GrapheneOS-Pixel-capable; viable peer-recovery network; in-person facilitator reachable in the developer's geographic vicinity): low hundreds globally, with the v1 pilot itself reaching 10–15. Q17 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md) commits the project to refining this estimate through partner-organization outreach._ The narrowness reflects honest operational scope rather than the threat-tier population's actual size.

**Path from v1 pilot to broader audience reach.** Broader-than-pilot reach depends on three sequential developments, each named here so partners and pilot users see the dependency chain rather than infer it: (a) v1.5/v1.6 architecture completeness per Section 7.1 (which requires Phase C funding for the pre-beta audit and the [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) foundation-incorporation trigger evaluation; reviewer-honoraria operations are aspirational rather than gating per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)); (b) partner-organization facilitator networks per Section 8.6 (which depend on Q5 conversations not yet held); (c) the v1.5/v1.6 audience-expansion items (Briar tier; in-app post-coercion recovery; possible non-peer recovery candidate path per D0014). The narrowness of v1 is not the project's permanent target audience — it is the audience for whom v1 architecture can ship soundly at the volunteer baseline. The architecture-vs-audience honesty extended through Section 4.2 calibration and D0014 means the brief does not claim audience breadth v1 cannot deliver.

**Audience expansion through the roadmap (qualified per §7.1).** Subsequent releases extend the addressable audience within the architectural-commitments scope. The v1.5/v1.6 split (per the §§8/9 review F9 and §7.1) replaces the earlier single v1.5 envelope:

- **v1.5** adds Briar as the highest-sensitivity tier and reproducible Android builds with reviewer attestations transitioning from source review to binary-equivalence verification (per Section 7.1 ship-conditions). Users in the v1 audience benefit from the additions; audience shape itself does not materially change.
- **v1.6** adds multi-profile UX, the in-app first-class post-coercion recovery flow, the duress-wipe pattern, and may include voice/video, localization, and v1.x non-peer recovery candidate-path evaluation per D0014. Localization is the audience-expansion lever per Q6 and Q19; the duress-wipe pattern serves users for whom security-conscious-device-choice is itself a tell, reconciling §2.2 above's named exclusion (those users are excluded from the v1 audience as architecturally designed; v1.6 with duress-wipe specifically extends the architecture toward serving them).
- **v2** adds the USB-bootable form factor and iOS support per Section 6.2 and §7.1. v2 iOS serves a different audience than v1's per §7.1's iOS-dependency honesty (no Briar tier on iOS; different release-security stack; App Store policies as upstream dependency); the brief does not present v2 iOS as "the same Cairn for more users."
- **v3** adds mesh radio integration for internet-shutdown scenarios per §7.1's mesh-dependency conditions.
- **v4+** adds an established-organization tier per §7.1 as a foundation-governance milestone, not just a product-tier expansion.

The product does not target consumer secure-messaging users at any version. The audience is explicitly the population whose adversaries treat their communications as worth substantial resources to compromise; the architectural cost of meeting that threat tier — GrapheneOS dependency in v1, in-person provisioning, peer-based recovery, the structural support for ongoing security operations that real users in the threat tier need a system for — is calibrated to that population and disproportionate for users at lower threat tiers.

### 2.3 Gap in the existing landscape

The cryptographic primitives this product relies on are not novel; the protocols it integrates are not novel; even the operational patterns it surfaces — social recovery, capability tokens, transparency-logged attestations — are individually well-traveled. The contribution is original construction at the integration boundary (Section 4.3) plus integration of existing pieces, surfaced through operational discipline calibrated to the audience. The survey below traces what each major product in the landscape does well and where each falls short for the threat tier in Section 3, then identifies what Cairn adds. The survey is calendar-dated to brief publication (May 2026); each product's roadmap continues to move and the brief acknowledges that the differentiation argument requires re-baselining over time. Section 5.4 contains the protocol-level comparative analysis; this section is at the product-and-audience level.

**Caveat on the differentiation argument.** The brief asserts that the integration is what existing projects do not deliver for this audience. The assertion currently rests on the structural argument below rather than on field-interview evidence with practitioner staff at the partner organizations Cairn intends to engage. Q18 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md) commits the project to surfacing whatever field-interview or partner-consultation evidence the project assembles before grant submission; until that evidence is in hand, sophisticated funders should treat §2.3 as the project's working analysis rather than as validated landscape research. If conversations with partner organizations during Q5 outreach demonstrate that an existing project (most likely Briar or SimpleX) could deliver Cairn's audience-facing claims through their own roadmap with appropriate UX and integration funding, the brief commits to reconsidering whether Cairn is the right vehicle versus contributing to an existing project's track — a reframing the §2 adversarial review identified as the central grant case question.

**Signal.** Strongest cryptographic protocol in the consumer-messaging space; mature client implementations across mobile and desktop; broad adoption that reduces the metadata signal of using it. Signal Foundation (US Delaware 501(c)(3)) ships usernames as a stable feature since February 2024 and Sealed Sender for metadata-protection; phone-number-at-registration persists and Signal Foundation has publicly defended this as a documented anti-abuse tradeoff rather than an unaddressed gap. The Signal Protocol itself is reused or referenced by most of the other tools listed below. Where Signal falls short for this audience: phone-number-at-registration is a forcing function for telco-infrastructure dependence the threat model treats as compromised even with Sealed Sender mitigating downstream metadata; centralized service operation through Signal Foundation is a pressure point for legal process; the audience is scoped to consumer privacy rather than the threat tier this product addresses. Cairn's contribution relative to Signal is the cleaner identity-not-tied-to-phone-number choice (no phone number at any layer including registration), the integrated trust-graph above messaging, and the multi-party release-security stack; Cairn's relationship to Signal is one of cryptographic descent — many design patterns trace back to the Signal Protocol or its forks — rather than feature competition.

**Matrix and Element.** Federated architecture with end-to-end encryption available; strong ecosystem of clients and bridges; popular among technically sophisticated users. Matrix has shipped multiple protocol-level mitigations and is actively migrating to MLS (RFC 9420) with implementations shipping in Element X through 2024–2025; Matrix-the-project treats the cross-client state-disagreement complexity as a known design problem with active mitigation work. Where Matrix falls short for this audience: federation pushes the metadata-disclosure problem to the per-user homeserver, which becomes a target for legal process or compromise in its own jurisdiction; the federation surface remains a structural metadata exposure even as the protocol-level cross-client work matures; cross-server federation tends to increase rather than decrease the metadata visible to network observers. Cairn does not federate.

**Wickr.** AWS sunset Wickr Me, the only non-enterprise consumer tier, on December 31, 2023; only AWS Wickr (paid enterprise) and AWS Wickr for Government remain. The Wickr Me sunset itself is evidence supporting the centralized-service-operation thesis that AWS ownership creates structural risk for civil-society users — a thesis Cairn's release-security stack (Section 5.5) is structured to address by exposing provenance Wickr's closed-source enterprise model does not. As a current comparator for this audience, Wickr is effectively absent: the non-enterprise tier no longer exists, and the enterprise tier was never operationally available to the populations Cairn targets.

**SimpleX.** Identifier-less queue-based messaging; no persistent user identity at the protocol level _in the v1 single-device configuration_ (multi-device introduces correlatable activity at queues — Section 5.4); forward-secret and post-compromise-secure double-ratchet derivative; self-hostable servers; current product ships contact/group/profile model, preset-server UX, and reliability improvements through 2024–2026. Cairn uses SimpleX as the v1 messaging substrate (per Section 5.4); the protocol itself is one of the right tools for this threat tier and the SimpleX team's roadmap aligns substantially with what the audience needs at the protocol layer. Where SimpleX as a standalone product falls short of what this audience needs: no integrated identity model above the protocol that propagates trust across the user's social network (each conversation requires out-of-band connection establishment with no overlay structure); no trust-graph for attestation propagation; no integrated social-recovery flow; release-security relies on the SimpleX team's own infrastructure rather than the multi-party-attestation pattern Cairn's threat tier requires. Cairn's contribution above SimpleX is integration: identity-trust-recovery on top of the identifier-less queue substrate.

**Briar.** Pure peer-to-peer over Tor; no servers; refined through years of use by journalists and activists in hostile environments. Briar's actual trust model includes the introduction protocol (mutual contacts cryptographically introducing two users), BQP (Bramble Question Protocol) for shared-secret verification, and forums/private groups with membership cryptographically rooted in introducer signatures — small-scale graph-shaped trust without a transparency log. Briar Mailbox (stable since 2023) materially changes the integration surface: store-and-forward for offline contacts that addresses one of the named integration gaps. Where Briar as a standalone product falls short of what this audience needs in v1: it owns identity, storage, and lifecycle in ways that make integration into a multi-protocol product non-trivial (D0004 records the engineering cost of binding Briar's identity model to Cairn's); the user-facing onboarding flow is designed for one-protocol use; the recovery model is Briar-specific (account export plus optional Mailbox) and does not provide the social-recovery integration across multiple protocols Cairn aims for. The protocol is correctly chosen for what it does; the product Briar ships is not what this audience needs as a primary tool. Cairn integrates Briar as the highest-sensitivity tier in v1.5 (per Section 5.4, [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md), and §7.1 ship-conditions including Briar's continued upstream maintenance).

**Session.** Decentralized messaging that migrated off the Signal Protocol to its own Session protocol in 2023 (motivated by the multi-device problem Session's onion-routed delivery created for Signal Protocol's ratchet state); uses the Session Network as transport. The migration itself supports the dependency-trajectory concern the brief raises: Session's cryptographic stack evolution is determined by the Session project on a timeline that may not align with the security posture the audience requires. Where Session falls short for this threat tier: the Session Network is itself an external dependency the project does not control and whose long-term operational trajectory is uncertain; no integrated trust-graph or social-recovery story.

**Cwtch.** Peer-to-peer over Tor, no servers, designed for the same threat tier as Briar but with an untrusted-group-server pattern (Cwtch's distinguishing contribution relative to Briar's pure peer-to-peer model). Smaller deployed footprint and ecosystem. Cwtch was considered as an alternative to Briar in Section 5.4's protocol selection; remains a candidate for future evaluation. The same gap applies as for Briar: a strong protocol shipped as a standalone product rather than as an integrated tool the audience can adopt without assembling the operational discipline themselves.

**Threema.** Swiss-jurisdictional, identifier-less (ID-not-phone-number) paid product used by parts of the Swiss government and several European militaries for unclassified communications. Threema is the closest existing analogue to Cairn's identifier-less, jurisdiction-aware, paid-and-deliberate positioning. Where Threema falls short for this audience: centralized service operation by a single Swiss company is a pressure point for legal process (with stronger jurisdictional protections than US-based comparators but not absolute); the paid-product model creates economic friction for grassroots civil-society users; closed-source client and server reduce the auditability that Cairn's release-security stack (Section 5.5) addresses; no integrated trust-graph or social-recovery integration. Cairn shares the identifier-less and non-US-jurisdiction-considerations posture; Cairn's contribution above Threema is open-source-auditable release security, the social-attestation trust graph, and the integration with peer-to-peer-over-Tor protocols (SimpleX, Briar) Threema does not use.

**Wire and Olvid.** Wire (Swiss/German MLS-based; used by EU institutions and some defense contexts) and Olvid (French identifier-less messaging used by parts of the French government) are both reasonable comparators for "non-US-jurisdictional, identity-not-tied-to-phone-number" positioning. Both fall short for Cairn's audience on the same axes Threema does: centralized service operation; closed or partially-closed source; no integrated trust-graph or social-recovery integration.

**Tails, SecureDrop, OnionShare, and CalyxOS.** Comparators a sophisticated funder portfolio reads as adjacent to Cairn. _Tails_ is the canonical amnesic-Linux distribution for journalist and activist use, with overlap into Cairn's audience; Tails serves session-bounded operations (no persistent identity across sessions; no integrated messaging beyond what the user brings) and is the right tool for users whose threat model centers on observable persistent state rather than continuous messaging. Cairn is the inverse of Tails: persistent cryptographic identity with structural protection of that identity across sessions, calibrated to ongoing-messaging audiences. _SecureDrop_ (Freedom of the Press Foundation) is the canonical funded tool for journalist source protection — an inbox-style system for sources to deliver materials to journalists anonymously, not bidirectional messaging. SecureDrop and Cairn solve adjacent but different problems for overlapping audiences; SecureDrop is correctly chosen for source-protection workflows, Cairn for ongoing identity-bearing communication. _OnionShare_ is a peer-to-peer file-sharing tool over Tor used by the same audience; OnionShare and Cairn solve different problems (file-sharing vs ongoing messaging) for overlapping audiences. _CalyxOS_ is an alternative Android-hardening distribution that competes with GrapheneOS for v1's device-baseline argument; the brief uses GrapheneOS per Section 3.4 and Section 5.5 because of the more aggressive security-hardening posture, but acknowledges that the GrapheneOS-only requirement narrows v1's audience more than a GrapheneOS-or-CalyxOS posture would. Q26 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md) tracks whether v1.x relaxes the GrapheneOS requirement to include CalyxOS for users in jurisdictions where GrapheneOS adoption is operationally riskier.

**The gap Cairn fills.** Across this landscape, the protocols and primitives this audience needs largely exist; the supporting infrastructure for the release-security stack (reproducible builds, transparency-logged signing) has substantial prior art (Section 4.3 acknowledges this explicitly). What does not exist for this audience is an integrated product that combines the cryptographic substrates plus original construction at the integration boundary (capability tokens, trust-graph operation envelope with cascade quarantine, share format, recovery-flow memory hygiene) with operational discipline calibrated to the threat tier:

- A coherent identity model across protocols, with tier separation that bounds compromise in scope and time rather than total (Section 5.1).
- A trust graph that propagates attestation, attestation withdrawal, key-compromise revocation, introduction, and key-rotation operations across the user's social network, anchored in commitment-only entries in a transparency log (Section 5.2 per [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md)).
- A social-recovery flow that works without centralized trustees, with peer-verification mechanisms that raise the cost of the unlock → peer enumeration → impersonation chain ([D0005](decisions/D0005-peer-verification.md)). Excluded audience populations are named explicitly with v1.x/v2 candidate paths per [D0014](decisions/D0014-non-peer-recovery.md).
- A release-security stack that substitutes a distributed trust set (5-of-N reviewer attestation; witness pool; OIDC jurisdiction) for the developer's single signing identity rather than eliminating trust placement (Section 5.5 and §4.3).
- A UX surface that surfaces these properties to non-expert users through familiar messaging idioms (Section 5.6).
- An honest threat model that names what is and is not protected, including the residual surfaces the architecture cannot close (Section 3).

Cairn's contribution is integration plus original construction at the integration layer (acknowledged in §8.1 and §9.3 as substantial original cryptographic engineering). The underlying protocols themselves are correctly delegated to projects (SimpleX, Briar, Tor, Sigstore, Sigsum) whose mission is to build and maintain them. The project's mission is the integration boundary plus the trust graph plus the operational discipline for the specific threat tier this brief describes.

---

## 3. Threat Model

### 3.1 An illustrative framing

Consider a journalist documenting government misconduct in a country where the press has been increasingly targeted, who is detained at a border crossing and whose phone is held for forensic examination for several days before being returned. Consider an organizer whose colleague was arrested last week, whose own communications and social connections may now be subject to reconstruction. Consider a humanitarian worker whose laptop was briefly out of their direct control during a hotel stay, in a city where this is known to be how device tampering occurs.

These are not edge cases. They are the routine operational reality of work whose legal standing varies sharply across jurisdictions — and that adversaries treat as actionable regardless of that variation. The threat model that follows specifies what this product is designed to defend against in those moments, and equally importantly, what it is not.

A note on audience. This threat model is written for the people who deploy and support the product on behalf of users — pilot administrators, NGO security trainers, journalists' digital-security staff — and not, in most cases, for the end users themselves. Many of the users it ultimately protects will not read this document. The model assumes their support network understands these tradeoffs on their behalf, not that they personally do.

### 3.2 Adversary landscape

The product assumes adversaries from several overlapping categories. The categories are not mutually exclusive — a single user may face several simultaneously, sometimes in coordination.

**State actors operating with legal process.** Government agencies acting through formal channels: lawful intercept of telecommunications infrastructure, court-compelled disclosure of cloud-stored data, formal mutual legal assistance treaties. Threats from this category are bounded by what the legal system technically allows, which varies dramatically by jurisdiction. In some target deployment environments, legal process is effectively unbounded; in others it provides meaningful constraint.

**State actors operating outside legal process.** Intelligence services and police acting without formal authorization: off-the-books detention and interrogation, covert surveillance of individuals not formally under investigation, deployment of capability against journalists and dissidents both domestically and abroad. This category is documented extensively in the work of academic researchers including the Citizen Lab at the University of Toronto, along with the Amnesty International Security Lab and Access Now's Digital Security Helpline, whose forensic analyses have identified deployments of commercial mercenary spyware against journalists and civil-society members across multiple jurisdictions.[^spyware-vendors]

**Organized criminal entities with SIGINT capability.** Cartels and organized crime groups in some regions operate IMSI catchers, packet capture infrastructure, and bribe officials for telecom intercept access. The capability does not match well-resourced state services but is real and present in some operating environments. Journalists covering organized crime, NGO field staff in cartel-affected regions, and dual-use technology workers all face this threat tier.

**Commercial mercenary spyware operators.** Companies marketing offensive capability — zero-click exploit chains, persistent device implants, forensic extraction — to government and quasi-government customers. As documented by Citizen Lab in June 2025, commercial mercenary spyware was used against European journalists via a zero-click attack exploiting CVE-2025-43200, identified after Apple notifications alerted targeted users.[^paragon-2025] The economics of this market mean that even smaller states can rent capability that previously required nation-state intelligence resources.

**Border and customs forensic operations.** Devices seized at borders or during detention, examined with commercial forensic extraction tools, sometimes returned with implants installed. Such tools have been documented in use against civil society in multiple cases, including the 2025 case of Kenyan pro-democracy activist Boniface Mwangi, whose phone was forensically examined while in police custody, and an extended cluster of cases involving Jordan-based civil society between late 2023 and mid-2025.[^forensic-extraction]

**HUMINT against the user's network.** Pressure on family and colleagues, planted contacts inside organizations, social engineering of users and their associates. Best technical security does not address this category; the design assumes some level of HUMINT exposure exists and tries to limit blast radius.

**Capability tier assumed.** Across these categories, the design assumes adversaries with: operational budgets sufficient to acquire commercial mercenary spyware and commercial forensic extraction tooling, lawful or compelled access to telecom and cloud infrastructure in their operating jurisdiction, ability to detain individuals for hours to days and forensically examine their devices, ability to apply pressure to family and contacts within their reach, and time horizons measured in months rather than minutes. The design does not assume targeted nation-state SIGINT against the product itself (which would imply attacks against the development pipeline, signing infrastructure, or upstream protocols), but acknowledges this as a residual risk worth monitoring.

### 3.3 Attack surfaces and consequences

The product is exposed across several distinct surfaces. Each is described with how adversaries attack it and what is lost when they succeed.

**Network surface.** Communications cross networks the user does not control. Adversaries with network position can perform deep packet inspection and protocol fingerprinting, operate fake base stations (IMSI catchers) that intercept cellular traffic, compel cooperation from telcos and ISPs for lawful intercept, manipulate routing via BGP, intercept TLS through mandated local certificate authorities, and correlate traffic patterns across services. Successful attack yields metadata about whom the user contacts and when, content of unencrypted traffic, and in some cases the ability to inject traffic that compromises endpoints.

**Endpoint surface.** The user's device itself is exposed to zero-click mercenary spyware delivered through messaging apps or other software, forensic extraction tooling when seized, evil-maid attacks when left unattended in adversary-accessible locations, and physical seizure with indefinite retention. Successful attack yields complete access to data on the device, ongoing surveillance of the user, and in seizure cases, indefinite loss of the device itself even if no information is extracted. The consequence is not bounded by what was on the device at the moment of compromise — it includes everything that can be retroactively decrypted from message history, every contact that can be enumerated, every cryptographic capability that can be used to impersonate.

**Returned-after-seizure surface.** Devices returned to users after detention, border seizure, or any period of physical adversary control must be treated as potentially compromised. Forensic extraction tooling can be paired with implants installed in firmware, in the secure-element boot chain, or in user-space components, with varying levels of persistence and detectability. The product cannot reliably detect such implants from within the running system — implants designed to survive forensic examination are also designed to survive runtime introspection. The operational guidance is that a seized device should be considered burned: the cryptographic identity it held should be revoked, recovery should produce a fresh identity on a fresh device, and the seized device should not be returned to active use. This guidance is the mitigation; the underlying surface remains exposed for users who lack the resources to replace a seized device.

**Update channel surface.** Software updates are a primary nation-state attack vector against communications products. Adversaries can attempt to push a malicious update to a single targeted user, compromise the developer's signing identity to push a malicious update broadly, or coerce the developer or build infrastructure into producing a malicious build. The product's primary defense per Section 5.5: at v1, Sigstore identity-based per-release signing + Rekor transparency log + Sigsum-anchored release log with witness cosignatures + multi-channel distribution (F-Droid primary) + developer source review; at v1.5 the stack adds reproducible builds and the recruited 5+/3-of-5 reviewer pool, delivering binary-equivalence multi-party verification per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md). No defense reduces this surface to zero. Successful attack yields complete compromise of every user who installs the update; the consequence is bounded only by how many users install before the attack is detected and the update is revoked. The transparency log is the detection mechanism, not the prevention mechanism — broad attacks may be detected within hours, but a targeted attack against a single user may go unnoticed indefinitely. The v1 supply-chain gap that v1.5 closes is acknowledged here as part of the v1 honest threat-model posture.

**Identity surface.** The user's cryptographic identity can be attacked through compelled disclosure of credentials under legal or extralegal pressure, social engineering and phishing, account takeover via SIM swap or similar techniques, and outright key theft when devices are compromised. Successful attack yields the ability to impersonate the user, to issue attestations on their behalf, to receive messages intended for them, and to revoke their own legitimate access.

**Provisioning ceremony surface.** A user's cryptographic identity is established at the provisioning ceremony — in v1, typically an in-person QR-code exchange that seeds the trust graph and derives the initial attestations the user holds. The ceremony is exposed to adversarial observation if conducted in surveilled spaces (cameras, room audio, shoulder-surfing), to compelled or coerced participation by either party (the user is detained at the moment of provisioning; the provisioner is the adversary or under their control), and to ceremony substitution if the QR exchange occurs across an adversary-mediated channel rather than physically co-located. Successful attack against the ceremony yields the resulting identity and any attestations it carries — and because the trust graph anchors on these initial attestations, ceremony compromise has effects that persist through the entire lifetime of that identity. Mitigations include conducting the ceremony in private, in person, with both parties holding their own devices; the design assumes some users will accept higher ceremony risk in exchange for operational feasibility.

**Metadata surface.** Even when content is fully encrypted, who-talks-to-whom-when can be reconstructed through traffic correlation across services, location pattern analysis across devices and apps, timing correlation between messages and observable events, and social graph inference from contact lists and group memberships. Successful attack yields identification of the user's collaborative network, mapping of organizational structure even when individual content cannot be read, and targeting prioritization for further action.

**Trust-graph propagation metadata.** Trust-graph operations (attestations, revocations, introductions, key rotations) propagate via the messaging layer alongside ordinary user-to-user traffic. Even when these operations are themselves encrypted, they have size and propagation patterns distinguishable from message content at server-level observation. A server seeing a fan-out of similar-sized blobs across multiple recipient queues within a short time window can fingerprint a revocation event or a key rotation; rotations specifically are correlated with coercion events per Section 5.1, which makes the fingerprint operationally meaningful to an adversary. Successful attack yields detection of trust-graph events (without their content): the user just rotated their operational identity, the user just revoked a peer, the user is propagating an attestation about someone. Mitigations include padding graph deltas to message-size bins, cover traffic for fan-out events, and routing rotation-related deltas through a separate channel during high-sensitivity periods; v1 commits to size-bin padding and does not commit to cover traffic (which has its own metadata-leak profile).

**Public transparency-log metadata.** The Sigsum transparency log that anchors trust-graph operations (Section 5.2) and release-binary attestations (Section 5.5) is public by design — anyone can read it. Public keys named in attestation, revocation, introduction, and key-rotation operations are therefore publicly enumerable. An adversary querying the log learns which public-key pairs have been related to each other and when, even without identifying which user holds which key. Successful attack against this surface yields partial reconstruction of the user's social graph (or the social graph of any pseudonymous identity the user holds) from log content alone, without compromising any single piece of infrastructure. The architectural mitigation is to log only commitments (Merkle roots or attestation hashes) rather than full operation content — keeping the audit property while removing the metadata channel — at the cost of needing local operation storage to verify against the committed roots. v1 commits to this commitment-only logging pattern; the operations themselves propagate via the messaging layer and live in the participants' local stores, not in Sigsum.

**Group membership and association surface.** When users participate in groups — formal cells, project channels, community channels — group membership itself is a target distinct from individual metadata. An adversary who compromises one group member gains visibility into the group's membership list, the volume and timing of group communications, and in some configurations the content of group messages. Successful attack against group membership yields a list of users to target next, mapping of organizational structure across the group, and information for prioritizing further attacks against members who have not yet been directly compromised. The design minimizes group-level metadata where the protocols permit (per-message recipient resolution rather than persistent member rosters where possible), but groups remain inherently associational and cannot be made fully private without losing their utility. Users who participate in groups should understand that the compromise of any group member has consequences for every other member.

**HUMINT surface.** Across documented cases of journalist and civil-society compromise, the most common vector is not technical exploitation but human intelligence: pressure on family and colleagues, planting of contacts inside organizations, social engineering of users and their associates, and outright informant recruitment from within the user's network. Access Now's Digital Security Helpline incident data and Front Line Defenders' protection casework both reflect HUMINT as a leading category of compromise across the protected populations they serve, and operational reviewers at training organizations report the same pattern.

HUMINT against a target's network operates at multiple removes. At the closest range, family members are detained and questioned to extract information about the user's activities, location, and contacts. At one remove, colleagues at the user's organization or affiliated organizations are approached — sometimes with direct threats, sometimes with offers of money or relief from unrelated legal pressure, sometimes with appeals to ideology or grievance. At further remove, new contacts are introduced to the target's social circle who report back to the adversary — a slower attack but harder to detect, because the target trusts the introduction chain rather than evaluating each new contact in isolation.

The best technical security does not address this category of attack. The product assumes some level of HUMINT exposure exists for any user with stakes high enough to require the product in the first place, and is designed to limit the blast radius of any single HUMINT compromise. The trust graph's design contributes directly to this: attestations are scoped, revocable, and visible, so a compromised contact who begins issuing suspicious attestations or making suspicious introductions can be identified and quarantined rather than silently expanding the adversary's foothold. Cellular compartmentation patterns (formal in v3+, organizational in v1) further limit what any single HUMINT compromise can reach. Users and the people who train them should treat HUMINT as the dominant risk category at this threat tier, and treat the design's HUMINT-limiting features — scoped attestations, soft-cascade quarantine on revocation, compartmentalized rosters — as among the most operationally important security properties of the product, even though they are quieter and less visible than encryption.

**Forced peer designation surface.** At provisioning, the user designates recovery peers from their contact list. A user under coercion at the moment of provisioning may be forced to designate peers chosen by the adversary, or peers the adversary intends to compromise later. This surface is distinct from the Recovery network surface below: that surface concerns later compromise of legitimately-chosen peers, while this surface concerns adversary-influenced peer selection at the moment of identity establishment. Successful attack yields adversary access to the user's master identity, recoverable on demand. The mitigation is operational — provisioning should occur outside of adversary presence, with peer designation made under conditions the user controls — but the surface persists for users whose provisioning conditions are themselves compromised. The pilot's in-person provisioning by a known facilitator reduces this risk meaningfully but does not eliminate it; broader deployments without trusted facilitators will face it more directly.

**Recovery network surface.** The peer network designated for social recovery is itself a high-value target and worth calling out separately. An adversary who identifies a user's recovery peers and successfully compromises three of them gains the user's master cryptographic identity even if the user themselves is not directly compromised. This is an architectural tradeoff: social recovery makes the user's identity restorable without trustees in friendly jurisdictions, at the cost of distributing the identity's security across a small social network. Peer selection — geographic distribution, social distance, ability to refuse coercion — directly affects the security of this surface.

**Tool-use surface (acknowledged, not mitigated).** In some jurisdictions, the mere fact that an individual runs GrapheneOS, uses Tor, or installs identifiable security software is itself sufficient pretext for further scrutiny. The product cannot meaningfully mitigate this — by the time someone is using the product, they have accepted being identifiable as a user. The design minimizes how identifiable the product's traffic is on the network (Tor obfuscation, generic-looking encrypted blobs to network observers), but cannot eliminate the underlying signal that security-conscious behavior is itself observable. Users should understand this limitation explicitly.

**Distribution and supply-chain surface.** In the v1 pilot model under the BYOD posture established in Section 10.1 (and reflected in Section 6.3), pilot users source their own GrapheneOS-capable Pixel devices through standard commercial channels. The developer prepares Cairn installation media, assists with GrapheneOS installation at the in-person ceremony where the user has not already installed it, and provisions identities for users. The supply chain therefore decomposes: hardware supply chain rests with the user's chosen device channel (Google's commercial distribution, certified resellers); application and identity supply chain concentrates in the developer for the pilot. An adversary who compromises the developer, the build pipeline, or any step of the application-preparation workflow can position malicious code before the user takes possession; an adversary targeting the hardware supply chain operates against a surface the user themselves selected, which the project does not control. Successful attack against either surface compromises pilot users without their knowledge and without any opportunity for them to independently verify the integrity of what they received. The mitigations are reproducible builds, transparency logging of release artifacts, external reviewer attestations, the project's small loaner-pool inventory (2–4 devices per Section 10.1) which lets the project verify a representative hardware sample, and operational discipline around installation-media preparation — but the trust placed in the pilot-distribution channel is real and worth naming explicitly. Post-pilot, as the product moves to broader distribution via F-Droid/Accrescent and direct download, the application supply-chain surface narrows but does not disappear: it shifts from the developer's hands to the build and signing infrastructure.

**Stakes.** The consequences of successful attack on any of these surfaces are bounded by the stakes of the user's work, and those stakes vary sharply by jurisdiction. For some users in some jurisdictions the realistic worst case is a fine, professional sanction, or job loss. For others it is years of imprisonment, with credible risk of torture or death in custody. The product is designed toward the upper end of this range — well above the casual-privacy tier that most consumer messaging tools target, and in the most severe deployment environments approaching the existential-survival tier that classified national-security work occupies. Design decisions throughout the product reflect this positioning: aggressive forward secrecy, minimum on-device persistence, plausible deniability where achievable, social recovery rather than centralized trustees, transparency-log auditability rather than internal accountability.

### 3.4 Trust roots

For the threat model to be honest, the product must specify what it does _not_ try to protect against — the foundations it trusts to be uncompromised. If any of these trust roots are broken, the product's security guarantees do not hold. Naming them explicitly is a form of honesty that reviewers should be able to evaluate.

The product trusts:

**The GrapheneOS operating system.** The hardened Android distribution is assumed not to be backdoored, deliberately weakened, or compromised through upstream supply chain. The trust is justified by the project's open development, reproducible builds, and security track record, but it is a trust nonetheless.

**The Pixel hardware.** Specifically the Titan M2 secure element and the verified boot chain. Pixel devices have been examined by independent researchers, but supply-chain attacks against specific devices delivered to specific users remain a theoretical possibility. The product assumes that devices obtained through normal commercial channels (or through the pilot's curated distribution) are not individually compromised.

**The cryptographic primitives.** Ed25519 signatures, Curve25519 key agreement, ChaCha20-Poly1305 authenticated encryption, and the underlying mathematical assumptions of elliptic-curve cryptography. These are widely deployed and extensively analyzed; their failure would represent a fundamental break in modern cryptography rather than a product-specific vulnerability.

**The Tor network.** Not as an absolute guarantee of anonymity — Tor's threat model has well-understood limits against global passive adversaries — but as a transport layer that meaningfully complicates traffic analysis and network-level surveillance for the threat tier this product addresses.

**The SimpleX and Briar protocols.** Their core cryptographic constructions — including SimpleX's double-ratchet derivative and Briar's pairwise key exchange — are assumed correct. Both projects have undergone external review and both remain subject to ongoing scrutiny. Implementation flaws are possible and the integration must handle them defensively, but the underlying protocols are trusted.

**The Sigsum transparency log.** The trust-graph attestations and revocations are anchored in Sigsum, which provides public auditability and tamper-evidence through a minimal-trust witness-cosignature model. The product trusts that enough independent witnesses are operating and that systemic cosignature collusion is not occurring. Sigsum is explicitly designed to minimize the trust placed in any single party, but it is still a trust root: if all witnesses are compromised or collude, log tampering becomes undetectable from outside the log.

**The build supply chain.** The toolchain that compiles the application (Rust toolchain, Kotlin toolchain, the Android NDK and SDK) and the tree of third-party dependencies pulled in at build time are assumed not to contain deliberately introduced backdoors. From v1.5 onward, reproducible builds plus external reviewer attestations of binary equivalence are the primary defenses — multiple independent parties producing or reviewing the artifact raises the cost of supply-chain tampering. At v1 per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md), the defenses are developer source review plus the witness-cosigned release log plus F-Droid distribution as the v1.5-rebuild-attestation substrate; the build-pipeline portion of the supply chain at v1 is not externally verified, which is the explicit v1 supply-chain gap. The underlying trust in compilers, language runtimes, and the transitive dependency graph remains at both v1 and v1.5. The xz-utils backdoor identified in 2024 is a recent reminder that this trust root has been actively attacked.

**The in-person facilitator (v1 pilot model).** The v1 pilot's provisioning ceremony is conducted by a trained facilitator — initially the developer themselves — who provides the device, conducts the QR exchange that seeds the trust graph, walks the user through recovery-peer selection, and mediates the user's first attestations. A compromised or coerced facilitator gains visibility into multiple users' identity material at the moment of provisioning and influences the structure of their early trust graph. The product trusts the facilitator to operate in the user's interest during the ceremony. The trust is bounded by pilot scope (10-15 users, one or two local groups) where the facilitator's operational discipline can be sustained directly; broader deployments must address facilitator concentration through mechanisms not yet specified (rotation of facilitator role, independent witness to provisioning ceremonies, post-provisioning audit of attestations against expected patterns).

**The external reviewer pool (v1.5 trust root; not a v1 trust root per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)).** From v1.5 onward, the reviewer pool that attests binary equivalence of each release against reproducible builds — described in Section 5.5 — is functionally a trust root. Compromise of enough reviewers at v1.5+ to issue concurring false attestations defeats the defense against developer-signing-identity compromise. The product trusts that the reviewer pool's recruitment criteria (geographic diversity, institutional diversity, demonstrated independence) produce a pool whose collusion threshold is high relative to the threat model in Section 3.2. The chosen pool size is 5+ reviewers with a 3-of-5 attestation threshold required for release. At v1, the recruited pool is not yet a trust root — v1 release security relies on developer source review plus the witness-cosigned release log plus F-Droid as the v1 distribution-substrate that v1.5's rebuild attestation activates against. The v1 supply-chain gap that the recruited pool will close at v1.5 is acknowledged in §5.5 and [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md) pilot consent.

**The OIDC identity provider used for Sigstore signing.** Sigstore's identity-based signing binds each release's signing certificate to a verified OIDC identity (Section 5.5). The OIDC provider — Google, GitHub, Microsoft, or whichever provider the project uses — can issue an OIDC token for the developer's identity to a party that is not the developer, under legal process in the provider's jurisdiction or under coercion of provider personnel. The product trusts the OIDC provider to attest only to actual developer authentication events. This trust placement adds the OIDC provider's jurisdiction to the project's effective trust surface; partner organizations and pilot users in jurisdictions where the OIDC provider's home jurisdiction is itself an adversary inherit the trust placement and should be informed of it.

**Witness-pool independence (a correlated-trust note).** The same partner pool that acts as Sigsum witnesses for the trust-graph audit (Section 5.2) also acts as Sigsum witnesses for the release-log audit (Section 5.5). Compromise of the witness pool affects both audit surfaces simultaneously. The product accepts the operational cost of this concentration in v1 (a single witness pool is easier to recruit, maintain, and audit than two independent pools at the project's current scale); v1.5 or later may separate the witness pools if the threat tier of one surface escalates relative to the other.
