Users in adversarial jurisdictions cannot rely on a provider to refuse cooperation with the legal process those jurisdictions can compel, cannot rely on a provider's continued operation across the time horizons their work requires, and cannot independently verify that the provider is holding the material under the terms claimed. This includes hardware-security-module-backed provider escrow, transparency-logged escrow, and multi-party-computation escrow with provider participation: the architectural rejection is of the provider trust relationship itself, not of the specific cryptographic construction. Social recovery is the alternative to this entire class of design, and the recovery network surface (Section 3.3) is the architectural cost of accepting that alternative.

### 5.4 Communications Protocols

The architecture commits to two messaging protocols over the project's lifetime: SimpleX as the everyday spine, and Briar as the highest-sensitivity tier where eliminating server-mediated metadata is worth its operational cost. v1 ships SimpleX-only; Briar integration is deferred to v1.5 per [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md) because the engineering cost of binding Briar's identity model to Cairn's operational identity is multi-month work that v1 cannot absorb on the target timeline. The architectural slot for Briar is preserved — protocol versioning, capability-token extensibility, and the messaging layer's transport abstraction all support adding a second protocol without breaking v1 deployments. For v1, users have one messaging tier; the highest-sensitivity-tier discipline is exercised operationally (what is and is not said over SimpleX) rather than mechanically (toggling into Briar). The two-tier architecture returns in v1.5.

**SimpleX as the primary spine.** SimpleX is the everyday messaging substrate. Its central architectural property is that there are no persistent user identifiers: queues are addressed cryptographically, the address of a queue is shared out-of-band when two users connect, and any given server sees only the messages routed through it rather than a user's broader contact graph. There is no account, no username, no phone number, no email address that ties activity together across servers — the identifier that links a user to a queue is a key, and the key is paired only with the specific recipient who needs to write to that queue. SimpleX's double-ratchet derivative provides forward secrecy _and post-compromise security_ for on-wire message content — past traffic cannot be decrypted if a current session key is compromised, and the protocol self-heals via the asymmetric ratchet step after compromise. The on-device message store, however, is decryptable under unlock regardless of ratchet state; forward secrecy is a wire-level property, not an at-rest property. Queues are short-lived and rotatable; servers are self-hostable if a user or group prefers to operate their own, or can default to the SimpleX network's published relays.

For the threat model this addresses the Network and Metadata surfaces from Section 3.3 in specific ways. No persistent identifier means no obvious join key for traffic correlation across services or across time. Per-recipient queue addressing means no single server holds a roster of the user's contacts. Self-hostability means the project does not need to operate user-depended-on infrastructure and a user with elevated requirements can choose their own substrate without abandoning the protocol — consistent with the minimal-project-operated-infrastructure principle in Section 4.2.

SimpleX is not a complete answer. A user who connects multiple devices to the same set of queues creates correlatable activity at those queues regardless of how the identifiers are constructed. A server, whether operated by the SimpleX project, by a third party, or by the user themselves, can be coerced, compromised, or compelled to retain logs it would not retain in normal operation; the protocol limits what such a compromise yields, but does not reduce it to zero. The trust placed in the SimpleX protocol's cryptographic correctness is one of the trust roots named in Section 3.4, and the same residual risks named there — implementation flaws, protocol-level vulnerabilities not yet discovered — apply here.

**Briar as the v1.5 highest-sensitivity tier.** Briar is committed for v1.5: pure peer-to-peer over Tor with no servers in the path, no queues, no third-party infrastructure visible into the exchange. Two parties communicate directly through Tor hidden services; messaging is synchronous in the strict sense (both parties online), with an asynchronous mailbox capability that bounds delay for offline recipients without claiming queue-based convenience. Briar's Bramble Transport Protocol (BTP) and Bramble Synchronisation Protocol (BSP) provide forward secrecy with characteristics specific to their design — distinct from SimpleX's double-ratchet derivative; the integration relies on these properties as published by the Briar project and named as a trust root in Section 3.4. The protocol is the right tool for conversations where eliminating server-mediated metadata is the dominant requirement — a journalist coordinating with a source, an activist communicating with someone whose name should not appear in any third party's logs even in aggregate. Briar's design has been refined over years of use by journalists and activists in hostile network environments; its operational track record is what makes it the right second-tier choice, even with the engineering cost of binding it to Cairn's operational identity.

The deferral is engineering-scope-driven, not design-driven. The two architectural challenges are (a) Briar's library architecture assumes ownership of identity, Tor lifecycle, storage, and contact flow, which conflicts with Cairn's "one operational identity across protocols" property and requires either forking `bramble-core` or accepting a dual-identity reality; and (b) Briar's synchronous-rendezvous cost interacts with users on irregular schedules in ways that need careful UX work to surface honestly. Both are solvable in v1.5 with the foundation v1 establishes; neither is solvable in v1 within the timeline.

**In v1, sensitivity tiering is operational rather than mechanical.** A user with a high-sensitivity conversation in v1 exercises discipline over what is sent through SimpleX — knowing that even the strongest server-mediated protocol leaves metadata a server can be compelled to disclose — rather than toggling into a different protocol. v1.5 restores the per-conversation extra-private mode toggle described in Section 5.6.

**Toggling Briar is itself a metadata signal (v1.5 forward).** When the v1.5 extra-private mode toggle ships, users should understand that selecting it produces observable signals at multiple layers: network-level observers see traffic-shape changes correlated with the toggle (Briar's persistent Tor hidden-service signature differs from SimpleX's queue-based pattern); forensic examination of the device reveals which conversations used the elevated tier; behavioral patterns indicate this-conversation-is-sensitive to anyone who can correlate device state with external events. For users facing traffic-analysis-capable adversaries the operational practice is either to route all sensitive conversations through Briar uniformly (eliminating the differential signal) or to accept that the toggle's selection is itself observable. The v1.5 tooltip surfaces this honestly so users can make a calibrated choice.

**Tor as the transport layer.** SimpleX runs over Tor in v1; Briar will join it in v1.5. Tor's role is named explicitly as a trust root in Section 3.4 with its known limitations against global passive adversaries, and that framing carries over here. Tor complicates traffic analysis and network-level surveillance for the threat tier without claiming to eliminate them — and Section 3.3's Tool-use surface acknowledges that the act of using identifiable security software is itself observable.

Pluggable transports (obfs4, meek, webtunnel, snowflake, whichever the Tor Project's current guidance indicates) bound but do not close the Tool-use surface against DPI-capable adversaries. They are an ongoing engineering commitment, not a one-time decision: transport choices appropriate at v1 release may be blocked by v2 because DPI evasion is a continuously-being-solved problem rather than a solved one. The project commits to tracking Tor Project transport guidance and prioritizing transport-update releases when active blocking is observed in pilot or partner jurisdictions; the practical implication is that users in DPI jurisdictions are offline when an active transport is blocked, until an updated transport propagates through reviewer attestation and installation. Specific transport selection is operational rather than architectural and is deferred to the system design spec; the commitment at this level is that the transport layer is replaceable without disturbing the protocols above it.

**Push notifications.** GrapheneOS does not ship with Google Play Services and the product does not depend on them. Push delivery requires a mechanism that operates without that dependency, and the architectural commitment is to a protocol with no required external service the project does not control. UnifiedPush is the architectural choice as a self-hostable open protocol that meets this constraint. The metadata implications of push are real: a push distributor, whichever protocol it implements, sees the timing of notifications delivered to a user even when it cannot see the content, and this is itself metadata of the kind named in Section 3.3.

**v1 default posture: push disabled.** v1 ships with push notifications off by default. The client polls at user-configurable intervals (default 15 minutes), accepting the battery and latency cost in exchange for not adding a third-party metadata channel. Users who explicitly opt into push at provisioning are walked through the implications by the facilitator and select a UnifiedPush distributor (NTFY self-hosted, the user's preferred public distributor, or — for users in environments where additional app installation is itself a tell — they keep the polling default). The choice is presented as informed-consent in onboarding rather than as a default the user passively accepts. v1.5 may revisit the default if pilot feedback indicates the polling latency is operationally unworkable; the architectural commitment is to make the user's metadata posture explicit rather than implicit.

**Voice and video.** SimpleX supports voice and video calling natively. Inclusion in v1 is contingent on time during the implementation period; the architecture preserves the slot but the feature is treated as a v1.x or v2 candidate per Section 6.2 rather than a v1 commitment. The protocol-versioning framework described in Section 6.4 accommodates this kind of incremental capability addition without forcing a client-wide migration.

**Alternatives considered.** The SimpleX-primary + Briar-second-tier selection (with Briar shipping in v1.5 per the scope decision above) was made against a survey of the available landscape, and several alternatives were rejected with specific reasoning.

_Matrix and the Element client_ were rejected as the primary protocol. The Matrix federation model requires every user to trust their homeserver or to operate one; homeservers see all of their users' traffic; cross-server federation makes the metadata surface larger rather than smaller. Cross-ref Section 4.2: the minimal-project-operated-infrastructure principle is poorly served by Matrix, which pushes infrastructure responsibility onto every user or onto a per-user choice of trusted operator. The complexity of the federation protocol is also a real cost for a security-focused project — more surface to audit, more surface to defend.

_Signal_ was rejected despite the strength of the Signal Protocol itself. Phone number registration is a non-starter for the target audience: the phone number is a tracking vector, a registration leak surface, and a forcing function for ties to telco infrastructure that the threat model treats as compromised. Signal's centralized architecture introduces operational dependence on the Signal Foundation, which is a single party that can be pressured, subpoenaed, or fail. The protocol itself remains a reference for forward-secrecy construction; the deployment model is the disqualifying factor.

_Session_ was rejected on multiple grounds. The Session Network — the protocol's transport substrate — is an external dependency the project does not control and whose long-term operational trajectory is uncertain. The cryptographic stack is a fork of the Signal Protocol with changes that have drawn scrutiny, and the protocol's evolution is determined by the Session project on a timeline that may not align with the security posture this product needs to maintain.

_Wickr_ was rejected. Concentration risk is the primary concern: Wickr is AWS-owned, and the threat tier this product addresses includes adversaries with both legal and extralegal means to apply pressure to a large cloud provider operating under a specific jurisdiction's legal framework. Wickr's enterprise focus is also a poor match for the grassroots audience, and the auditability available for Wickr does not match what open-source projects like SimpleX and Briar provide.

_Cwtch_ was considered as an alternative to Briar in the highest-sensitivity tier. Its properties — peer-to-peer over Tor, no servers, designed for the same threat tier as Briar — are similar enough that the choice is closer than the others. Briar is selected for v1 on the basis of its longer operational track record, its existing relationships with NGO partners likely to be involved in pilot facilitation, and the larger body of deployed experience among the target user population. Cwtch remains a candidate for future evaluation and the integration shape is similar enough that a switch is not architecturally costly if later experience favors it.

_A custom protocol_ was considered and rejected for the obvious reasons. The protocols this product needs are solved problems with implementations under active audit; building a new one would multiply the audit burden, place the project in the path of protocol-level cryptanalytic scrutiny it is not staffed to absorb, and lose the cumulative review effort that SimpleX and Briar have already received. The project's contribution is integration, identity, trust graph, and operational discipline — the layer above the protocols — and the protocols themselves are correctly delegated to projects whose mission is to build and maintain them.

### 5.5 Updates and Release Security

Software updates are among the most consequential attack surfaces this product exposes (Section 3.3, Update channel surface). A single malicious release reaches every user who installs it, and an adversary who can produce one indistinguishable from a legitimate release has accomplished, in one step, what they would otherwise have to accomplish per-device. The defense is structural: no single credential, no single party, and no single distribution channel is trusted to assert that a release is genuine. A release is genuine when its provenance is independently visible — to the developer, to anyone who chooses to check the public record after the fact, and from v1.5 onward to a recruited pool of external reviewers verifying against reproducible builds.

**v1 release-security stack vs v1.5 expansion (per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)).** The architecture-simplification review surfaced that the recruited 5+/3-of-5 reviewer pool committed to as a v1 gate would attest _source_, not binaries (reproducible builds land at v1.5). The v1 pool's marginal property over comparable open-source security tools (Briar's F-Droid + reproducible-builds posture; Signal Android's equivalent) is narrower than first framed, and recruiting the pool as a v1 ship-gate imposes a Q5-outreach-conditional v1 launch dependency the simplification review identified as the largest single recurring partner-coordination loop in the operational picture. The decision per D0015 defers the recruited pool to v1.5, where it lands alongside reproducible builds and F-Droid rebuild verification — the combination that delivers binary-equivalence multi-party verification. v1 ships with developer signing + public log audit + multi-channel distribution; v1.5 adds reviewer-pool attestation + reproducible-build binary equivalence.

**Two signing layers: long-lived APK key and per-release Sigstore attestation.** Android requires APKs to be signed with the APK Signing Scheme (v2/v3/v4 block in the APK); Android's installer and Play Protect verify against the APK's own signing key chain at install. The project must therefore hold a long-lived Android signing identity for APK signature continuity (required for updates to install over prior versions). That key is protected as carefully as the threat model demands — held in a hardware security token, with rotation supported via APK Signature Scheme v3 key rotation if a future compromise requires it. On top of the long-lived APK key, each release is also signed with Sigstore identity-based signing: a fresh keypair generated for that release, bound to a verified OIDC identity through Sigstore's Fulcio certificate authority, with the signing event recorded in Sigstore's Rekor transparency log. Sigstore is the per-release attestation layer, not the primary signing identity. Compromise of a Sigstore signing event affects exactly the release it attested; compromise of the long-lived APK key requires the rotation flow.

**Developer source review in v1; recruited pool + reproducible builds in v1.5 (per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)).** v1 ships with developer-conducted source review (the developer reads their own release commits before tagging and signing) plus the public log substrate that lets any third party independently verify the release post-hoc — Sigstore-signed artifacts in Rekor, release log entries in Sigsum, F-Droid distribution that makes the v1 release available for community rebuild verification when reproducible builds arrive. Reproducible builds — deterministic builds where the binary the developer signs is byte-identical to a binary produced by an independent build from the same source — are deferred to v1.5 per [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md). The reason for the deferral is engineering scope: Android reproducible builds require pinning the Android Gradle Plugin, the build tools, the NDK, and the Rust toolchain (per [D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md)) to specific deterministic versions, with continuous verification across host environments. This is achievable but is 4–8 weeks of toolchain work plus ongoing maintenance, and the project prioritizes shipping v1 with developer source review plus the F-Droid distribution pattern that activates rebuild attestation at v1.5.

The v1 supply-chain gap remains acknowledged: developer source review does not detect a compromised build pipeline producing a malicious binary from clean source. v1 pilot consent (per [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md)) names this gap explicitly. v1.5's combined commitment — reproducible builds + recruited reviewer pool + F-Droid rebuild verification — closes the gap with multi-party binary-equivalence attestation.

**Recruited reviewer pool (v1.5 commitment, recruitment work proceeds at v1) — 5+ pool, 3-of-5 threshold.** The architectural target ships at v1.5: at least five recruited reviewers with a threshold of three attestations required for a release to be considered properly verified. The 5+ pool size provides margin against single-reviewer compromise, attrition, and unavailability; the 3-of-5 threshold mirrors the Shamir parameter in 5.3 and means the compromise of two reviewers is insufficient to issue a false-but-quorum-attested release. Recruitment criteria for v1.5 onward: geographic diversity (different jurisdictions, so no single legal process can compel all), institutional diversity (different employers, different funders), demonstrated independence of the developer (no prior contractual relationship, no shared organizational affiliation). The reviewer pool enters Section 3.4's trust-root enumeration at v1.5; in v1 the reviewer pool is not yet a trust root.

Recruitment work proceeds in parallel with v1 engineering through Q5 (NGO partner outreach per §8.6); willing reviewers identified during v1 may "soft-ship" attestations against v1 releases as Sigsum log entries before the architectural commitment lands at v1.5, but v1 release shipping is not gated on pool formation. If Phase C honoraria funding (§10.3) closes only at the lower bound, v1.5 may ship with a smaller pool (3+ recruited at lower bound) growing toward the 5+ architectural target across subsequent releases.

Reviewers, once recruited, are not contributors and do not write code; their function is independent verification with no incentive aligned with shipping. From v1.5 they verify binary equivalence against the reproducible build; if any recruited reviewers join during v1, they verify source equivalent to the developer's own source review. Their attestations are Sigsum-anchored regardless of verification target. A user verifying a v1.5+ release checks the developer's Sigstore signature against Rekor and the reviewer attestations against Sigsum; either alone is insufficient. Reviewer rotation cadence, compensation (honoraria for time spent at v1.5 onward), onboarding (a Docker/Nix-pinned reviewer toolkit), and the operational policy for shipping below quorum (from v1.5: the project does not ship below 3-of-5 attestation; releases wait for quorum) are specified in Section 8.2. At v1, releases ship when developer source review and the pre-pilot audit (per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md)) and the partner-mediated pilot consent and exit protocol (per D0013) are in place.

**OIDC trust placement.** The Sigstore identity that signs each release is bound to an OIDC identity (the developer's verified email or equivalent) through Sigstore's Fulcio. The OIDC provider — chosen at project start and named in Section 3.4 trust roots — can issue OIDC tokens for the developer's identity under legal process in its jurisdiction or under coercion of its personnel. The project commits to operational defenses on the OIDC identity: hardware-security-key requirement on the OIDC provider, alerts on every token issuance event, regular audit of Rekor entries against an out-of-band log of when the developer actually signed. The OIDC provider's jurisdiction is a property partner organizations and pilot users should be aware of; v1 uses a U.S.-based OIDC provider in pilot (the most operationally mature option), with the explicit acknowledgment that this places U.S. legal process in the effective trust surface. v1.5 may transition to a different OIDC provider if pilot experience or partner feedback indicates the jurisdiction choice is operationally inappropriate.

**Public transparency log of releases.** Every signed release artifact is logged from v1; every reviewer attestation is logged from v1.5 onward (with willing reviewers' attestations logged in v1 if Q5 outreach produces them earlier). A release that does not appear in the log is not a release; the client refuses to install it. This is what closes the targeted-attack surface as much as it can be closed at this layer: an adversary cannot deliver a signed update to a single user out-of-band, because that user's client will not find the corresponding Sigsum entry and will reject the artifact. Witnesses — recruited NGO and academic partners (Q5; witness pool is a v1 commitment per §8.6) — cosign the release log state, so log tampering by the log operator is itself detectable. The shared witness pool is acknowledged as a correlation risk in Section 3.4: compromise of enough witnesses to break log integrity defeats both the trust-graph audit and the release-binary audit at once. The project accepts this correlation in v1 (operational cost of two independent witness pools is high at current scale) and revisits the separation in v1.5 or later if the threat tier of one surface escalates relative to the other. The detection mechanism is not prevention; an adversary willing to burn a signing identity to attack one user can produce a logged-but-malicious release, and that compromise affects however many users install it before reviewers (from v1.5) and witnesses (from v1) catch the anomaly. At v1 specifically, the detection lean rests more heavily on the witness-cosigned log plus F-Droid community attention than on a recruited reviewer pool; v1.5's recruited pool adds the explicit attestation layer the v1 stack defers. The transparency log makes the timeline of any such event auditable after the fact, which is the property the design optimizes for.

**Rollback resistance.** Version numbers in signed release metadata are monotonic, and the client refuses to install a release whose version is lower than the highest version it has previously installed. An adversary in possession of an older signed release — for example, one that was legitimate at the time but contains a since-patched vulnerability — cannot use that artifact's still-valid signature to downgrade a user back into the vulnerable state. This is a small property with a large operational effect: it means the security guarantees of an update are not undone by the mere existence of older legitimate artifacts.

**Multi-channel distribution (v1: F-Droid, Accrescent, project-controlled direct download).** The v1 signed artifacts are distributed through F-Droid (primary; the substrate that v1.5's reproducible-build rebuild attestation activates against per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)), Accrescent, and direct download from a project-controlled domain — matching the §6.1 v1 distribution-channel scope. Heavier-touch channels (a project-operated Tor onion service; offline signed images suitable for hand-delivery in environments where the primary channels are blocked or surveilled) are deferred to v1.5+ when funded operations capacity exists to maintain them sustainably, consistent with §4.2's minimal-project-operated-infrastructure principle (per architecture-simplification review F17). Multi-channel distribution does not change what is being delivered — every channel carries the same Sigstore-signed, Sigsum-logged artifact — but it lets users cross-check what they received against what other users received, and protects against channel-specific blocking by adversaries with the ability to interdict particular routes. Pilot users in jurisdictions where F-Droid is blocked obtain releases through Accrescent or direct download without any change in the verification path; v1.5+ expands the alternative channels.

**Mitigation summary for Section 3.3 surfaces.** The stack above is the product's primary defense against the Update channel attack surface named in Section 3.3, and a significant component of the defense against the Distribution and supply-chain surface. For the update channel: a malicious update cannot ship without producing artifacts in Rekor and Sigsum that diverge from independent witnesses' log audit, and from v1.5 onward from recruited reviewer attestations; a targeted update cannot ship at all because the client requires the log entry. For the supply-chain surface in v1 (per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)): developer source review plus the witness-cosigned release log catches a smaller class of compromises than v1.5's reproducible-build pipeline + recruited reviewer pool will; the developer's build pipeline producing a malicious binary from clean source is not detected by v1's stack and is the explicit v1 supply-chain gap [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md) names in pilot consent. v1.5 closes the gap with reproducible builds (binary equivalence verifiable by anyone running the rebuild) + the recruited reviewer pool (multi-party attestation of equivalence). What this defense does not address even at v1.5: a coordinated compromise of the developer's identities (long-lived APK key and Sigstore identity) plus enough reviewer identities to issue concurring false attestations; a targeted attack delivered through a distribution channel the project has not detected as compromised; and users who do not verify reviewer attestations at install time. The pilot's device-preparation workflow — the developer installing GrapheneOS and the application before handing over the device — remains a trust placement separate from the release-security stack, narrowed but not eliminated by these mechanisms.

**Signing identity compromise plan.** Two compromise scenarios with different response patterns. _Sigstore-identity compromise_ (the OIDC identity used for per-release attestation): the developer publishes a revocation through an out-of-band channel established at project start (a signed announcement through partner organization channels, with the verification key separately held); the reviewer pool refuses to issue further attestations against releases signed by the compromised identity; the project transitions to a new Sigstore identity, with the transition attested by reviewers and recorded in Sigsum. Because every signing event was already logged in Rekor, the compromise timeline is reconstructable. _Long-lived APK key compromise_ (the harder scenario): the response uses APK Signature Scheme v3 key rotation. All installed clients must process the rotation in an update before subsequent updates can be signed by the new key. This is a multi-release recovery process that the project hopes never to execute; it is the structural reason the APK key is protected with the strongest mechanism the deployment can sustain (hardware-token-held, ideally with multi-party access procedures).

**Alternatives considered.**

_The Update Framework (TUF)._ TUF is the gold-standard general-purpose secure-update framework, with a sophisticated role-and-threshold model — root, targets, snapshot, timestamp roles, each with their own key sets and rotation policies, and threshold-signature support across roles. At Cairn's solo-developer scale, the operational complexity of TUF (key ceremonies, role hierarchies, threshold signature management, periodic role rotation) exceeds what one developer can maintain rigorously over time, and an inconsistently-maintained TUF deployment is worse than a consistently-maintained simpler stack. The Sigstore-plus-reproducible-builds-plus-reviewer-attestations combination captures most of TUF's relevant security properties — no long-lived signing key, transparency log of all signing events, multiple-party verification of release contents — at substantially lower operational cost. TUF remains a candidate for re-evaluation post-v1 if project scale grows enough to justify the operational overhead, or if specific TUF properties (key threshold management, formal role separation) become operationally relevant.

_Custom multi-signature scheme._ A bespoke release-signing system was considered and rejected. The scrutiny budget applied to Sigstore — public audit, broad adoption across the open-source ecosystem, ongoing maintenance by a multi-party foundation — exceeds anything a solo developer could replicate. No specific gap was identified that a custom scheme would close better than the chosen stack. The general engineering principle (Section 3.4 trust-roots framing: trust widely-deployed analyzed primitives, do not invent) applies straightforwardly here.

_Single developer key with long lifetime._ The status quo for many small projects: a single PGP or similar long-lived signing key, used across all releases over a span of years. This is rejected because a long-lived signing key is exactly the kind of credential nation-state adversaries target most heavily, and a single compromise yields the ability to sign every future release indistinguishably from the developer. The structural answer to "what if my key is stolen" is to not have a key to steal — short-lived signing identities, with the signing event itself anchored in a transparency log, leave no persistent credential as a target.

_No update mechanism — manual rebuild only._ Considered and rejected. Sustainable security requires updates: vulnerabilities are discovered, dependencies change, the threat landscape moves. The question is not whether to ship updates but how to ship them with the integrity properties this threat model demands. A no-updates posture is itself a security failure mode over the project's operating timeline.

The release-security stack is one component of the broader assurance program described in Section 8.2 (Release security operational practice) and Section 8.5 (Audit and assurance), which together specify how the project's security posture is maintained over the project's lifetime rather than asserted once at v1 release.

### 5.6 UX Principles

The product's interaction language is deliberately conventional. The threat model already costs the user something by being visible: a Pixel running GrapheneOS, a Tor-shaped network footprint, an installed application whose presence is itself observable (Section 3.3, Tool-use surface). Forcing the user to learn a novel messaging idiom on top of that visibility compounds friction with no defensive payoff — and friction in security software translates directly to operational mistakes. The design's working assumption is that the interesting cryptographic and trust-graph work belongs behind a surface the user already knows how to operate, not in front of it.

**Signal-familiar surface.** Threads, contact list, voice notes, attachments, group chats. A user coming from Signal, WhatsApp, or any mainstream messenger should recognize the layout, the gestures, and the mental model on first launch without orientation. This is not a UX taste preference; it is a security property. Every minute spent learning where a button lives is a minute not spent on operational discipline, and every novel interaction is a place where a user, under stress, will guess wrong. The interesting design work in this product happens in the layers behind the chat surface — the identity tiers (5.1), the trust graph (5.2), the recovery model (5.3), the messaging protocol substrate (5.4) — and surfaces only where the user must make an informed choice. The conventional foreground is what makes the unconventional background tolerable.

**Subtle trust signals.** Trust-graph state — verification status on a contact, freshness of an attestation chain, the soft-quarantine flag from a cascading revocation (5.2) — is conveyed through small, consistent visual cues attached to the entities they describe. A contact row carries its verification badge; an attestation chain is drillable from the contact detail; a revocation flag appears against the contact and the conversations that depend on it. The badges are visible enough to be findable when the user wants them and quiet enough not to dominate the chat surface during normal use. This is a deliberate middle ground. Hiding trust signals entirely would remove the user's ability to verify state and to notice anomalies — the trust graph would still function, but the user could not see when it had something to say. Surfacing trust signals as continual notifications would train the user to dismiss them, which the literature on security warning fatigue consistently demonstrates degrades engagement with security UI rather than improving it. Information on demand, attached to the entity it describes, is the target.

**Single-profile UI in v1; invisible multi-profile in v1.5.** Multi-profile support is architecturally present (per-jurisdiction, per-role, per-organization), but the v1 UI ships single-profile-only per [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md): no profile switcher, no chrome implying that switching exists. v1.5 adds the multi-profile UX with the property that users who do not engage with it continue to see the single-identity application — no telegraphing to users who have only one. The principle holds in v1.5: telegraphing the existence of multiple profiles to users who have only one — and, by extension, to anyone examining their device — would create an inferred question the architecture does not need to answer.

**Compelled-unlock guidance.** Per Decision [D0002](decisions/D0002-duress-profile.md), the product does not include duress-profile concealment in v1 or any planned version. The architectural answer to compelled unlock is tier separation (5.1): the master identity is Shamir-split among recovery peers and is not present on the device, so even full coerced unlock does not yield the master, the ability to issue master-level attestations, or the recovery shares themselves. What unlock yields is the operational identity, the on-device message history, the contact list, and the user's trust-graph view — which together identify the user's recovery peer set. The architectural protection of the master is conditional on recovery peers refusing to release shares to an adversary holding impersonation material; this is a real protection but is not categorical, and depends on the peer-verification mechanism captured separately in the Section 5.3 recovery flow. In v1, the post-coercion recovery flow lives in documentation that users and facilitators reference; the in-app first-class action that walks the user through revocation, peer contact, master reconstruction, and reissuance lands in v1.5 per [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md). The v1 documentation explains why no duress concealment is offered — detected concealment is itself prosecutable in compelled-decryption jurisdictions, and the tier model already does the protective work concealment aspires to — so the user understands the design's commitment rather than experiencing it as a missing feature.

**Recovery accessible without cryptographic literacy.** Two recovery paths are exposed in plain language. The primary path is Shamir-based social recovery: "contact three of the people you designated when you set this up, and they each give you a code that puts your identity back together." The secondary path is fresh identity with in-person reintroduction by an existing contact, accepting the loss of historical state: "start over with a new identity and let someone who already knows you vouch for it." The user is told what each path does and what each path costs — the second loses message history and prior attestations — without being asked to understand threshold cryptography. The user's job is to choose; the product's job is to make the consequences of each choice legible.

**Honest communication of limitations.** Where a security claim has limits, the UI says so. Verification status is rendered as "verified through chain of attestations" rather than "secure"; encryption status is "encrypted end-to-end via SimpleX" rather than "unbreakable"; Tor transport is "routed through Tor (resists most network observers)" rather than "anonymous." The intent is calibration. A user who understands the actual shape of the protection — what it covers, what it doesn't — makes better operational decisions than a user who has been told the product is secure full stop. Section 3 specifies what the threat model does and does not cover; the UI's job is to keep that calibration present in daily use rather than letting it decay into the misplaced confidence that absolute-sounding labels produce.

**Defaults over configuration.** The threat model does not assume the user is a security expert (3.1, audience note: the model assumes the user's support network understands the tradeoffs on the user's behalf). Security-relevant decisions are accordingly made by the design as defaults rather than presented as user choices. Where a user must choose — recovery peers in v1; profile compartmentation and extra-private mode toggle from v1.5 — the choice is staged with explicit consequences and a recommended default that fits the v1 audience. A misconfigured user is more vulnerable than a default-configured one, and a configuration surface broad enough to accommodate every expert preference is also broad enough to accommodate every novice mistake. The product errs toward the latter risk.

**Onboarding via facilitator.** First-run is structured around the v1 pilot model: in-person provisioning conducted by a trained facilitator who is present with the user during the ceremony. The provisioning ceremony, recovery peer designation, initial profile creation, and trust-graph seeding are walked through together. This is consistent with the threat-model audience note (3.1): the people who understand the tradeoffs are the facilitators, not necessarily the users, and the onboarding flow is designed so the facilitator's presence is the unit of operational discipline. The flow is architected to extend to remote onboarding in later versions, but v1 does not pretend to a self-serve onboarding it cannot yet support safely.

**Alternatives considered.** The UX philosophy was weighed against several alternative orientations.

A power-user surface exposing the full configuration space — protocol selection per message, signing-key visibility, capability-token scope editing, transparency-log inspection — was rejected. The orientation conflicts directly with the v1 audience: users who lack the context to make these decisions well would be asked to make them anyway, and the configuration surface itself widens the attack surface. A misconfigured user is more exposed than a default-configured one, and a UI that invites misconfiguration is a UI that produces it.

Aggressive security nudges and warnings — modal verification prompts before sensitive sends, repeated reminders to verify contacts, persistent banners on unverified conversations — were rejected on evidence. The published literature on security warning fatigue is consistent across browser-certificate warnings, OS permission prompts, and enterprise-software security dialogs: continual prompting trains users to dismiss security UI rather than engage with it, and the dismissal generalizes to the prompts that actually matter. The product's approach is information-on-demand rather than continual notification: trust state is always findable, never insistent.

Hiding the security surface entirely — no badges, no verification indicators, no visible trust-graph state — was rejected on the opposite ground. It would remove the user's ability to verify trust state and to notice when something has changed. A revoked contact, a stale attestation, a quarantine flag from a cascading revocation: each is information the user needs to act on, and a UI that hides it has decided on the user's behalf that they do not need to know. The subtle-but-consistent badge model is the deliberate middle ground between nag and silence.

### 5.7 Implementation language and build architecture

The implementation is split between a Rust core and a Kotlin Android UI shell, with [UniFFI](https://mozilla.github.io/uniffi-rs/) generating Kotlin bindings from Rust definitions. The split is principally a security choice rather than an engineering-ergonomics choice: Rust's memory model gives explicit control over secret-material lifetime that the JVM's garbage-collected model does not. See [D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md) for the full decision and the alternatives considered.

**Boundary.** All cryptographic operations, all trust-graph state, all protocol integrations, the storage encryption layer, and anything touching secret material live in the Rust core. The Kotlin UI handles rendering, navigation, Android lifecycle integration, and display-only data structures. Secret material does not cross from Rust into Kotlin; the core hands the UI public keys, ciphertexts, and display-safe metadata without intermediate Kotlin `String` or `ByteArray` that the JVM garbage collector might retain.

**Security properties Rust delivers.** The [`zeroize`](https://crates.io/crates/zeroize) crate enforces secret-material destruction on `Drop`. The [`secrecy`](https://crates.io/crates/secrecy) crate wraps secret types so accidental cloning, logging, or comparison is a compile error. The [`subtle`](https://crates.io/crates/subtle) crate provides constant-time comparison primitives that prevent timing side channels. Rust's typestate pattern lets the compiler enforce protocol state machines — for example, the eight-field signed-operation schema in 5.2 can be expressed as algebraic data types with compile-time field-presence enforcement. None of these have idiomatic Kotlin equivalents; the JVM's garbage collector specifically makes secret-material destruction unreliable. For a threat model that includes forensic memory extraction (Section 3.3 Endpoint surface, Returned-after-seizure surface), the Rust memory model is materially better than the JVM's, not just preferentially.

**Forward-compatibility implications.** The Rust-core architecture is reusable across the project's roadmap: v2 USB form factor uses the same core compiled to a different platform; v3 mesh radio integration can target embedded hardware where Kotlin cannot; iOS support in v2 replaces the Kotlin UI with Swift while keeping the Rust core. This forward-compatibility property is part of why the Rust-core decision is made in v1 even though v1 ships Android-only.

The reproducible-build pipeline that lands in v1.5 must reproduce both the Rust artifact and the Kotlin artifact. The v1 source-review attestation (Section 5.5) covers source in both languages.

**Persistent storage architecture.** v1 has multiple persistent stores: the SimpleX-integration store (whatever shape the integration takes), the Cairn-specific store for capability tokens, trust-graph-related state, and contact identities, and the Android Keystore-managed key material. The Cairn-specific store is SQLite with explicit schema versioning _fields_ shipping from v1 onward; the property-based migration test framework is deferred to v1.5 (per architecture-simplification review F3 — v1 has one schema, so no migration-framework target exists until v1.5 produces the first real schema migration). At v1, basic schema migration tests (forward and backward round-trip) cover the single schema; the dedicated property-based migration framework lands alongside v1.5's first real migration. Cross-store consistency under crash, migration, and recovery is handled by treating each store independently and using the trust graph as the source of truth for cryptographic state — if a local store is lost or corrupted, the user re-syncs from Sigsum and from peers. Specific library choices (Room vs. SQLDelight vs. direct SQLite via Rust) are deferred to the system design spec.

**Test, documentation, telemetry, and i18n acknowledgments.** Section 5 specifies the architecture; the implementation also requires substantial work in areas the brief mentions but does not specify here.

- _Test infrastructure._ Property-based testing for the trust-graph operation semantics and the cascade quarantine; fuzzing for the COSE/CBOR parsers, the Shamir reconstruction, and the capability-token verifier; known-answer tests for cryptographic primitives; differential testing against the SimpleX reference implementation where Cairn reuses its protocol.
- _Documentation._ User-facing guide, facilitator handbook for the in-person provisioning ceremony, peer-recovery handbook for share-holders, security model document for users who want to understand the trust roots, troubleshooting, post-coercion recovery written guidance (the v1 substitute for an in-app flow per [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md)). Partner organizations (Tactical Tech, EFF SSD, Front Line Defenders) are candidates for co-producing the user-facing and facilitator documentation; Section 8.6 covers partnership scope.
- _Crash reporting and feedback (v1 routing per [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md))._ Per architecture-simplification review F1, the project-operated SimpleX queue for crash reporting cuts from v1 — the §4.2 minimal-project-operated-infrastructure principle ships without exception at v1. Pilot crash reports and feedback flow through the partner-mediated reporting channel per D0013 (the same channel pilot users use for tool-mediated harm reports and exit requests; the partner organization aggregates and forwards). v1.5+ may add an in-app encrypted crash-reporting flow if pilot evidence demonstrates the partner channel does not capture the operational data the project needs; until then, the partner channel is the sole feedback route.
- _Internationalization slot._ v1 ships English-only per [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md), but the architectural slot uses Android string resources from the start so that v1.5 localization additions do not require a code refactor. Security-critical strings (trust-graph badges, recovery flow text, compelled-unlock guidance) will require domain-expert translation review when languages are added; partner organizations are the natural collaborators per Section 8.6.

---

## 6. v1 Scope

### 6.1 What ships in v1

The v1 release is deliberately scoped to the smallest cut of the architecture that delivers a defensible product to the v1 audience (Section 2.2 and [D0014](decisions/D0014-non-peer-recovery.md)) and provides the foundation from which v1.5 completes the design. v1 is not the architectural endpoint. The engineering estimate is approximately 9–12 person-months of focused engineering effort given the scope cuts recorded in [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md); calendar-time-to-v1 depends on whether the developer is operating at the "as available" volunteer cadence per [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md) or whether grant funding has freed full-time engineering capacity (Section 10.1's runway and Section 10.4's maintainer-compensation aspiration). The brief does not commit to a calendar date.

**Solo-developer absorption of §8.1 specialist roles.** Section 8.1 identifies three specialist roles (part-time cryptographic consulting; UX-focused engineer; documentation and community-management role) as funding-gated additions, with §5.6 UX alone estimated at 30–50% of v1 implementation effort. v1 absorbs these specialist scopes into the solo developer's working set at the volunteer baseline. The honest implication: §5.6 UX ships at MVP quality in v1 with deferred polish in v1.5/v1.6 if Phase C UX-engineer funding closes (Section 10.3); cryptographic-design review is the self-audit pattern in Section 8.5 plus the pre-pilot external audit per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md), not rolling specialist consulting; partner-collaborative documentation (below) is framed as intent subject to partner outreach (§8.6) rather than committed solo output. Section 9.1's solo-developer SPOF risk applies categorically to v1 scope; D0004's scope cuts are calibrated to what one developer can hold context for across the v1 implementation period.

**Platform.** Android-only, running on GrapheneOS on Pixel hardware. Other platforms (iOS, non-Pixel Android) are explicitly deferred or rejected (see 6.2).

**Messaging substrate.** SimpleX is the v1 messaging substrate — identifier-less queues, no persistent user identity at the protocol level, double-ratchet-derivative forward secrecy and post-compromise security for on-wire content. Briar is deferred to v1.5 per [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md); v1 sensitivity tiering is operational (user discipline over what is sent through SimpleX) rather than mechanical (protocol selection). Section 5.4 covers the protocol-level discussion.

**Identity, trust, and recovery (the v1 cryptographic engineering surface).** The three-tier identity model from Section 5.1 ships in full: master Ed25519 seed Shamir-split among recovery peers, operational keypair hardware-gated in the device's secure element (Titan M2 / StrongBox on supported Pixels, TEE-backed otherwise), and device-scoped capability tokens issued by the operational identity. The cryptographic envelope construction per [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md) ships in v1: COSE_Sign1 structures with deterministic CBOR encoding (RFC 9052, RFC 8949 §4.2.1) using the `coset` crate, used for both capability tokens and the trust-graph operation envelope. The trust graph from Section 5.2 ships with five operation types — attestation, attestation withdrawal, key compromise revocation, introduction, key rotation — distinguished per D0006 to close the cascade-laundering attack; the nine-field signed-operation schema with prior-hash chain implementation and issuer-cert-hash binding for per-(issuer, subject) anti-equivocation; the cascade quarantine semantics on revocation (attestation withdrawal soft-flags downstream attestations forward from the withdrawal date; key compromise revocation hard-suspends post-`revoked_as_of` attestations and soft-flags prior ones); and the 90-day stale-flag auto-quarantine escalation per Section 5.2. Trust evaluation queries Sigsum directly in v1 with commitment-only anchoring (hashes of operations, not operation content) and no local CRDT per D0004. Social recovery from Section 5.3 ships with the pre-shared peer challenge plus 48-hour delay-and-confirm mechanism from [D0005](decisions/D0005-peer-verification.md); v1 recovery requires online Sigsum connectivity (v1.5 adds offline-tolerant caching). The operational-identity rotation flow per Section 5.1 ships in v1 for the provisioning and recovery moments; proactive rotation flow may defer to v1.5 with the architectural slot preserved per the rotation-window-exposure framing in §5.1.

The v1 release relies on the recovery model excluding several audience populations explicitly (per [D0014](decisions/D0014-non-peer-recovery.md)); these users are named as out of scope for v1 in Section 2.2 with v1.x/v2 non-peer recovery paths under evaluation.

**Provisioning.** In-person QR-code-based provisioning ceremony, conducted in v1 by the developer as facilitator for pilot users. The facilitator walks the user through identity generation, recovery peer designation, pre-shared challenge establishment, and the initial trust-graph seeding. The pilot is gated on the partner-mediated consent and exit protocol per [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md); broader-deployment provisioning models (facilitator networks, post-pilot remote onboarding) are not committed in v1.

**UI surface.** Signal-familiar messaging surface in Kotlin: threads, contact list, voice notes, attachments, group chats. Trust badges and verification indicators per Section 5.6. Single-profile only in v1; multi-profile compartmentation UI is deferred to v1.6. No per-conversation extra-private mode toggle (depends on Briar landing in v1.5). Compelled-unlock guidance ships as written documentation rather than an in-app first-class flow.

**Push notifications and notification posture (per architecture-simplification review F12).** Per Section 5.4, UnifiedPush is the architectural choice; v1 ships polling-only with no in-app distributor-selection UX. v1 client polls at user-configurable intervals (default 15 minutes) with battery-aware polling-loop implementation. The architectural slot for UnifiedPush integration is preserved per §6.4 forward-compatibility commitments. The v1.5 revisitation of push-default plus distributor-selection UX depends on pilot feedback — distributor selection becomes operationally relevant when polling-vs-push becomes a user choice rather than a project default.

**Crash reporting and feedback routing (per architecture-simplification review F1; routed via [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md) partner channel).** The project-operated SimpleX crash-reporting queue cuts from v1; the §4.2 minimal-project-operated-infrastructure principle ships without exception at v1. Pilot users send crash reports and feedback through the partner-mediated reporting channel per D0013 (the same channel pilot users use for tool-mediated harm reports and exit requests). The partner organization aggregates and forwards operational signals to the project. v1.5+ may add an in-app encrypted crash-reporting flow if pilot evidence demonstrates the partner channel does not capture the operational data the project needs.

**Release security (per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)).** v1 ships developer-signed releases plus public log audit plus multi-channel distribution; the recruited 5+/3-of-5 reviewer pool defers to v1.5 alongside reproducible builds. The v1 stack: two-layer signing per Section 5.5 (long-lived APK signing key for Android signature continuity, whose compromise requires APK Signature Scheme v3 key rotation; plus Sigstore identity-based signing for per-release attestation); Rekor transparency log entries for all release signatures; Sigsum-anchored release log with witness cosignatures via the witness pool (which _is_ a v1 commitment, recruitment conditional on Q5 per Section 8.6); developer source review of each release; rollback resistance (monotonic version numbers in signed metadata; client refuses lower-version installs; requires persistent client state surviving reinstall/wipe). v1 distribution channels: F-Droid (primary; the v1 substrate that v1.5's rebuild attestation activates against), Accrescent, and direct download from a project-controlled domain per Section 4.2. The v1 supply-chain gap (a compromised build pipeline producing a malicious binary from clean source would not be detected by developer source review) is acknowledged in Section 5.5 and in [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md) pilot consent. **v1.5 expansion.** Reproducible Android builds plus the recruited 5+ reviewer pool with 3-of-5 attestation threshold land at v1.5, combining to deliver binary-equivalence multi-party verification. Recruitment work proceeds at v1 through Q5 outreach in parallel with v1 engineering; if willing reviewers form before v1 ship, their attestations land as Sigsum entries against v1 releases, but v1 ship is not gated on pool formation. Cadence framing per [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md) governs v1 ship timing as a function of engineering scope completion plus pre-pilot audit closing plus the pilot consent protocol landing — not pool quorum.

**Pre-pilot assurance.** Per Section 8.5 and [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md), v1 pilot deployment is gated on the pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit (1–2 person-weeks at named subsidy programs; Section 10.2 first-dollar threshold). The pre-pilot audit covers the COSE_Sign1 envelope, Shamir SSS reconstruction with memory hygiene, trust-graph operation envelope's nine-field schema and signature chain (including issuer-cert-hash binding), and recovery-flow cryptographic operations. Pilot deployment proceeds when the pre-pilot audit closes; pilot does not proceed before the audit per the no-skip-the-audit posture.

**Documentation.** User guide, facilitator handbook for the in-person provisioning ceremony, peer-recovery handbook for share-holders, written post-coercion recovery guidance, troubleshooting reference, and a security-model overview suitable for technically literate users. Partner organizations are natural collaborators for the user-facing and facilitator documentation (Section 8.6 Q5); the brief frames partner-collaborative documentation as intent subject to partner outreach rather than committed solo output. Developer-solo-feasible documentation (the technical security-model overview, the troubleshooting reference, the developer-facing handoff documents per Section 9.4) is committed unilaterally.

**Implementation architecture.** Rust core plus Kotlin UI per [D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md), with UniFFI bindings between them. Security-relevant logic, secret-material handling, protocol integrations, and storage encryption all live in the Rust core; the Kotlin layer handles UI, Android lifecycle integration, and display-only data structures. UniFFI binding maintenance, integration testing at the Rust/Kotlin boundary, and Rust↔Kotlin reproducible-build coordination are explicit ongoing components of the v1 working set (consistent with D0003's bilingual context cost acknowledgment).

### 6.2 What's explicitly deferred

The v1 architecture deliberately defers several capabilities to subsequent versions or indefinitely. Each deferral is documented with its target version (or indefinite status) so funders, partner organizations, and pilot users have an honest picture of the v1 boundary.

**Deferred to v1.5** (the "architecture completeness" release; ships when Phase C funding for the pre-beta audit closes, the [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) foundation-incorporation trigger evaluation is complete, and engineering scope completes per §7.1; reviewer-honoraria operations are a Phase C aspiration but no longer a strict v1.5 ship-gate per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)).

- Briar integration as the highest-sensitivity tier (per [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md)). Includes the per-conversation extra-private mode toggle that depends on Briar landing. Conditional on Briar's continued upstream maintenance.
- Reproducible Android builds (per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)) — the v1.5 substrate for binary-equivalence verification.
- **Recruited 5+ reviewer pool with 3-of-5 attestation threshold (per D0015).** The architectural target ships at v1.5, combined with reproducible builds so reviewers attest binary equivalence rather than source. Recruitment work proceeds at v1 through Q5 outreach; willing v1 reviewers may soft-ship attestations as Sigsum log entries, but v1 release is not gated on pool formation. If Phase C honoraria funding (§10.3) closes only at the lower bound, v1.5 may ship with a smaller pool growing toward the 5+ architectural target across subsequent releases.
- Local caching of fetched trust-graph operations for offline tolerance (per D0004; the full local CRDT remains not planned).

**Deferred to v1.6** (the "deferred UX" release; ships after v1.5 per §7.1 release sequence).

- Multi-profile compartmentation UX (v1 ships single-profile-only).
- In-app post-coercion recovery flow as a first-class action (v1 ships documentation only; per [D0002](decisions/D0002-duress-profile.md)).
- Duress-wipe pattern (destruction-on-duress-passphrase, modeled on GrapheneOS's duress PIN; per D0002).
- §5.6 UX polish funded by Phase C UX engineer per §10.3 (if Phase C UX-engineer funding closes; otherwise the work absorbs back into the developer's Phase A-cadence backlog at reduced quality bar).
- Possible v1.x non-peer recovery candidate path evaluation per [D0014](decisions/D0014-non-peer-recovery.md).

**Deferred to v2** (~12-18 months post-v1).

- USB-bootable form factor, providing portable cryptographic identity and amnesic operating environment for borrowed-laptop scenarios.
- iOS support, opening the platform to users who cannot or will not move to GrapheneOS-Pixel.

**Deferred to v3** (~18-24 months post-v1).

- Mesh radio integration (Meshtastic, MeshCore), addressing internet-shutdown scenarios.

**Deferred to v4 and beyond.**

- Established-organization tier with formal administrator and governance controls, intended as the subsidy mechanism for the grassroots use case the rest of the roadmap supports.
- Optional Matrix federation for enterprise users.
- Hardware partnerships for pre-keyed devices.

**Deferred case-by-case to v1.x or later.**

- Voice and video calling (SimpleX supports them; integration depends on time during the implementation period and on pilot priorities).
- Broader localization beyond English (post-pilot, depending on pilot user demographics; the i18n architectural slot is present in v1 per Section 5.7).

**Indefinitely out of scope.**

- Non-Pixel Android support. The product remains anchored to the GrapheneOS-on-Pixel security baseline; weakening the platform requirement would weaken the architecture's guarantees in ways the threat tier does not tolerate.
- Duress-profile concealment (as distinct from duress-wipe). Per [D0002](decisions/D0002-duress-profile.md): detected concealment is itself prosecutable in compelled-decryption jurisdictions; the tier-separated identity model is the architectural answer to compelled unlock.
- Local CRDT for trust-graph state. Per D0004, v1 queries Sigsum directly; v1.5 adds caching; the full CRDT is a v2+ candidate only if pilot evidence justifies the engineering investment.

### 6.3 Pilot deployment plan

The v1 pilot is the project's first direct evidence about whether the architectural choices work for the audience they target. It is also the moment at which the project's operational discipline (Section 8) meets real users in real conditions.

**Scale and composition.** The pilot targets 10–15 users in one or two local groups already known to the developer. The specific groups are not named in this design document; identification and final commitment depends on conversations the developer holds with candidate communities during the design-brief phase (tracked as Q4 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md)). The constraint is intentional — at pilot scale the developer can sustain the facilitator role personally (Section 5.6, Section 8.6), and operational issues surface quickly enough to inform v1.5 design without overwhelming the project's capacity to respond.

**Device provisioning (BYOD per Section 10.1).** Pilot users source their own GrapheneOS-capable Pixel devices through standard commercial channels. The project retains a small loaner pool (2–4 devices) for users who cannot acquire hardware themselves and for facilitator training and testing. At the in-person ceremony, the developer assists with GrapheneOS installation where the user has not already installed it, installs Cairn, and conducts identity provisioning with the user present. The application-and-identity supply chain concentrates in the developer per the Distribution and supply-chain surface in Section 3.3; hardware supply chain rests with the user's chosen channel. The operational mitigation for the application-and-identity surface is the reproducible-build pipeline that lands in v1.5, plus the source-review attestation in v1.

**Provisioning ceremony.** Conducted in person by the developer for each pilot user. The ceremony covers identity generation, recovery peer designation, pre-shared challenge establishment with each peer, initial trust-graph seeding, and walkthrough of the post-coercion recovery guidance (per Section 5.6, Section 8.6).

**Pilot duration.** Six months of active use before any broader release decision. Six months is long enough for pilot users to experience the architectural commitments under realistic operational conditions — including at least one quarterly release cycle, attestation rotation, and possibly a recovery scenario or peer-rotation event — and short enough that observations from the early pilot can inform v1.5 design before that work begins in earnest.

**Feedback collection.** A dedicated in-app support channel (cross-ref Section 5.7 acknowledgments on crash reporting and feedback) gives pilot users a direct channel to report operational issues. Partner organization debriefs at the 3-month and 6-month marks (per Section 8.6) provide external perspective on the pilot's progress. Pilot users are explicitly told that their operational feedback shapes v1.5 design and that they will be publicly acknowledged in the v1.5 release notes if they choose to be.

**Budget and funding posture (per Section 10 phase gates).** Hardware is BYOD per Section 10.1: pilot users source their own GrapheneOS-capable Pixel devices through standard channels. The project absorbs only the small loaner-pool cost (~$1,500–3,000 for 2–4 devices) as part of Phase A. The pilot itself does not depend on external grant funding for hardware. However, _pilot deployment_ is gated on the pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit per Section 8.5 and [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md), which Section 10.2 frames as the Phase B first-dollar threshold (subsidized floor ~$15–30K; addressable through OTF Secure Audit, Cure53 mission rates, or NLnet NGI Zero Trust per Section 10.5). Section 10's phase-gated cost model details the path. Additionally, pilot enrollment is gated on the partner-mediated consent and exit protocol per [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md), which the project commits to having in place before enrollment begins. The honest framing is that pilot hardware is self-funded and pilot operations proceed at the volunteer-baseline cadence, but pilot _launch_ depends on Phase B audit funding closing and on partner-organization mediation arrangements landing.

### 6.4 Forward-compatibility design choices in v1

Several v1 design choices are made explicitly to keep future-version work non-breaking for v1 deployments. These are the architectural commitments that bound what v1.5 and v2 can change without forcing v1 users into a hard migration.

**Protocol versioning fields in all signed messages from day one.** Every signed message — capability token, trust-graph operation, message envelope — carries a protocol version. Old clients receiving messages with unknown versions either reject them with a clear error or, for the trust-graph case, retain them verbatim for forwarding to peers that may understand them. The forward-compatibility property is unambiguous from v1.

**Capability tokens with arbitrary scope strings.** Scope vocabulary is not enumerated at v1; capability tokens carry scope strings (`messaging-send`, `trust-graph-attest`, `recovery-receive`, and so on). Later versions can introduce new scopes (`mesh-relay` in v3, `usb-attached-device` in v2) without breaking compatibility — older clients simply do not recognize the new scope and decline operations requiring it.

**Multi-device pairing specified for v2 but not built in v1** (per [D0007](decisions/D0007-multi-device.md)). v1 capability tokens support multiple device keys per operational identity at the schema level; v1 client behavior is single-device-per-identity. v2 may introduce additional protocol operations (e.g., `device_attestation`) that link multiple device keys to one operational identity in the trust graph; v1 clients accept the new operation types as forward-compatible and ignore them for local computation per the protocol-versioning property above.

**Device-to-device pairing flow specified generically.** The same pairing flow used for the v1 provisioning ceremony (in-person QR exchange, with the operational identity signing the new device's capability token) is the flow used for v2 USB pairing and v3 mesh-node enrollment. The architectural slot exists at v1; the user-facing surfaces and protocol-side handling of new device types are added in v2 and v3 without changing the v1 pairing semantics.

**Trust graph operation types designed for extension.** The five v1 operation types (per Section 5.2) carry a stable schema with a protocol version field; new operation types added in later versions are unknown-but-retained by v1 clients. The cascade quarantine semantics, the prior-hash chains, and the Sigsum commitment-only logging all support new operation types without v1 changes.

**Storage schemas with version fields (v1); property-based migration framework (v1.5).** Persistent storage (per Section 5.7) uses explicit schema-versioning fields from v1 onward. The property-based migration test framework lands at v1.5 alongside the first real schema migration (per architecture-simplification review F3 — v1 has one schema, so no migration-framework target exists at v1; the framework is honestly deferred rather than absorbed as zero-cost v1 commitment). v1.5 and later versions can extend the schema (adding fields, adding tables) with v1 clients gracefully handling the unknown content as long as later versions hold to the documented forward-compatibility rules.

**Build system: v1 Android-only; multi-target deferred to v2 (per architecture-simplification review F2).** The v1 build pipeline produces the GrapheneOS-Pixel APK and does not include cross-compilation scaffolding for v2 USB images, v2 iOS bundles, or v3 mesh-node firmware. Multi-target build-system work is deferred to v2, when funding for the platform-expansion engineering work closes per §10.3 / §10.4 (rather than v1 absorbing premature optimization that v2/v3 may never reach per §10.8). UniFFI binding maintenance for the Rust core ↔ Kotlin UI v1 boundary remains a v1 working-set component per [D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md); §10.1 Phase A absorbs that bilingual-context cost. v2 build-system work — cross-compilation paths, build matrix expansion, CI configuration for additional targets — lands when v2 work commences.

The same honesty note applies to the remaining §6.4 commitments after the simplification-review cuts (F2, F3, F12): protocol versioning enforcement across every signed message, capability-token scope-vocabulary discipline, and extensible-schema design across five operation types are dedicated v1 engineering line-items rather than zero-cost architectural commitments. The property-based migration test framework and multi-target build pipeline (previously enumerated here as v1 commitments) defer to v1.5 and v2 respectively per F3 and F2. UnifiedPush distributor-selection UX (previously a v1 commitment per §6.1) defers to v1.5 per F12. The 9–12 person-month estimate in §6.1 absorbs the remaining v1 commitments.

---

## 7. Roadmap

The roadmap describes the project's planned release trajectory beyond v1. Each release has a stated scope and explicit dependencies on the releases, funding events, and governance milestones that precede it. The brief deliberately does not stamp calendar windows on release headers: Section 10.1 commits the project to "as available" volunteer-baseline cadence consistent with [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md), and Section 10.8 disclaims "specific timelines tied to funding events. The phase descriptions are 'what unlocks what,' not 'by when.'" The roadmap below uses phase-gate language to mirror that posture: each release is described as "ships when [conditions A, B, C are met]," with the conditions referencing audit gates (§8.5, [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md)), funding gates (§10.2 Phase B, §10.3 Phase C), governance gates (§8.4 foundation incorporation, [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md)), and partner-organization preconditions (§8.6 Q5 outreach, [D0013](decisions/D0013-pilot-consent-exit.md)) rather than calendar windows. Person-month engineering estimates are stated where useful; calendar-time-to-ship depends on funding state per §10.1.

The forward-compatibility design choices in Section 6.4 are intended to bound the migration work each release imposes on v1 deployments to additive changes rather than breaking ones.

### 7.1 Release sequence

**v1.** Scope per Section 6.1: SimpleX-only messaging, single-profile UI, developer-signed releases with public log audit and multi-channel distribution (per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)), documentation-form post-coercion guidance, Rust core plus Kotlin UI implementation. Audience: pilot users (10–15 per Section 6.3) running GrapheneOS on Pixel, onboarded in person by the developer-as-facilitator. v1 ships when: (a) the engineering scope per §6.1 completes (~9–12 person-months of focused effort at the volunteer baseline per D0008); (b) the pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit per Section 8.5 / D0011 closes (Phase B funding event); (c) the partner-mediated pilot consent and exit protocol per D0013 is in place; (d) developer source review of v1 release candidates completes and the witness-pool cosignature substrate is in place per §5.5 (the recruited 5+/3-of-5 reviewer pool defers to v1.5 per D0015; recruitment work proceeds at v1 through Q5 outreach but v1 ship is not gated on pool formation). v1 is a closed pilot release, not a public release in the usual sense.

**v1.5 — architecture completeness.** Adds Briar as the highest-sensitivity tier (subject to Briar's continued upstream maintenance and release cadence — a dependency the brief does not control) and the per-conversation extra-private mode toggle that depends on it. Adds reproducible Android builds and the recruited 5+ reviewer pool with 3-of-5 attestation threshold, combining to deliver binary-equivalence multi-party verification (per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md); recruitment outcomes through Q5 outreach determine the v1.5 first-release pool size, with the 5+ architectural target growing across subsequent releases if Phase C honoraria funding closes only at the lower bound). Adds local caching of trust-graph operations for offline tolerance. v1.5 closes the architectural gap that D0004 deferred without expanding scope further. v1.5 ships when: (a) the engineering scope completes (~6–9 person-months at volunteer cadence); (b) the pre-beta full audit per §8.5 / D0011 closes (Phase C funding event for the broader-release substrate); (c) Phase C funding for reviewer honoraria operations closes per §10.3 (or if it does not close, v1.5 ships with whatever volunteer-attestation pool has formed); (d) the [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) foundation-incorporation trigger evaluation is complete (the evaluation outcome may be to incorporate per [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md), to defer further, or to commit to indefinite fiscal-sponsor operation — the decision is on the record at v1.5 broader-release planning rather than scheduled for a specific timeline). If the trigger activates, the structural mitigations broader-release users receive (formalized Safe Harbor per D0012, board-bound governance, formal partner advisory authority per D0009) become operative through the incorporation work; if the trigger does not activate, the structural mitigations remain at stated-intent posture indefinitely per §10.7. v1.5 broader-than-pilot release is structurally gated on these events — not engineering-only sequenced.

**v1.6 — deferred UX.** Adds the in-app first-class compelled-unlock recovery flow, multi-profile compartmentation UX, the duress-wipe pattern per [D0002](decisions/D0002-duress-profile.md), and the §10.3 Phase C UX-engineer-led polish of Section 5.6 surfaces (if Phase C UX-engineer funding closes; otherwise the work absorbs back into the developer's Phase A-cadence backlog). May add voice and video calling (SimpleX supports them; depends on pilot priorities) and may begin localization to languages indicated by pilot user demographics per Q19 and §10.4. v1.6 may evaluate one or more non-peer recovery candidate paths per [D0014](decisions/D0014-non-peer-recovery.md). The v1.5/v1.6 split reflects honest engineering scope per the §§8/9 review F9. v1.6 ships when: (a) v1.5 has shipped broader-than-pilot; (b) the engineering scope per §6.1 deferrals completes (~6–9 person-months at volunteer cadence, or compressed under UX-engineer funding); (c) reviewer-pool cadence per §8.2 (volunteer-attestation at the lower bound; honoraria operations if Phase C funding closes) supports the additional release.
