Engineering estimates and pacing assume volunteer baseline; funded-state cadence and team growth would compress the timeline correspondingly.

**v2.** USB-bootable form factor and iOS support. The USB form factor provides portable cryptographic identity for borrowed-laptop scenarios, with the multi-device protocol extension from [D0007](decisions/D0007-multi-device.md) deployed at this version per Section 6.4 forward-compatibility commitments. The USB substrate selection (Tails-derivative; Debian-live-based; from-scratch) is itself a v1.6/v2 question the brief defers to Q24 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md). iOS support extends the platform to users who cannot or will not move to GrapheneOS-Pixel; the iOS work depends on several upstream conditions the project does not control: (a) Apple App Store policies for Tor-integrating apps (which have historically affected anonymity-tool distribution); (b) iOS code-signing posture, which is not a native fit for Sigstore identity-based signing; (c) Briar's iOS unavailability — Briar's Tor-over-LAN/Bluetooth model has no iOS port, meaning v2 iOS users do not have access to the v1.5-promised highest-sensitivity Briar tier and the release-security stack per §5.5 requires restatement for iOS. v2 iOS therefore serves a meaningfully different (and lower) threat tier than v1's, and is honestly framed as a different audience rather than an extension of v1's. v2 ships when: (a) v1.5 architecture completeness has shipped; (b) iOS upstream dependencies are addressable (or the iOS portion is deferred to a later version with explicit reframing); (c) Phase D funding supports team growth per §8.1 and §9.1 — v2 scope is not solo-feasible at the volunteer baseline.

**v3.** Mesh radio integration via Meshtastic and MeshCore, addressing internet-shutdown scenarios. The integration is protocol-agnostic — supporting both Meshtastic's flood-routing model and MeshCore's intelligent-routing model — so users can follow local mesh community conventions. v3 depends on Meshtastic and MeshCore protocol stability over multi-year windows (both communities have made breaking changes within 18-month windows, and §8.6 contains no partner category for mesh-radio communities; partner outreach extension would be required) and on the v3-vs-v4+ hardware-partnership question being resolved (§6.2 lists hardware partnerships as v4+; §10.5 lists them as v3+ aspiration; the inconsistency is acknowledged and the resolution is named as Q25 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md)). v3 includes the trust-graph extension to handle mesh-relay capability scopes and the introduction of mesh-node device types in the multi-device model established in v2. v3 ships when: (a) v2 has shipped (USB establishes the multi-device pattern mesh nodes extend); (b) mesh-community partner relationships are established; (c) the hardware-partnership question resolves; (d) Phase D funding supports the additional engineering scope.

**v4 and beyond.** Established-organization tier with formal admin and governance controls, intended as the subsidy mechanism for the grassroots use case the rest of the roadmap supports. v4's "established-organization tier" presupposes the foundation entity exists with the legal/operational capacity to enter B2B-style relationships per §8.4 — it is the foundation-governance milestone, not just a product-tier expansion. Optional Matrix federation for enterprise users — the rejection of Matrix as a primary protocol in Section 5.4 does not preclude offering it as an opt-in channel for organizations that maintain their own homeservers. Hardware partnerships for pre-keyed devices may emerge as the user base grows and as device manufacturers consider security-tooling pre-installation. The honest framing — applied equally to v1.5, v2, and v3 above — is that the project reaches v4 only if the prior versions have delivered enough operational value to attract the funding and organizational commitment v4 work requires. §10.8 explicitly does not promise that the project reaches Phase C or Phase D; the same framing extends to the roadmap beyond v1.

### 7.2 Dependencies between releases

The architectural commitments in v1 are designed so each subsequent version extends the architecture without breaking v1 deployments. The specific dependencies:

**v1 enables v1.5.** The Briar integration in v1.5 reuses the v1 identity model, capability-token format, and trust-graph operation envelope; the operational identity signs Briar-channel attestations the same way it signs SimpleX-channel ones. The local trust-graph caching in v1.5 sits atop the Sigsum commitment-only logging already present in v1. The in-app post-coercion flow in v1.5 walks the user through the same revoke-recover-reissue sequence that v1 documents in written form. The duress-wipe pattern in v1.5 builds on the v1 secure-element key management; no architectural change is needed, only a new code path that destroys the key material on a duress passphrase.

**v1 and v1.5 enable v2.** The capability-token schema's multi-device support per [D0007](decisions/D0007-multi-device.md) is the architectural slot for v2's USB form factor. The v1 device-to-device pairing flow (Section 6.4) is the pairing flow USB uses. The Rust core per [D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md) cross-compiles to whatever platform the USB-bootable image targets; v1's Kotlin UI is replaced with a USB-environment-appropriate UI shell — likely a minimal terminal or framebuffer UI — without changing the core. iOS support reuses the Rust core with a Swift UI replacing Kotlin.

**v2 enables v3.** USB establishes the multi-device pattern (one operational identity, multiple device keys, multiple devices) that mesh radios extend to mesh nodes. The mesh-relay capability scope sits in the v1 forward-compatible scope vocabulary; the trust-graph extension to handle mesh device types reuses the v2 multi-device-attestation operation type.

**What requires architectural revisitation.** Through v3, no significant revisitation of v1 architecture is anticipated within current planning. Section 9.1 acknowledges, however, that the §5 adversarial review surfaced multiple cases where forward-compatibility claims required explicit decisions during implementation (D0007 demoted the multi-device claim; D0006 added schema fields that v1 deployments cannot retroactively gain); another such case may emerge during implementation or pilot, and the brief surfaces this as a known risk rather than a guarantee. The forward-compatibility design choices in Section 6.4 are intended to bound the migration work to additive changes, not to eliminate the possibility of architectural-revisitation surprises. v4's established-organization tier likely requires architectural extension for admin operations (organizational policies, audit logs of administrative actions, group governance) that v1 does not need to anticipate. Optional Matrix federation in v4 is explicitly an opt-in channel that does not change the primary protocol stack; it depends on v4's organizational tier providing the governance surface a Matrix federation participant needs.

**Governance and assurance milestones interlocked with the release sequence.** Several broader-release transitions in §7.1 are gated on milestones outside the engineering scope. These are surfaced here so a roadmap-first reader sees them at the same time the release sequence does:

- _Pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit_ per Section 8.5 / [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md) — Phase B event that gates v1 pilot deployment. Without it, v1 development continues but pilot does not deploy.
- _Pre-beta full audit_ per Section 8.5 / D0011 — Phase C event that gates v1.5 broader-than-pilot release. Without it, v1.5 work may continue but broader release is deferred.
- _Foundation incorporation_ per Section 8.4 / [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md) / [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) — deferred from v1 / Phase C scope with a v1.5 broader-release evaluation trigger. The decision is evaluated at v1.5 broader-release planning against the D0016 trigger criteria (pilot evidence of structural-mitigation need; funding scale crossing the fiscal-sponsor-vs-foundation-overhead breakeven; funder requirement for incorporation; personal-liability scenario; structural-mitigation cascade). If the trigger activates, the structural mitigations broader-release users receive (formalized Safe Harbor per [D0012](decisions/D0012-researcher-safe-harbor.md), board-bound governance, formal partner advisory authority per [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md), reviewer-honoraria operating model per Section 8.2) shift from "stated intent" to "structurally enforceable" — and the foundation incorporation process per D0010 commences. If the trigger does not activate, the structural mitigations remain at stated-intent posture indefinitely; the project continues operating under fiscal-sponsor arrangement with D0009 partner advisory authority as the permanent governance scaffold. Trigger non-activation is the expected outcome under several plausible operational trajectories (low sustained grant intake; no partner-organization requirements for formal Safe Harbor; no personal-liability incidents). The brief does not commit to a specific calendar timeline for trigger activation.
- _Reviewer honoraria operations_ per Section 8.2 — Phase C event that, when it closes at v1.5 onward, shifts release cadence from volunteer-baseline median 4–6 months per D0008 to quarterly target with sustained recruited-pool operations. Without Phase C honoraria funding closing, release cadence reverts to the volunteer-attestation baseline per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md); v1.5+ ships with whatever volunteer-attestation pool has formed through Q5 outreach rather than waiting for honoraria-funded recruitment.
- _Recurring audit cycle_ per Section 8.5 — second external cryptographic audit approximately 18–24 months after the first, amortized in Phase D budget (§10.4). Recurring audits land between v2 and v3 timing under current Phase D projection; audit findings may trigger architectural-revisitation work the roadmap does not currently plan for.

The roadmap's release headers in §7.1 reference these events directly. A funder, partner, or pilot user evaluating "when does v1.5 ship?" should read the release-condition list against the governance milestone list above, not interpolate from calendar windows the brief does not commit to.

### 7.3 Out-of-scope indefinitely

The project does not intend to do certain things, and naming them is part of the architectural commitment to honest scope. The list distinguishes things the project chooses not to do (architectural decisions under the project's control) from things that depend on external conditions the project does not control.

**Architectural choices not to do.**

- _Compete with Signal for mass-market secure messaging._ The threat tier Cairn addresses is meaningfully smaller than Signal's audience; the architectural cost of meeting that threat tier (GrapheneOS dependency, in-person provisioning, peer-based recovery) is disproportionate for users at lower threat tiers. Cairn is not the right tool for casual privacy, and the project does not pursue use cases where Signal is the better fit.
- _Build custom cryptographic primitives._ The cryptographic primitives Cairn uses are off-the-shelf — Ed25519, Curve25519, ChaCha20-Poly1305, Shamir Secret Sharing, COSE structures, Sigsum, Sigstore. The project's contribution is integration and operational discipline, not primitive invention (Section 4.3). Custom primitives would multiply the audit burden without producing security properties the existing primitives lack.
- _Run user-facing infrastructure that users must depend on._ The minimal-project-operated-infrastructure principle in Section 4.2 constrains the project to operating only infrastructure whose loss is recoverable: release distribution, Sigsum witness participation, project documentation. The user's daily messaging operates over substrates the project does not own.
- _Duress-profile concealment._ Per [D0002](decisions/D0002-duress-profile.md); the architectural answer to compelled unlock is tier separation, not concealment.
- _Compromise of GrapheneOS or Pixel hardware._ Section 3.5 names these as indefinitely out of scope; addressing them would require building a different product from the ground up. Cairn's security baseline depends on the platform's integrity, named explicitly in Section 3.4 trust roots.

**Conditions outside the project's control.**

- _Broader hardware availability._ The GrapheneOS-Pixel requirement narrows the v1 audience meaningfully (Section 2.2). Expansion of the addressable audience depends on Pixel hardware availability in target deployment regions, on GrapheneOS continuing to support the Pixel platform, and on alternative hardened-mobile-OS-with-secure-element options reaching the security baseline GrapheneOS provides. None of these are within the project's influence.
- _Regulatory changes in target jurisdictions._ Compelled-decryption laws (Section 3.3 references RIPA, Australia's TOLA Act, France's Article 434-15-2), platform-level encryption mandates, and regulations affecting Tor or pluggable-transport availability all shape what Cairn can deliver to users in specific jurisdictions. The project tracks these developments through partner relationships (Section 8.6 threat-intel category) but does not commit to specific responses to specific regulatory changes.
- _Reviewer pool composition over time._ Recruitment is the project's responsibility (Section 8.6); retention depends on partner organizations' own priorities and capacity over multi-year timelines.

The roadmap commits the project to specific extensions of the v1 architecture across v1.5, v2, and v3. The honest framing of v4 and beyond is that the project will reach v4 only if the prior versions have delivered enough operational value to attract the funding and organizational commitment that established-organization-tier work requires. The roadmap is a plan, not a guarantee; the architectural commitments in Section 6.4 ensure that the plan's deferrals do not foreclose later work even if specific timing estimates slip.

---

## 8. Operational and Governance Plan

This section describes how the project is operated, signed, audited, and sustained. Where Sections 2-7 establish architecture and scope, this section establishes the operational commitments that bind the project's daily practice. The register is deliberately conditional rather than aspirational: the project commits to what it unilaterally controls (documentation discipline, licensing, release-mechanism architecture, disclosure policy); it states intent subject to conditions for items dependent on funding, partner outreach, or external grant cycles. Specific partner organizations are listed as candidates rather than confirmed partners except where prior conversations have established a relationship.

Several commitments below depend additionally on the project's continued ability to operate at scale. Where the cadence, scope, or speed of a commitment depends on the volunteer reviewer pool's engagement or partner organizations' availability, those dependencies are named explicitly in the subsection rather than absorbed into "commits to" phrasing. The Sections 8/9 adversarial review identified several places where the earlier draft conflated unilateral commitment with conditional intent; specific operational adjustments under the self-funded-MVP baseline are recorded in [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md) (release cadence as slippage acceptance) and the prose below has been revised accordingly. Readers should treat "commits" in this section as the project's unilateral commitments only where the surrounding text does not name an external dependency; conditional commitments are explicitly framed as "intent subject to..." or named with their dependency.

### 8.1 Development team

**v1 implementation is solo.** v1 ships as the work of one developer. Section 9.1 acknowledges the single-point-of-failure risk this concentrates, and Section 9.4 documents the operational mitigations the project commits to (documentation discipline, public source from day one, decisions/ pattern, successor-handover preparation). The structural commitment is that the project state is recoverable by any successor if the developer becomes unavailable; there is no developer-only knowledge that would be lost.

**Team scaling is conditional on funding.** When and how the team grows depends on grant outcomes (Q3) and pilot feedback. The project's intent, subject to funding, is to add roles in approximately this order:

- **Part-time cryptographic consulting** (rolling review during implementation). The Section 5 adversarial review surfaced the gap that v1 ships substantial original cryptographic engineering (capability tokens, trust-graph operation envelope, share format, recovery-flow memory hygiene). For the v1 self-funded-MVP baseline, the audit posture is the self-audit plus pre-beta external review described in 8.5; rolling cryptographic consulting at $5-10K/month for 3-6 months of design-and-implementation phase is an aspirational addition when partnership or grant funding closes (Q3). Section 9.1 acknowledges the residual engineering risk this concentrates during the self-audit period.
- **UX-focused engineer** (Section 5.6 implementation effort is 30-50% of v1 total per the engineering review). Engaging an engineer specifically for the Signal-familiar surface, trust-badge rendering, recovery flow walkthroughs, and calibrated security-communication work would meaningfully accelerate v1.
- **Documentation and community-management role** (Section 5.7 acknowledges substantial documentation work; pilot facilitation requires partner-organization coordination). Likely a part-time contracted role.

Hires and contractor engagements prioritize institutional and geographic diversity. The project does not commit to specific compensation ranges in the design brief; compensation is set against market rates for security-focused engineering when grant funding permits and at below-market levels (with explicit acknowledgment to candidates) before funding closes. Hire commitments do not bind external candidates to availability the project cannot guarantee.

**Volunteer contribution path.** OSS-style contribution through the GitHub workflow is open from day one, subject to the contribution governance in 8.3. Volunteer contributors do not replace funded engineering for security-critical components; they extend the project's capacity for UX work, documentation, localization, and integration testing where pre-merge review by the developer (and external reviewers for security-relevant code) is feasible.

### 8.2 Release security operational policy

Section 5.5 specifies the release-security architecture. Per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md), v1 ships with developer-signed releases + public log audit (Rekor + Sigsum) + witness cosignatures + multi-channel distribution (F-Droid primary, Accrescent, direct download) + developer source review; the recruited 5+ reviewer pool with 3-of-5 attestation threshold defers to v1.5 alongside reproducible builds. Section 8.2 specifies the operational policy that runs the v1 architecture and the v1.5 expansion.

**Release cadence at v1 (per [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md) and D0015).** v1 release cadence is governed by engineering scope completion plus pre-pilot audit closing (per D0011 / §8.5) plus the partner-mediated pilot consent and exit protocol landing (per D0013) — not by reviewer-pool quorum formation, which deferred to v1.5. At the v1 self-funded-MVP volunteer baseline, the expected median interval between releases is 4-6 months, governed by D0008's slippage-acceptance posture and the developer's "as available" engineering cadence. Security-critical patches use the emergency-release path (below) when median timing is operationally inappropriate.

**Release cadence at v1.5 onward.** Once the recruited pool ships at v1.5, releases ship when 3-of-5 reviewer attestations form (the architectural threshold from Section 5.5). The cadence framing at v1.5+ is "as-quorum-forms" during volunteer-attestation operations and "target quarterly" once Phase C honoraria operations fund per Q3. Users and partners should plan against the actual cadence pattern. The architectural commitment (3-of-5 attestation threshold per release at v1.5 onward) does not change with the cadence reframing; only the timing expectation does.

**Emergency-release path (v1.5 onward).** Security-critical patches that cannot wait for the v1.5+ cadence use an expedited process: 2-of-5 reviewer attestation threshold for the emergency release, with the full 3-of-5 threshold verified in a follow-up release within 4 weeks. The expedited path requires a documented security justification (the specific vulnerability addressed, why it cannot wait for standard cadence, the security impact bound). At v1, emergency releases ship under the developer-source-review baseline with the same security justification documented; emergency releases at v1 do not require a quorum threshold because the v1 stack does not require a pool. Emergency releases are clearly labeled as such in the transparency log so users can audit which releases went through which process.

**Reviewer recruitment (Q5 outreach at v1; pool activates at v1.5).** Recruitment work proceeds at v1 through Q5 (NGO partner outreach per §8.6) in parallel with v1 engineering. The architectural target for v1.5 is 5+ recruited reviewers with 3-of-5 attestation threshold. Willing reviewers identified during v1 may soft-ship attestations against v1 releases as Sigsum log entries before the architectural commitment lands at v1.5, but v1 release is not gated on pool formation. If Phase C honoraria funding (§10.3) closes only at the lower bound or does not close before v1.5 ships, v1.5 may ship with a smaller volunteer-attestation pool (3+ recruited at lower bound) growing toward the 5+ architectural target across subsequent releases.

**Reviewer compensation (v1.5 onward).** For the v1.5+ self-funded-MVP baseline, the reviewer pool operates on a volunteer-attestation basis — reviewers contribute time because the project's mission aligns with their own work, with public acknowledgment in release notes as the recognition mechanism. Honoraria become the operational model once partnership or grant funding closes (Q3); the project's commitment is that reviewer time is compensated fairly relative to security-engineering market rates for the equivalent work as soon as the project can sustain it, with the amount specified in published partnership terms at that point. Honoraria are budgeted as a grant-cycle-bound operational cost in Section 10 — committed for the duration of the grant cycle from which they are funded, with the project's intent (not commitment) to renew honoraria in subsequent cycles. At v1, no honoraria commitment applies because the recruited pool has not yet been activated; reviewers willing to soft-ship attestations against v1 releases do so on the same volunteer-attestation basis the v1.5 baseline establishes. The volunteer-to-honoraria-to-volunteer transition is a possible trajectory the reviewer pool should evaluate at recruitment; the project commits to surfacing the funding state to the pool transparently so reviewers can decide whether the project's posture aligns with their own availability over time.

**Reviewer onboarding (v1.5 onward; v1 setup proceeds during Q5 outreach).** The project maintains a Docker- or Nix-pinned reviewer toolkit that reproduces the build environment a reviewer needs to perform their function. From v1.5 the toolkit supports binary-equivalence verification against reproducible builds; for any v1 reviewers willing to soft-ship attestations during the recruitment period, the toolkit supports source review (a checked-out source tree at a tagged release commit, with tooling for running the project's own property-based tests and fuzz tests against the source). The project's commitment for the volunteer baseline is to maintain the toolkit at LTS-class pins refreshed semi-annually rather than per-release — acknowledging that toolchain-currency drift between reviewers is operationally acceptable. Per-release toolkit maintenance becomes the commitment once honoraria fund and the engineering time for upstream-tracking responsiveness is sustainably budgeted. Reviewer-side toolkit issues are supported on best-effort during the volunteer baseline; the toolkit itself is open source so review of the review process is itself possible.

**Reviewer rotation (v1.5 onward).** The project targets an 18-month rotation cadence with overlap between outgoing and incoming reviewers so attestation continuity is not disrupted from v1.5 onward. Rotation is conditional on the reviewer pool being recruitable at the rotation cycle; if the recruited pool is smaller than 5+ or rotation is impractical, the project commits to making the constraint visible rather than silently shipping with degraded review properties. At v1, no rotation commitment applies because the recruited pool has not yet activated.

**Public release artifacts.** Each v1 release publishes: the Sigstore-signed artifact, the Rekor entry, the witness-pool cosignatures of the Sigsum release log entry, any reviewer attestations that have soft-shipped during recruitment (Sigsum-anchored if present), public release notes including security-relevant changes, and a CHANGELOG that explicitly distinguishes security-relevant changes from feature/UX changes. From v1.5 onward, each release additionally publishes the recruited reviewer attestations as Sigsum entries reaching the 3-of-5 threshold. The transparency log preserves this record indefinitely.

**Incident response.** Section 5.5 documents the Sigstore-identity-compromise and APK-key-compromise response plans. Section 9.4 documents the broader incident response posture. The operational specifics — runbooks, escalation contacts, communication channels — live in `docs/runbooks/` once that directory is established (likely concurrent with v1 alpha).

### 8.3 Code and contribution governance

**License: Apache 2.0.** The project is released under Apache 2.0 — a permissive license that maximizes downstream reuse by allied projects, allows adoption by enterprise users (relevant for the v4+ established-organization tier), and does not impose copyleft constraints that would inhibit partner organizations from incorporating components into their own tooling. The license choice was made at project inception (referenced in handoff materials and consistent with the partner ecosystem the project operates in).

**Contribution intake: Developer Certificate of Origin.** Contributors sign off on commits using DCO ([developercertificate.org](https://developercertificate.org)) rather than a Contributor License Agreement. The DCO is lighter touch — no separate signing infrastructure, sign-off is a one-line addition to commit messages — and sufficient for IP-cleanliness. The project does not require copyright assignment.

**Commit signing.** Sigstore-anchored commit signatures are required for any commit touching cryptographic primitives, identity tier code, trust graph operations, recovery flow, capability-token handling, or release-security tooling. Commits touching UI, documentation, build tooling not in the security path, or localization may be unsigned with maintainer countersignature. The line between security-critical and non-security-critical commits is documented in the contribution guide and re-evaluated during the project's quarterly review cycle.

**Code review.** External-reviewer-pool review is required for security-critical changes — the same pool from Section 5.5, or a subset designated for code review specifically. Maintainer review is sufficient for non-security changes. Cryptographic changes additionally require sign-off from the rolling cryptographic consultant (when that role is filled per 8.1). The project does not merge security-critical changes without external review under normal circumstances. Under genuine deadline pressure (security-critical patch responding to active threat), the emergency-release path's reduced threshold (per 8.2: 2-of-5 attestation, with 3-of-5 follow-up in the next release) applies to commit-level review as well: 2 reviewer sign-offs at commit time, with the full external review completed in the subsequent release cycle. The project distinguishes "no merge without review" (true) from "no merge with fewer than 3 reviews" (only true outside the emergency path); the earlier "unconditional" framing conflated the two.

**Code of conduct.** The project adopts Contributor Covenant v2.1 as the baseline, with project-specific additions for the threat-tier context: no doxxing of contributors, careful handling of pilot user information, explicit discouragement of speculation about user identities or operational specifics. Enforcement is by a small project-internal team (developer + 1-2 maintainers when available) with the option to refer disputes to a partner organization's conduct process when the project is too small to handle them internally.

**Maintainer succession.** Documented in operations runbook concurrent with v1 alpha (per 8.2). The Section 9.4 sunset plan is the long-term backstop if succession itself fails.

### 8.4 Path to foundation

**Intent: evaluate foundation incorporation at v1.5 broader-release planning per [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md); operate under fiscal-sponsor arrangement for the duration of the deferral, which may be permanent.** Foundation incorporation is deferred from v1 / Phase C scope with a v1.5 broader-release evaluation trigger that surfaces five criteria (pilot evidence of structural-mitigation need; funding scale crossing the fiscal-sponsor-vs-foundation-overhead breakeven; funder requirement for incorporation; personal-liability scenario; structural-mitigation cascade). If two or more criteria activate at the v1.5 evaluation, the project proceeds with incorporation per [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md)'s framework; if fewer than two criteria activate, the project continues operating under fiscal-sponsor arrangement with [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) partner advisory authority as the permanent governance scaffold. Trigger non-activation is the expected outcome under several plausible operational trajectories (low sustained grant intake; no partner-organization requirements for formal Safe Harbor; no personal-liability incidents requiring incorporation mitigation).

The brief does not commit to a specific calendar timeline for trigger activation. The "approximately 18–24 months post-v1" framing earlier drafts of the brief carried has been superseded by the trigger framework — that figure was an assertion the brief did not have the evidence to support, and the trigger captures the evidence-dependent nature of the decision honestly.

**If the trigger activates: incorporation framework per D0010.** The candidate jurisdictions, legal-consultation framework, and selection criteria below remain the project's working understanding of how incorporation would proceed if the trigger activates. The analysis is the substrate D0010 specifies; the trigger evaluation determines whether the project acts on it.

**Candidate jurisdictions (placeholder pending legal consultation, per [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md)).** The analysis below is the project's working understanding pending consultation with specialized non-profit counsel in each candidate jurisdiction. The brief does not present this analysis as a settled evaluation; the Sections 8/9 adversarial review identified that the previous version was at a depth a foundation lawyer would not consider sufficient for incorporation decision. The candidates considered are:

- **US 501(c)(3) public charity.** Strongest donor tax-deductibility for U.S. donors (international donor tax-deductibility depends on tax treaties); mature legal framework; well-understood operational requirements. The disadvantage: U.S. jurisdiction is one whose legal process is already in the project's trust placement (Section 3.4, Sigstore OIDC trust root). A U.S.-incorporated foundation operating Cairn would compound that placement; partners and users would have a single jurisdictional concentration to evaluate.
- **Dutch Stichting.** Strong donor protections, lower jurisdictional adverse-action risk relative to the U.S. for the target user populations, EU regulatory framework with mature data-protection law. Tax treaty considerations apply for non-EU donor tax-deductibility.
- **Swiss Verein or non-profit Aktiengesellschaft.** Historically privacy-friendly jurisdiction, neutral political stance, strong banking and donor-management infrastructure. Lower donor tax-deductibility outside Switzerland. (Earlier brief versions incorrectly attributed this model to Briar Project; Briar's commercial entity was a German UG operating alongside a UK Community Interest Company, not a Swiss Verein.)
- **UK Community Interest Company or charity.** Simpler incorporation relative to charity status, English-language governance familiar to many partner organizations, post-Brexit regulatory framework with some uncertainty. (This is the structure Briar Project CIC uses.)

The earlier draft characterized the Dutch Stichting candidate as the "Signal Foundation precedent"; Signal Foundation is in fact incorporated as a Delaware 501(c)(3), not a Dutch Stichting. The factual correction matters because partners' legal advisors notice the error in first read, and accuracy on this point is a baseline credibility marker.

**Permanent fiscal-sponsor arrangement under the D0016 deferral.** During the deferral period (which may be permanent if the trigger does not activate), grant intake from candidate funders (OTF, Ford, OSF, Mozilla, NLnet) requires either a fiscal sponsor or routing through a partner organization, because grants to natural persons are non-standard at most program structures. Under the D0016 framework, the fiscal sponsor is operationally permanent rather than transitional — the selection should be made on permanent-operational-fit criteria (sustainability of fee structure; established multi-year track record; cultural alignment with the project's mission; jurisdictional fit) rather than transitional-suitability criteria. NLnet's grantee-sponsorship model avoids the 5–15% fee that other fiscal sponsors charge per routed grant and is therefore the financially preferable route within the fiscal-sponsor framing where mission fit allows. Candidate fiscal sponsors to evaluate during outreach (per Q15 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md)):

- **Software Freedom Conservancy.** Established 501(c)(3) hosting security and free-software projects; experienced with sponsorship agreements preserving maintainer autonomy.
- **Open Collective Foundation / Open Source Collective.** Lower-overhead sponsorship; appropriate for smaller initial grants.
- **Code for Science & Society.** Mission-aligned for civil-society security tools; experienced with OTF, Ford, Mozilla grants.
- **NumFOCUS.** Established sponsor for security and scientific computing projects.
- **NLnet Foundation** (Netherlands-based). Operates funded calls (NGI Zero) that can serve as both grantmaker and de facto fiscal sponsor for European-jurisdiction-friendly arrangements.

The project commits to engaging the fiscal-sponsor question before any first grant intake. Specific fiscal-sponsor selection is deferred to Q15 outreach. Under the [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) deferral, fiscal-sponsor selection should be treated as a permanent operational decision rather than a transitional one — the project may operate under the chosen fiscal sponsor indefinitely.

**Diligence considerations for legal consultation.** When the project engages legal counsel ahead of incorporation, the following considerations require evaluation that the brief does not analyze at depth pre-consultation:

- Tax-treaty implications for the international donor base across candidate jurisdictions.
- Specific case law on encryption product distribution from the candidate jurisdiction (US EAR/ITAR, EU Dual Use Regulation, Wassenaar Arrangement adherence).
- Donor-identity disclosure regimes (US Form 990, Swiss strong-protection norms, Dutch/UK intermediate regimes); relevant for donors whose identity disclosure is itself a security risk.
- Asset seizure and litigation venue standards per jurisdiction.
- Banking access for privacy-tech non-profits in the candidate jurisdiction.
- IP-ownership and assignment mechanics for the natural-person-to-foundation transition.

**Selection criteria when the D0016 trigger activates and incorporation proceeds.** The criteria above are the considerations legal consultation will use; the project's current criteria are: jurisdictional legal protections (especially around source-data subpoenas and donor disclosure); long-term board governance stability and operating cost; tax-treaty support for international donations; precedent and operational experience of similar projects in each jurisdiction.

**Board composition criteria (conditional on incorporation).** If the D0016 trigger activates and the project incorporates, the intended board would include (at minimum): a technical advisor with cryptographic expertise; a representative from a partner organization in the user-facing-support role (Front Line Defenders, Tactical Tech, or equivalent); a community representative drawn from pilot users or pilot-adjacent users with operational experience of the threat tier; an executive director or equivalent operational role (compensated when funded); and a treasurer or audit-committee chair with non-profit financial-governance experience. The board would take over the partner advisory authority role for the developer-unavailability contingency (per [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md)), formalizing what continues to be a partner-arranged role under the deferral. The board does not own the trust graph, the signing identities, or operate user-facing infrastructure — those remain at the architectural layer consistent with the minimal-project-operated-infrastructure principle in 4.2.

**Governance scaffold under the D0016 deferral (the not-incorporated baseline).** If the D0016 trigger does not activate, [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) partner advisory authority is the project's permanent governance scaffold rather than a transitional state. The project commits to: maintaining the D0009 monthly dead-man's-switch check-in indefinitely; renewing the partner advisory authority arrangement per Q14 selection at its multi-year cycle indefinitely; surfacing the governance posture transparently in §9.4 so partners and pilot users understand the partner-arranged advisory authority is the project's permanent governance commitment under the deferral. The structural mitigations (formalized Safe Harbor, board-bound governance, formal partner advisory authority) remain at "stated intent" posture indefinitely unless the trigger activates.

### 8.5 Audit and assurance

**v1 self-audit and tooling.** During implementation, the project commits to: property-based testing for the trust-graph CRDT (per [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md) cascade semantics) and operation envelope; fuzz testing for COSE/CBOR parsers, the Shamir reconstruction code, and the capability-token verifier; known-answer tests for cryptographic primitives (matching test vectors from RFC 8032, RFC 9052, and equivalent standards); differential testing against the SimpleX reference implementation where Cairn reuses its protocol semantics; continuous integration enforcing all of the above on every commit; static analysis via Clippy and equivalent Kotlin tooling.

**Rolling cryptographic consultation during implementation.** Per 8.1, the project's preferred funding allocation includes part-time cryptographic consulting through the implementation phase. The consultant reviews design decisions as they are made rather than evaluating finished code, which is the leverage point the Section 5 review identified.

**Two-stage external audit (per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md)).** The project commissions two external audits at different points in the v1 lifecycle, addressing both the audit-budget-vs-market-rate gap and the pilot-users-on-unaudited-crypto exposure identified in the Sections 8/9 review:

- _Pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit._ Limited scope: capability-token COSE_Sign1 envelope verification (per [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md)); Shamir Secret Sharing reconstruction and memory hygiene (per [D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md)); trust-graph operation envelope's nine-field schema and signature chain; recovery-flow cryptographic operations. Budget: $15-30K at subsidy-tier rates (Open Tech Fund Secure Audit program, Cure53 mission-org rates), $30-50K at unsubsidized small-engagement rates. Realistically 1-2 person-weeks of senior auditor time. Purpose: pilot users receive an implementation whose cryptographic core has been externally reviewed; the audit-after-pilot exposure is bounded to the integration boundary rather than to the primitives.
- _Pre-beta full audit._ Broader scope: full cryptographic-primitive review (re-confirming against v1.5 changes); full trust-graph operation handling; full recovery flow; capability-token construction; release-security stack including Sigstore, Rekor, Sigsum integrations and the v1.5 recruited reviewer pool operational verification (the pool's attestation toolkit, the v1.5 reproducible-build pipeline, and the binary-equivalence verification process per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)); UnifiedPush implementation if push is enabled in v1.5. Budget: $60-220K reflecting market rates at named firms (Cure53 / Quarkslab toward the lower end; Trail of Bits / NCC Group at the upper end). Lower bound depends on subsidy programs (Open Tech Fund audit grants; Cure53 mission-org rates; Trail of Bits civic-tech engagement rates); upper bound is unsubsidized engagement at standard rates for a 4-6 week engagement. The audit explicitly does not duplicate upstream-project audits (SimpleX, Briar, Sigstore, Sigsum each have their own audit history).

The project commits to applying to named subsidy programs as the first funding route for both stages: Open Tech Fund Secure Audit program, Cure53 mission-org rates, Mozilla Open Source Audit Awards, NLnet NGI Zero Trust calls. The lower-bound budgets are conditional on one or more subsidy programs closing for Cairn.

If audit funding does not close before pilot completion (for the pre-beta audit), the project keeps the pilot active and defers broader release rather than skipping the audit. The no-skip-the-audit commitment holds as a structural posture: the project does not ship broader-than-pilot release without a pre-beta audit. The previous draft framed this as "unconditional"; the Sections 8/9 review identified that the commitment depends on the project's continued ability to operate the pilot indefinitely under self-funded posture, which is not unilaterally guaranteed. The honest framing is that the project does not skip the audit under any planned circumstance — but if pilot operations themselves become unsustainable, the project initiates the [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) developer-unavailability contingency or the 9.4 sunset process rather than shipping unaudited.

**Continuous review post-launch.** External code review for security-critical changes (per 8.3). Annual review-pool engagement renewal. Trust-roots health reporting per Section 9.4. A second external cryptographic audit approximately 18-24 months after the first (timing depends on the rate of cryptographic-relevant changes and the pilot-feedback evidence).

**Audit-report publication.** Full audit reports published on the project's transparency log alongside releases. Remediation status is tracked publicly through a documented issue-resolution log. The project does not publish audit reports that are partially-remediated without explicit acknowledgment of unresolved findings.

**Bug bounty and the broker market.** A meaningful bug bounty requires meaningful funding (industry-typical small-project bounty pools start at $10K-$50K; pools at the project's threat tier are typically $100K+) and dedicated triage capacity. v1 does not have either. The harder question, however, is the bounty-versus-broker dynamic at this threat tier: a $100K bounty pool is below the price _adversaries_ pay for the same vulnerabilities through Zerodium-equivalent brokers (public pricing for Android one-click chains has been $1-2.5M in recent years). A bug-bounty program at any realistic funded level operates against the same researcher pool that has higher-paying buyers; the v1 deferral cannot be solved by "v2+ funding" alone, and the project should not frame it as a funding-only question.

The realistic v1 model — and one suited to the threat tier — is coordinated-disclosure relationships with research labs whose work model is structured to _disclose_ rather than _sell_: Citizen Lab, Amnesty International Security Lab, Access Now Digital Security Helpline's technical staff, EFF Threat Lab, academic security-research groups. Cairn's audience overlaps with their research subjects; their incentive is publication and protection, not vulnerability monetization. Combined with the disclosure policy below, this is the realistic answer for the v1 phase. A bounty pool is a v2+ candidate as a supplement once funding permits, not as the primary defense.

**Researcher protection: stated intent indefinitely under the [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) deferral; formalization _if_ the trigger activates per [D0012](decisions/D0012-researcher-safe-harbor.md).** The project operates a documented security disclosure policy with PGP-encrypted contact, 90-day default disclosure timeline, and public acknowledgment of good-faith researchers. The project's stated intent is not to pursue legal action against research disclosed in good faith. Under the D0016 deferral, this is a published preference rather than a legal protection — the project is operated by a natural person (the developer) whose commitment is not enforceable against future personal action, successors, or coercion-induced exceptions. _If_ the D0016 trigger activates and the project incorporates per Section 8.4, the project will formalize researcher protection through a Safe Harbor commitment based on a standard template (candidates: disclose.io, Bugcrowd "We Will Not Sue", EFF Coders' Rights Project; template selection tracked as Q16 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md)). If the trigger does not activate, the published-preference posture persists indefinitely. The current absence of formal Safe Harbor is acknowledged here so researchers can make an informed decision about disclosure to Cairn; the deferral framing means the absence may be permanent rather than transitional.

### 8.6 Partnership approach

The project's relationship with partner organizations is collaborative rather than vendor/customer. Partners contribute to the project's mission; the project contributes to partners' work supporting their user communities. The partnership commitments below describe the project's intentions and the role categories the project will seek partners for; they are not confirmed partnership arrangements. Q5 (NGO partner outreach) tracks the conversations that will turn these intentions into agreements. The brief makes no commitments on behalf of partner organizations that have not been consulted.

**Role categories the project seeks partners for.**

- **Technical reviewers (v1.5 ship target per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md); recruitment work proceeds at v1).** Section 5.5 release attestation; 5+ pool with 3-of-5 threshold from v1.5 onward. Recruitment criteria: institutional independence from the developer, geographic and jurisdictional diversity, demonstrated technical capacity to evaluate cryptographic and Android implementation code. Candidate sources include academic security-research groups (UC Berkeley, ETH Zurich, KU Leuven), individuals affiliated with civil-society security organizations (Tactical Tech analytic staff, Access Now helpline staff, EFF staff with relevant background — contingent on each organization's institutional process for cross-project review participation, which Cairn does not presume), and individuals with established public records in the field. Citizen Lab and similar academic research centers operate under university research governance; participation in third-party project release attestation is not their standard collaboration model, and the brief identifies Citizen Lab primarily as a potential research collaborator under the threat-intelligence category below rather than as a routine reviewer. Recruitment work at v1 builds the pool that activates as a release-security commitment at v1.5; willing reviewers identified during v1 may soft-ship attestations against v1 releases but v1 release is not gated on pool formation.
- **Sigsum witnesses** (Section 5.2 trust-graph audit and Section 5.5 release log cosignature). Recruitment criteria: independent of reviewers where feasible (to mitigate the shared-witness-pool concentration acknowledged in 3.4), operational capacity to maintain witness cosignature over multi-year timelines, alignment with the project's mission. Candidate organizations overlap with the reviewer pool but with explicit intent to assemble non-overlapping subsets.
- **Pilot facilitation** (Section 5.6 in-person provisioning, user onboarding, recovery-flow walkthroughs). v1 pilot facilitator is the developer; broader deployment depends on partner organizations operating as facilitator networks. Candidate organizations and the relevant capacities the project would discuss with them: Tactical Tech (Security in-a-Box training infrastructure); Front Line Defenders (protection casework); Access Now Digital Security Helpline (technical-support infrastructure that may be relevant to a future facilitator-network model). Each partnership shape would be negotiated with the organization's program leadership; the brief does not characterize partner organizations' existing user relationships as available channels for Cairn pilot expansion, since those relationships belong to the partner and are theirs to evaluate against any Cairn ask.
- **Threat intelligence** (incident reporting, forensic analysis of compromise events affecting Cairn users). The project does not duplicate the forensic capacity of Citizen Lab, Amnesty International Security Lab, or similar; partnership intent is bidirectional information-sharing where compromise events affect Cairn users and the project's data could inform threat-intel research.
- **Localization** (Q6 — native-speaker security trainers as translators, with security-critical UI text requiring domain-expert translation review). Candidate organizations: Tactical Tech's localization network, Front Line Defenders' translation infrastructure, individual translators recruited through partner organizations who maintain native-language trainer networks.
- **User training and onboarding** (front-line support for users facing the threat tier, post-onboarding). Front Line Defenders, Access Now Digital Security Helpline, regional civil-society security organizations (CDR, SHARE Foundation, EngageMedia, others depending on regional pilot scope).
- **Threat intel reception and processing.** The project receives incident reports from partner organizations and integrates the findings into release-security responses and into the trust-roots health reporting in 9.4.

**Specific partner-role assignments emerge from outreach.** The candidate organizations listed above are organizations whose mission and capacity match the role category; assignment to a specific role at a specific organization happens through Q5 outreach, not through the design brief. Some organizations may decline, suggest alternative role configurations, or propose partnership shapes the project has not anticipated; the brief preserves the project's flexibility to respond rather than committing the project to inflexible role assignments before conversations begin.

**Partnership terms the project commits to.** Clear documentation of what partners contribute and receive; honoraria budgeted as ongoing operational cost (Section 10) for roles requiring sustained time commitment; public acknowledgment of partner contributions on the project's documentation and release artifacts; partnership agreements that preserve partner organizations' editorial and operational independence — the project does not require partners to endorse positions, decline working with other projects, or limit their public communication about Cairn.

**The honest framing.** The partnership picture above is the project's intent. It depends on conversations the project has not yet held, on funding (Q3) that has not yet closed, and on partner organizations' own capacity and priorities, which the project does not control. Partner relationships are the project's largest source of operational risk after the solo-developer SPOF (Section 9.1); they are also the project's strongest path to operational sustainability. The conversations that turn this intent into binding agreements begin once the design brief is sharable.

---

## 9. Risks and Limitations

### 9.1 Project risks

The product cannot ship if the project does not function. The project's own risks — distinct from the product's design choices — are named here so funders, partners, and pilot users can evaluate them before committing.

**Solo-developer single-point-of-failure.** The v1 implementation is the work of one developer. Illness, burnout, family obligation, accident, or any other interruption that takes the developer offline for a sustained period stops v1 development. This is the most concentrated risk in the project's structure. Mitigations live in Section 9.4 (sustainability commitments, documentation discipline, partner relationships) but the fundamental constraint remains: at v1 scale, the developer is the project. v2+ scale assumes team growth (Section 8.1 placeholder); v1 does not.

**Funding non-materialization, in two distinct horizons.** The project's funding strategy (Q3 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md)) is structured so that v1 MVP and pilot do not depend on grants: the developer covers development on their own time and pilot hardware out of pocket, with the architecture demonstrably working before any partner conversation or funding ask. This stance removes the "fund-then-build" risk that affects many similar projects.

_Self-funding floor._ The developer's self-funding capacity is finite. The brief does not state a specific calendar floor in this section because the floor depends on the developer's personal financial situation and on the cost of v1 hardware acquisition for the pilot. Section 10 (when drafted) will state the developer's effective self-funding runway in calendar terms — the number of months the developer can sustain v1 development plus pilot operations under self-funded posture with no audit, no honoraria, and no team scaling. A program officer evaluating timeline risk needs that number; the project commits to providing it as the financial floor of all v1 funding-related risk discussions, rather than leaving it implicit.

_Post-pilot operations risk._ The pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit and the pre-beta full audit (Section 8.5; per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md), $15-30K and $60-150K respectively at subsidized tier), reviewer-pool honoraria as the project moves from volunteer baseline to compensated operations (Section 8.2), and v1.5 implementation work all involve cost that exceeds the developer's self-funded capacity over time. If grants and partnership funding do not materialize between MVP and broader release, the project sustains the pilot indefinitely without the broader-release transition rather than skipping the audit or burning out the volunteer reviewer pool — with the qualification (per Section 8.5) that if pilot operations themselves become unsustainable, the project initiates the [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) developer-unavailability contingency or the 9.4 sunset process rather than shipping unaudited.

_Long-horizon sustainability risk._ The project's foundation incorporation, v2 and v3 development, and partner-organization infrastructure all depend on funding at scales individual donations alone cannot reach. The Open Technology Fund (primary candidate), foundation backups (Ford, Open Society, Mozilla, Knight, Omidyar), and European democracy funds (SIDA, GIZ, EIDHR, Dutch Foreign Ministry, NLnet NGI Zero, Internet Society Foundation) are the routes; grant cycles operate on multi-month timescales, so the project plans for non-linear funding timing rather than assuming alignment with development milestones. Reviewer-pool erosion risk increases during grant-cycle transitions where honoraria continuity is uncertain (the volunteer-to-honoraria-to-volunteer trajectory is a real possibility per Section 8.2).

**Pilot may not validate the architecture.** The pilot deployment (10-15 users in 1-2 local groups per Section 6.3) is the project's only direct evidence about whether the architectural choices work for the audience they target. Specific failure modes that would constitute a pilot non-validation: users find the recovery flow operationally unworkable (the 48-hour cooling-off window, the peer-challenge protocol, the in-person verification requirement); pilot peers cannot or will not sustain their roles as share-holders across the pilot period; the facilitator-led provisioning model does not scale even at pilot scale; the trust-graph UX is unintelligible despite the calibrated language; the polling-default for push notifications proves too costly for routine operation. If any of these surface in the pilot, the project must choose between scope cuts (reducing the architecture to match what works), redesign (reworking the failing piece), or honest sunset (acknowledging the design does not meet its stated goals).

**An architectural mistake in v1 could constrain future versions.** The forward-compatibility design choices in Section 6.4 are intended to prevent this — protocol versioning, extensible scope strings, storage versioning — but the Section 5 adversarial review surfaced multiple cases where forward-compatibility claims required explicit decisions ([D0007](decisions/D0007-multi-device.md) demoted the multi-device claim; [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md) added schema fields that v1 deployments cannot retroactively gain). Another such case may emerge during implementation or pilot, and the v1 design that has already shipped may not be cleanly extensible. Mitigations: continued adversarial review at each section, partner technical review before v1 ships, and willingness to break v1 backward compatibility for v1.5 if the alternative is shipping a broken architecture forward.

**Reviewer and witness pools may not form or may erode.** Per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md), the recruited reviewer pool defers to v1.5 — v1 release shipping is not gated on pool formation, and the v1-ship risk associated with pool non-formation is therefore minimal. The witness pool, by contrast, is a v1 commitment (Section 5.5 release log + Section 5.2 trust-graph audit; Q5 outreach drives recruitment). From v1.5 onward, the reviewer pool re-enters the risk register: recruitment is contingent on Q3 (funding for honoraria) and Q5 (NGO partner outreach) resolving; erosion is a longer-term risk (reviewers who attest one release but not the next, witnesses who stop cosigning the log state, partners who shift focus). The release-cadence dependence on 3-of-5 attestation at v1.5+ means erosion translates directly to release delays at v1.5+ — but not at v1, where cadence depends on engineering scope plus pre-pilot audit plus pilot consent protocol, not pool quorum.

**Brief-to-funded-development timeline may slip.** Section 10.4 estimates roughly 6 months from brief completion to funded development. If partner outreach is slower than expected, if external review surfaces material that requires substantive revisions, or if funding cycles do not align with the project's readiness, this timeline extends — and the longer the gap, the higher the risk that early commitments (pilot users, reviewer pool, funding window) drift out of alignment.

### 9.2 Product limitations

The product does not do everything the audience may want or need. The limitations below are honest about what v1 ships and what is deferred to subsequent versions or indefinitely.

**v1 does not survive internet shutdowns.** The v3 mesh radio integration (Meshtastic, MeshCore) is the architectural answer to state-imposed connectivity disruption. v1 and v1.5 require functional internet access — without it, neither SimpleX nor Briar (v1.5) can operate. For populations in jurisdictions with recurring connectivity disruption — a recognized adversarial tactic in several regions the audience operates in — v1 is not the right tool until v3 ships, roughly 18-24 months post-v1.

**v1 does not support the borrowed-laptop workflow.** The v2 USB-bootable form factor is the architectural answer to scenarios where the user does not have continuous custody of trusted hardware. For users who travel without a primary device, who must work from shared computers, or who have specific operational reasons not to carry a phone, v1 is not the right tool until v2 ships, roughly 12-18 months post-v1.

**The addressable user base in v1 is meaningfully smaller than the full target audience.** The GrapheneOS-on-Pixel requirement narrows the v1 audience to users who already use Pixel hardware (or can be supplied with one through the pilot) and who have or can install GrapheneOS. Globally, this is a minority of mobile-device users in the threat tier Section 2 targets. The product is honest about this constraint (Section 2.2): v1 is not "secure messaging for journalists" — it is "secure messaging for journalists using GrapheneOS-Pixel, onboarded in person." The audience expands across v1.5 (Briar tier, full architecture), v2 (iOS support, USB form factor), and v3 (mesh radio), but never to "anyone with any device."

**Social recovery depends on the user's peer network having specific properties.** The Shamir-3-of-5 model assumes the user has at least five people they trust to act as recovery peers — geographically distributed, socially distant from each other, capable of refusing coercion under pressure. Users without such a network can still use the product but cannot use the recovery mechanism in its intended form. The fresh-identity-with-in-person-reintroduction path (Section 5.3) is the fallback, accepting loss of historical state. For users in extremely isolated operational contexts — a single journalist with no peer network of similar threat tier — this is a real limitation.

**Recovery requires online connectivity to Sigsum in v1.** The v1 trust-graph evaluation queries Sigsum directly (per [D0004](decisions/D0004-v1-scope-cuts.md)); a user offline at the moment of recovery cannot evaluate new attestations they have not yet seen. v1.5 adds local caching that mitigates the most common case but does not address full-offline recovery. The CRDT that would have supported full-offline operation is not planned (per D0004); users requiring offline trust evaluation should consider the product not appropriate for their use case at v1 or any planned version.

**v1 ships English-only.** Localization (Q6 in open-questions) extends post-pilot, with the i18n architectural slot present in v1 (string resources, Section 5.7). Users for whom English is not operationally accessible cannot use the v1 product safely — security-critical UI text being read in a second language is its own failure mode. v1.5 begins localization to languages indicated by pilot user demographics; broader language coverage extends across v1.5 and v2.

**Push notifications are off by default in v1.** The polling default (Section 5.4) trades latency and battery cost for the metadata-channel reduction that disabling push provides. For users whose operational rhythm requires near-real-time message delivery, this is a meaningful UX cost. v1.5 may revisit the default based on pilot feedback (Q12).

**Forward-compatibility decisions might prove incorrect.** Section 6.4 lists the forward-compatibility commitments; the Section 5 review surfaced two of them (multi-device awareness, CRDT) requiring explicit demotion or removal. Another such case may emerge in v1.5 or v2 implementation. The mitigation is willingness to break compatibility cleanly when the alternative is shipping a broken extension forward.

**v1 → v1.5/v1.6 transition compatibility.** v1 clients continuing to work when v1.5/v1.6 ships is contingent on the forward-compatibility design (Section 6.4) holding. D0006 and D0007 already represent forward-compatibility commitments demoting during Section 5 review; the risk is real. Users on v1 should expect at minimum one update cycle to interoperate with v1.5 clients post-v1.5 launch.

**GrapheneOS supply-chain dependency.** GrapheneOS itself depends on Google's distribution of Pixel firmware images for verified boot, secure-element drivers, and the platform's broader supply chain. Google can withdraw Pixel devices from market, modify Pixel firmware in ways that affect GrapheneOS compatibility, or be coerced into changes that compromise the platform. Cairn's v1 platform requirement (Section 6.1) breaks if Google ceases shipping Pixel devices or restricts GrapheneOS-compatible firmware. Neither is within the project's influence; the limitation persists across v1 and v1.5.

**Fresh-identity recovery path produces a usable identity only when the user's network is partly intact.** Section 5.3's fresh-identity-by-reintroduction fallback assumes the user has a network capable of reintroducing them. Users whose entire network has been compromised — the worst case for the audience this product targets — do not have a usable recovery path beyond the fresh identity itself, which has an empty trust graph and no historical context. The architecture cannot reconstruct what does not exist in the user's social network at recovery time.

**Sigstore certificate expiration and offline-verification limits.** Sigstore identities are tied to short-lived certificates, and release-attestation verification at install time depends on Rekor entries that the client either fetches at install or caches from an earlier known-good state. A user offline at install time for long enough that previously-verified releases have expired Sigstore certificates and no Rekor anchor cached may face verification failures or weaker fallback checks. The architecture does not solve this for fully-offline users.

**UnifiedPush distributor metadata channel.** When push notifications are enabled (v1 ships off by default per Section 5.4; v1.5 may revisit), the UnifiedPush distributor sees per-user notification arrival timing even when it cannot see content. The distributor is a metadata-channel surface that Section 3.3's Metadata surface acknowledges generically; this v1 product limitation makes the specific surface explicit.

**Tor network capacity dependency.** Tor's threat model assumes adequate guard-node and relay capacity for the user population. If pilot scale grows to thousands of users in regions where Tor is actively blocked, the project depends on Tor Project's own scaling and pluggable-transport evolution. This is named in 3.4 trust roots but is also a v1 product limit in the sense that user-experience continuity depends on infrastructure the project does not operate.

### 9.3 Security limitations

What the architecture does not protect against, named explicitly. The limits cluster into three categories: trust-root compromises, residual attack surfaces named in Section 3.3 as not closed by the architecture, and threat-model out-of-scope items.

**Trust-root compromises.** The architecture depends on the trust roots named in Section 3.4. Each is a place where compromise breaks security guarantees the architecture otherwise provides:

- **GrapheneOS itself.** A backdoored or compromised GrapheneOS image undermines hardware-backed key storage, verified boot, and the platform isolation Cairn's security properties depend on. Cairn cannot detect such a compromise; the user trusts the GrapheneOS supply chain and the platform's own integrity properties.
- **Pixel hardware.** A device with a compromised secure element — through forensic-extraction tooling that achieves bypass, through a supply-chain attack against a specific device, through a vendor-side firmware update issued under coercion — collapses the operational-identity tier's protection (Section 5.1). The hardware element holds the operational key, and if its policy enforcement can be bypassed the key is extractable.
- **Long-lived APK signing key.** A compromise of this key, held in a hardware security token by the developer, lets the adversary sign releases the client cannot distinguish from legitimate. APK Signature Scheme v3 key rotation is the recovery path but is multi-release and operationally heavy (Section 5.5).
- **Sigstore OIDC provider.** The OIDC provider can issue tokens for the developer's identity under legal process in its jurisdiction or under coercion of its personnel (Q11). v1 uses a U.S.-based provider in pilot; the trust placement effectively includes U.S. legal process.
- **Reviewer pool (v1.5+ trust root per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md)).** From v1.5 onward, compromise of three of the five reviewers (the attestation threshold) defeats the multi-party signing verification (Section 5.5). The pool is recruited for institutional and geographic diversity (Q10), which raises the cost of three-of-five compromise but does not preclude it. At v1, the recruited pool is not yet a trust root; v1 release security relies on developer source review plus the witness-cosigned release log plus F-Droid, with the v1 supply-chain gap acknowledged in §5.5.
- **Sigsum witness pool.** Compromise of the witness pool, or systemic cosignature collusion, defeats the log-state integrity check that supports both the trust-graph audit (Section 5.2) and the release-binary audit (Section 5.5). The shared-witness-pool concentration is acknowledged in Section 3.4.
- **Build supply chain.** A compromised Rust toolchain, Kotlin compiler, NDK, or third-party dependency can inject malicious code that reproducible builds (in v1.5) would catch only if independent builders rebuild from clean toolchains. v1's source-review attestation does not catch this class of attack.
- **The user.** Voluntary credential disclosure, active sabotage by the user against their own network, sharing of recovery peer phrases — none of these are addressable by architecture.
- **Cryptographic primitives (classical cryptanalysis).** Ed25519, Curve25519, ChaCha20-Poly1305 — a fundamental classical-cryptanalytic break in any of these primitives would invalidate the security properties the architecture depends on. The primitives are widely deployed and extensively analyzed; their failure would represent a fundamental break in modern cryptography rather than a product-specific vulnerability. The post-quantum case is handled separately (see threat-model out-of-scope below); the classical case is residual risk worth naming.
- **Tor network.** Tor's threat model has well-understood limits against global passive adversaries; a fundamental break in Tor's anonymity properties would invalidate the transport-layer protections Sections 5.4 and 3.4 rely on. The architecture treats Tor as a trust root with bounded protection rather than absolute anonymity.
- **SimpleX (and Briar in v1.5) protocols.** Implementation flaws in the underlying messaging protocols are possible; the architecture relies on the protocols' cryptographic correctness as published by the SimpleX and Briar projects. A protocol-level vulnerability (similar to the historical issues in OTR or early Signal Protocol versions) would propagate to Cairn users.
- **Sigsum protocol.** The Sigsum transparency-log protocol itself is a relatively new construction; protocol-level vulnerabilities in the log-state cosignature scheme or the witness-cosignature aggregation would defeat the audit properties that Sections 5.2 and 5.5 depend on.
- **In-person facilitator (v1 pilot model).** The facilitator concentrates trust during provisioning: provides the device, conducts the QR ceremony, walks through recovery-peer selection, mediates first attestations. A compromised or coerced facilitator gains visibility into multiple users' identity material. Section 3.4 names this; 9.3 acknowledges it here as a compromise scenario whose mitigation is the pilot's small-scope developer-only facilitator model, with broader-deployment ceremony design yet to be specified.
- **Reviewer toolkit.** The Docker/Nix-pinned reviewer environment the project provides (Section 8.2) sits in the trust path between reviewer attestations and the build the user installs. A compromised toolkit could yield false verification capacity. The toolkit's open-source nature and reproducibility-of-itself are partial defenses; toolkit compromise that survives those is residual.
- **Out-of-band incident response channel and verification key.** The compromise-revocation path (Section 5.5) depends on a pre-staged out-of-band communication channel and a separately-held verification key. If the channel or key is compromised at the moment of incident response — partner organization channels coerced, verification key lost or seized — the revocation cannot reach users. The mitigation is redundancy across multiple partner channels (specifics deferred to Q10 partnership outreach).
- **Cross-functional partner concentration.** The same candidate partner organizations appear across multiple Cairn role categories — reviewer pool, Sigsum witnesses, pilot facilitation, threat intelligence, localization, training. Compromise of a single partner organization has multi-surface impact across these functions. 3.4's witness-pool-independence note acknowledges one cross-function pair; the broader concentration is acknowledged here as a residual risk that v2+ partnership development addresses through structural role separation across non-overlapping organizational subsets.

**Residual attack surfaces.** Section 3.3 names surfaces the architecture acknowledges but does not close:

- **HUMINT against the user's network.** The dominant compromise vector in real cases. The trust graph's design contributes mitigation (scoped attestations, soft-cascade quarantine, stricter re-attestation behavior per [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md)) but does not eliminate the surface.
- **Tool-use surface.** Running GrapheneOS, using Tor, installing Cairn — each is itself observable in some jurisdictions and constitutes pretext for further scrutiny. The architecture cannot mitigate this; the user accepts being identifiable by the time they are using the product.
- **Evil-maid attacks.** A device tampered while unattended; the tier model does not detect or defend (Section 5.1 acknowledges this explicitly).
- **Recovery network surface.** An adversary who identifies and compromises three of five recovery peers, plus obtains the peer challenges, can reach the master. The peer-verification mechanism ([D0005](decisions/D0005-peer-verification.md)) is the principal defense; the surface is acknowledged as the architectural cost of refusing centralized trustees.
- **Forced peer designation at provisioning.** A user coerced at the ceremony to designate adversary-chosen peers. The pilot's in-person facilitator model is the primary mitigation; broader deployments will need ceremony-design choices not yet specified.
- **Trust-graph propagation metadata.** Even with commitment-only logging, propagation events have distinctive temporal fingerprints. The architecture mitigates through size-bin padding (Section 5.2) but does not eliminate the channel.
- **Public transparency-log metadata.** Even commitments expose timing and structure of operations; while the operations themselves are not in the log, the existence and pattern of operations is.
- **Distribution and supply-chain surface (pilot).** The developer-as-facilitator role concentrates trust in one person during pilot device preparation. v2+ deployment models must address this through mechanisms not yet specified.
- **Reconstruction window during recovery and provisioning.** The master seed exists in active memory during these moments; an implant resident on the device can capture it. The Rust core's memory hygiene ([D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md)) bounds this surface as far as software can, but the window cannot be eliminated.
- **Coercion during the rotation flow.** A sophisticated adversary can compel the user to initiate rotation, exposing the master during reconstruction. The peer-verification mechanism (D0005) is the principal defense.
- **Network surface (general).** Deep packet inspection, fake base stations (IMSI catchers), BGP manipulation, TLS interception via mandated local certificate authorities, traffic correlation across services. Tor as the transport layer (Section 5.4) mitigates much of this but does not eliminate it for adversaries with global passive vantage points or capable DPI infrastructure.
- **Endpoint surface (zero-click spyware on running device).** Sophisticated zero-click exploit chains delivered through messaging, browser, or other application surfaces. The architecture's tier separation bounds _what an attacker gains_ from device compromise (per Section 5.1) but does not prevent the compromise itself; the operational identity, on-device message history, and contact list are exposed under successful zero-click compromise. Evil-maid is the unattended-device subset; this surface is the attended-but-network-exposed subset.
- **Returned-after-seizure surface (distinct from reconstruction window).** A device returned after seizure may carry implants installed during the seizure. The operational guidance is that seized devices should be considered burned; the architecture cannot reliably detect implants in firmware or user-space from within the running system. The reconstruction-window surface is a different concern (the master exists in memory during recovery on a _fresh_ device); this surface is the in-routine-use-of-a-tampered-device case.
- **Update channel surface.** A malicious update to a single user (targeted) or to all users (broad).
