Section 5.5 mitigates through Sigstore + Sigsum + reviewer attestations, but the architecture does not eliminate: targeted attacks via distribution channels the project has not detected as compromised; broad attacks against the long-lived APK signing key (named above); users who do not verify reviewer attestations at install time.
- **Identity surface (compelled disclosure, SIM swap, phishing, key theft).** Beyond the tier-separation defenses (Section 5.1), the user's cryptographic identity can be attacked through credential disclosure under coercion, social engineering and phishing of recovery channels, SIM-swap or similar telco-side techniques against the user's secondary channels, and key theft when devices are compromised. The architecture limits what each yields but does not prevent the attempts.
- **Provisioning ceremony surface.** Adversarial observation of the ceremony in surveilled spaces, ceremony substitution across adversary-mediated channels, coerced participation by either party. The in-person facilitator model is the principal mitigation; broader deployments without trusted facilitators will face this surface more directly.
- **General metadata surface.** Beyond the trust-graph-specific and transparency-log-specific channels named above, traffic correlation across services, location pattern analysis across devices and apps, and timing correlation between messages and observable events all remain residual. Cairn's identifier-less messaging substrate (SimpleX) mitigates significant portions of this surface but cannot close it.
- **Group membership and association surface.** When users participate in groups, group membership itself is a target distinct from individual metadata: compromise of one group member exposes the membership list, group communication timing, and in some configurations message content. Section 5.4's group model attempts to minimize group-level metadata where the protocols permit (per-message recipient resolution rather than persistent rosters), but groups remain inherently associational; the surface cannot be made fully private without losing group utility.

**Threat-model out-of-scope items.** Section 3.5 names these explicitly; they are listed here so 9.3 is complete as a single reference:

- **TEMPEST-class electromagnetic emanation attacks.** Out of scope; require physical mitigations beyond software's reach.
- **Targeted nation-state SIGINT against the development pipeline.** A residual risk worth monitoring but not within the architecture's defensive scope (Section 3.2 acknowledges).
- **Pattern-of-life metadata leakage outside the product.** Other applications on the device, device location services, app-store side channels — the architecture cannot control what the user does outside the Cairn application.
- **Coordinated compromise across enough independent parties** to defeat the multi-party-attestation defense (developer + 3 reviewers + witness quorum simultaneously).
- **Post-quantum cryptanalysis.** Ed25519 and Curve25519 are vulnerable to Shor's algorithm on a sufficiently capable quantum computer. v1 does not include PQ primitives; the protocol-versioning fields enable later migration. The "harvest now, decrypt later" concern is acknowledged in Section 3.4.
- **Duress-profile concealment.** Indefinitely out of scope per [D0002](decisions/D0002-duress-profile.md). The architectural answer to compelled unlock is tier separation plus the recovery flow, not concealment.
- **Side-channel attacks against TEE/secure element.** Cache-timing attacks, power analysis, electromagnetic emissions of the secure element. Academic research has demonstrated side-channel attacks against TEEs broadly; whether they apply to Titan M2 specifically in the configurations Cairn uses is research-dependent. The architecture treats these as in the same out-of-scope category as TEMPEST.
- **Supply-chain attacks against Rust crate tree.** Beyond the xz-utils-class compromise of build-supply-chain dependencies (which the build-supply-chain trust root acknowledges), the project's transitive Rust dependency graph is a substantial surface. Mitigations: `cargo-vet`, `cargo-crev`, and similar dependency-audit tooling; periodic dependency review during the documentation cadence; the project commits to publishing the dependency tree as part of release transparency so external review of the tree is possible.
- **Sigstore-specific operational risks.** Sigstore operational issues that may surface as security problems: Fulcio's OIDC dependence (named above), Rekor's single-operator log model, certificate-chain validation edge cases. These are not architecturally addressed by Cairn but are tracked as part of the trust-roots health monitoring below.
- **The project's own legal jurisdiction.** The project (the developer in v1, the foundation post-incorporation) is in some legal jurisdiction whose law applies. v1 jurisdiction is the developer's residence; v1.5+ jurisdiction may shift with foundation incorporation (Section 8.4 / [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md)). The jurisdiction-specific exposure — export controls (EAR/Dual Use Regulation), donor-disclosure obligations, lawful intercept jurisdiction — is acknowledged as residual; specific jurisdictional analysis is part of pre-incorporation legal consultation.

**v1 pilot-specific exposure: pre-audit implementation.** Pilot users (10-15 users at the threat tier Section 3 describes) receive a Cairn implementation whose cryptographic core has been externally reviewed only at the limited pre-pilot audit scope (per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md)). The integration boundary — trust-graph state handling, recovery-flow orchestration, release-security stack as integrated — carries audit-not-yet-complete risk until the pre-beta full audit, post-pilot. This is a residual surface specific to the pilot user population; broader-release users do not face it. Pilot user documentation explicitly names this so pilot users can make an informed decision about participation.

**Implementation-process limit.** v1 ships substantial original cryptographic engineering (capability tokens, trust-graph operation envelope, share format per [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md), recovery-flow memory hygiene per [D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md)) performed without rolling external cryptographic consultation under the self-funded-MVP posture (Section 8.1). The self-audit (Section 8.5) plus the two-stage external audit (Section 8.5 per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md)) are the project's review gates; between them, the implementation phase is a known elevated risk window. Pilot users are informed of this posture at provisioning.

### 9.4 Mitigations and monitoring

For each significant risk above, the project commits to specific mitigations and monitoring practices. These are not claims to have eliminated the risk; they are commitments about how the project will reduce likelihood or impact, and how it will know if a risk has materialized.

**Mitigations for project risks.**

- _Solo-developer SPOF._ The project commits to documenting all architectural decisions (the decisions/ directory pattern this brief establishes), maintaining the design brief as a living document, and making all source code and infrastructure configuration publicly inspectable from day one. If the developer becomes unavailable, the project state is recoverable by any successor. The recruitment plan in Section 8.1 addresses scaling beyond solo; v1 does not.
- _Funding non-materialization._ Parallel grant applications across OTF and 2-3 backup foundations are the primary mitigation. Self-funding through brief completion bridges the gap if grants slip. Project sunset is the worst case: if funding does not materialize within 18 months of brief completion, the project's design documentation remains a public artifact others can adopt.
- _Pilot non-validation._ Continuous adversarial review at each section drafting (the pattern established in this brief); willingness to apply scope cuts (D0004 is the precedent); pilot feedback collection through an in-app channel. The project will seek partner-organization debriefs at intervals appropriate to partner capacity and pilot evolution; the project's intent is approximately 3-month and 6-month cadence subject to partner availability, and cadence is something to negotiate with specific partner organizations during Q5 outreach rather than published as a unilateral commitment. If specific pilot outcomes trigger redesign, the project commits to publishing the findings even when unfavorable.
- _Architectural mistakes constraining future versions._ Forward-compatibility design (Section 6.4); willingness to break v1 backward compatibility for v1.5 if the alternative is forward-broken architecture; external technical review at v1 feature-complete.
- _Reviewer/witness pool erosion._ Pool sizing 5+ rather than the minimum 3 (Section 5.5); rotation cadence and onboarding documentation; partner-organization relationships (Q5, Q10) that, where partner capacity allows, may supply replacement candidates; honoraria as grant-cycle-bound operational cost when funding closes. The mitigation depends on partner-organization candidate availability the project does not control; the supply is not guaranteed.

**Mitigations for product limitations.** The roadmap is the primary mitigation — v1.5 closes some gaps, v2 closes others, v3 closes the internet-shutdown gap. Specifically:

- _Internet shutdowns_ → v3 mesh integration.
- _Borrowed-laptop scenarios_ → v2 USB form factor.
- _GrapheneOS-Pixel-only_ → v2 iOS support; v2+ broader Android support remains rejected because the security baseline depends on the platform.
- _Social-recovery network properties_ → fresh-identity path as fallback; v1.5 may include facilitator-mediated peer-network bootstrapping for users without sufficient existing networks.
- _Online recovery dependency_ → v1.5 local caching of fetched attestations.
- _English-only_ → v1.5 begins localization; i18n slot present in v1.
- _Push-off default_ → pilot feedback drives revisitation in v1.5 (Q12).

**Mitigations for security limitations.** The architecture's defenses are described in Section 5; the mitigations here are operational practices rather than additional architecture.

- _Trust-root health monitoring._ The project commits to publishing a "trust-roots health" report — biennial at the v1 self-funded-MVP baseline (acknowledging the solo-developer SPOF and the multi-day-per-cycle production cost), transitioning to annual once funding closes and the project sustains the documentation-and-community-management role per 8.1. The report covers the trust roots in Section 3.4: GrapheneOS security posture, Pixel forensic-extraction news, cryptographic-primitive cryptanalytic developments, Tor network operational state, SimpleX and Briar protocol-level changes, Sigstore/Sigsum incidents, build supply-chain advisories affecting project dependencies, OIDC provider posture, reviewer-pool composition, in-person facilitator concentration (during pilot scope), witness-pool independence assessment, and (post-incorporation) foundation jurisdiction adverse-action observations. The report is a public artifact partners and users can consult. Whenever incident evidence suggests one of the roots has degraded the project publishes an interim advisory regardless of biennial-or-annual cycle.
- _Vulnerability disclosure._ A documented security disclosure policy with PGP-encrypted contact, a 90-day default disclosure timeline, and acknowledgment commitments for good-faith researchers. Per [D0012](decisions/D0012-researcher-safe-harbor.md), the project's stated intent is not to pursue legal action against disclosed-in-good-faith research; until foundation incorporation this is a published preference rather than a legal protection, and formalization through a Safe Harbor template (disclose.io or equivalent) lands at foundation incorporation. Researchers evaluating disclosure to v1 pilot-phase Cairn can make an informed decision based on the stated preference plus the explicit acknowledgment of the protection's current legal limit.
- _Incident response._ Pre-staged response plans for each named compromise scenario. Section 5.5 documents the Sigstore-identity and APK-key compromise plans; v1.5 documents equivalents for reviewer-pool and witness-pool compromise. The plans live in `docs/runbooks/` once that directory is established.
- _Pattern-of-life acknowledgment._ User and facilitator documentation explicitly names that the product cannot defend against metadata leakage from other applications on the device; operational guidance includes recommendations for device-level isolation and external-app discipline.
- _Post-quantum monitoring._ The project tracks PQ-capable hardware development and NIST PQ standardization. When credible "harvest now" capability emerges or when PQ primitives stabilize sufficiently for the threat tier, the protocol-versioning fields support a migration that does not break v1 deployments.

**Sunset and migration plan (developer-initiated).** If the project becomes unsustainable through a path the developer can manage — funding ends gracefully, the developer makes a planned decision to wind down, partner organizations withdraw with notice, or the architecture proves unworkable in pilot with time for orderly shutdown — the project commits to a specific sunset process:

- A public announcement at least six months before any infrastructure is taken down.
- Documentation handed to a candidate successor organization. Candidate successors that _do_ adopt orphaned security-tooling projects: Software Freedom Conservancy, NLnet Foundation, Calyx Institute, or a fiscal sponsor maintaining the codebase (per the candidates discussed in 8.4). Open Tech Fund is a _funder_ rather than a successor and is removed from the earlier candidate list; Tor Project Foundation does not adopt orphaned messaging projects as policy and is similarly removed. Successor adoption is conditional on the candidate organization's agreement, which the project does not currently have; specific successor arrangements emerge during partnership development (Q5).
- Guidance to users on migration to the most-comparable alternative tools available at the time of sunset (Briar, Signal, Session as candidate alternatives; the actual recommendation depends on the user's specific use case at sunset).
- _Pilot-user migration support._ Users with established Cairn identities, recovery peer networks, and trust graphs need a transition path. The sunset advisory includes: signing-identity-rotation guidance for users who want to maintain continuity with successor builds; explicit recommendations on which alternative tool matches which pilot use case; pre-sunset notice that lets users export their contact lists and trust-graph state in a structured format.
- Preservation of the source code, decision documentation, and pilot findings as public archives (Software Heritage for source; Internet Archive or equivalent for documentation; the Sigsum and Rekor log entries persist as long as their operators do, which the project does not control post-sunset). The transparency-log dependency tail is acknowledged: future verification of historical releases depends on operators the project does not control.
- A final security advisory if any unresolved issues are known at sunset, so successor projects can address them.

**Sudden-unavailability contingency (developer-driven sunset cannot execute) — per [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md).** The developer-initiated sunset plan above presumes the developer is available, lucid, and acting in coordination with successor organizations. The conditions that trigger sunset (developer no longer available per 9.1 SPOF) often also trigger sunset failure (no one to execute the six-month announcement, the migration support, the final advisory). The threat tier this product targets specifically includes adversarial conditions that could render the developer unavailable: coercion, illness, detention, asset seizure that includes signing infrastructure. The project addresses this through layered contingency:

- _Dead-man's-switch monthly check-in._ The developer performs a monthly check-in to a pre-arranged signal — a signed status message published to the project's transparency log on a fixed monthly schedule. A missed check-in is the trigger for the advisory authority below.
- _Pre-arranged partner advisory authority._ A named partner organization (Q14 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md); specific organization deferred to Q5 outreach but constrained by criteria: organizational stability, institutional independence from the developer, operational capacity for short-notice public advisory, jurisdictional placement that does not concentrate advisory authority in the developer's own legal-process exposure) has pre-arranged authority to publish a project status advisory if the developer's monthly check-in misses by 60 days (two consecutive missed cycles). The advisory states the developer's last verified status, names known-good alternatives for users to migrate to, and either declares the project temporarily suspended (if recovery is anticipated) or initiates the sunset process (if not). The advisory authority is unconditional within the partner's operational tempo — the partner does not require further developer authorization once the 60-day trigger fires.
- _Pre-staged successor documentation._ Successor-handover documentation lives in the project's public repository from v1 alpha and is updated on the same monthly cadence as the dead-man's-switch check-in. Contents include current architectural state, decision documents, encrypted pilot-user contact roster (decryption-key custody arranged with the partner advisory authority), reviewer-pool and witness-pool rosters, signing-identity-recovery procedure, and current operational state of Sigstore/Sigsum interactions.
- _Sunset advisory under sudden unavailability._ When the 60-day trigger fires, the partner advisory authority follows a pre-staged advisory script (drafted once at v1 alpha, partner-rehearsed, annual review). If sunset is initiated through this path, the six-month sunset window begins from the partner's advisory publication rather than from a developer-issued statement that cannot be produced.

The sudden-unavailability contingency operates at additional cost: approximately 2-3 hours per month of developer time (signed-check-in message authoring plus successor-documentation maintenance) plus partner-organization commitment to hold the advisory authority role. Specific partner selection is tracked as Q14; the architectural commitment to the mechanism is unconditional, but the partner identity emerges from Q5 outreach.

The sunset is the worst case; the commitment is that the worst case is not silent, regardless of the path that leads to it. Section 8.4 covers the foundation-incorporation path that is the project's mitigation against needing sunset at all; the board takes over the partner advisory authority role post-incorporation, formalizing what v1 establishes as a partner arrangement.

---

## 10. Funding and Resourcing

_Purpose: identify what the project can deliver at the volunteer baseline, what specifically requires external funding, and what sequence of funding events unlocks broader release._

The section is organized as a phase-gated cost model rather than a flat v1 budget. Phases distinguish the project's unilateral commitments (what gets built without external dollars) from its conditional commitments (what depends on grant cycles, subsidy programs, or partner co-funding). The structure inverts the conventional design-brief budget — instead of presenting a total figure that requires complete funding to deliver, it presents specific funding events with named items each event unlocks. Readers asking "what does my dollar unlock?" can match against the phase descriptions; readers asking "what is the project committed to regardless?" find that in Phase A. The funding-stance background — self-funded volunteer baseline as the operating reality, partnership and grant funding as the trajectory rather than the precondition — is established in Sections 8 and 9.1 and is referenced here rather than re-derived.

### 10.1 Phase A — Volunteer baseline (no external funding)

Phase A is what the project delivers at the volunteer baseline with no external grant or subsidy required. The work proceeds as the developer's time allows; the brief does not commit to a calendar schedule for Phase A completion because the operating reality is an "as available" cadence consistent with [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md). Funders and partners reading the brief should treat Phase A items as the project's unilateral commitment; Phase A completes when it completes, and the brief does not characterize that timeline in months or person-years because doing so would imply a guarantee the volunteer baseline cannot back.

**Deliverables in Phase A.**

- Architecture documentation, threat model, decisions records, open-questions tracker (largely complete at brief publication).
- Rust core implementation per [D0003](decisions/D0003-implementation-language.md): cryptographic primitives wrapper (`zeroize`/`secrecy`/`subtle`), capability-token construction and verification per [D0006](decisions/D0006-cryptographic-envelope.md), trust-graph operation envelope and CRDT, Shamir Secret Sharing reconstruction with memory hygiene, recovery flow primitives.
- Kotlin Android UI via UniFFI: Signal-familiar surfaces (Section 5.6), recovery flow walkthroughs, trust-badge rendering, calibrated security-communication patterns.
- Internal testing on developer-owned hardware: property-based tests for the trust-graph CRDT, fuzz tests for COSE/CBOR parsing and Shamir reconstruction, known-answer tests for primitives, differential tests against the SimpleX reference where Cairn reuses semantics.
- CI/CD setup using GitHub Actions on the free tier.
- Reviewer-pool recruitment outreach (Section 8.2 volunteer-attestation baseline): conversations, candidate evaluation, onboarding through the documented reviewer toolkit.
- Partner-organization outreach (Section 8.6): conversations leading toward pilot facilitation, threat intelligence, and localization arrangements. No funding required for conversations themselves.
- Self-audit and tooling per Section 8.5: property-based testing, fuzz testing, known-answer tests, differential tests, static analysis. The pre-pilot external audit (D0011) is gated to Phase B; the self-audit work is Phase A.

**Costs absorbed personally in Phase A.**

- Developer's time, at "as available" cadence consistent with [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md).
- Existing developer hardware for implementation work.
- Small infrastructure: domain registration (~$15-50/year), GitHub free tier for source hosting and CI, free-tier static documentation hosting.
- Two to four GrapheneOS-capable Pixel devices retained by the project for developer testing and a small loaner pool for partner-facilitated pilot training (~$1,500-3,000 one-time). The bulk of pilot deployment is BYOD: pilot users source their own GrapheneOS-capable Pixel devices through the standard channels documented in Section 5 and the user-onboarding documentation.
- Total absorbed in Phase A: approximately $1,500-3,500 across the duration, primarily hardware. The developer's time is the dominant resource, and the brief does not assign a dollar figure to it because the project's posture is that the developer's contribution is volunteer until grant funding makes a maintainer-compensation question concrete (Phase D aspiration; see 10.4 and Section 8.1).

**What Phase A cannot deliver.**

- The pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md). Phase A's self-audit reduces but does not eliminate the residual cryptographic-correctness risk that pilot deployment carries; the audit is the first material funding event.
- Pre-incorporation legal consultation per [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md) at the depth required for foundation jurisdiction selection.
- Reviewer honoraria. Section 8.2's volunteer-attestation model is the Phase A operating reality; honoraria require grant funding (Phase B/C).
- Specialized consulting (cryptographic, UX, documentation) beyond the developer's own capacity. The team-scaling intent in Section 8.1 is funding-gated.
- Pilot deployment at scale beyond the developer's own facilitation capacity. Broader pilot facilitation depends on partner-organization arrangements; the partner arrangements depend on partner-side funding evaluations the project does not control.

**Phase A risk.** Developer burnout extends Phase A indefinitely or terminates it prematurely. The mitigations are documentation discipline (so the work is recoverable to a successor), the [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) sudden-unavailability handling, and surfacing the funding state transparently to the reviewer pool and partner conversations rather than implying a non-volunteer baseline exists.

### 10.2 Phase B — First-dollar threshold (pilot readiness)

Phase B is the smallest funding event that unlocks pilot readiness. The two structural items are pre-incorporation legal consultation (which precedes any grant-routing arrangement) and the pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit (which precedes any pilot user receiving a Cairn build). Phase B is where the brief's commitments transition from unilateral to conditional.

**Phase B line items.**

- _Pre-incorporation legal consultation_ per [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md): $1,500-9,000 for an initial engagement with non-profit counsel covering fiscal-sponsor selection support, jurisdictional shortlist evaluation, and IP-ownership/assignment considerations for the natural-person-to-foundation transition. Lower bound reflects approximately 5 hours of senior non-profit counsel time at $300/hour; upper bound reflects 15 hours at $600/hour. **Phase placement.** The brief frames this as a Phase A/B straddle item with an explicit resolution rule: the developer absorbs the consultation cost personally as a Phase A item if able to sustain it (the consultation is then a non-funding-gated input to Phase B sequencing); the consultation is grant-funded as a Phase B item if a smaller flexible grant closes first. Either way, the consultation must complete before Phase C foundation-incorporation work begins (Section 10.3 dependency). The Phase A absorbed cost in Section 10.1 does not include the consultation; if absorbed, total Phase A absorbed cost rises by up to $9,000.
- _Fiscal-sponsor setup_ per [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md) and Q15 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md): typically free-to-low at lower-overhead sponsors (Open Collective Foundation, Code for Science & Society); fiscal-sponsorship fees (5–15% typical of routed grants, often 10–15% at established sponsors per current SFC and OSC published rates) are paid as a percentage of subsequent grants rather than as an up-front cost. **Onboarding timing.** Fiscal-sponsor application-to-onboarding cycles run 2–6 months at SFC, OCF, and CS&S, often with a queue. The project's sequencing in Section 10.6 makes fiscal-sponsor onboarding a Phase A pursuit that precedes Phase B grant intake — the project initiates sponsor onboarding before first grant award so that grant funds can be accepted on the award timeline rather than after a 2–6 month delay. NLnet's grantee-sponsorship model is the exception: NLnet sponsors its own grantees, avoiding this gap, which makes NLnet routes the lowest-friction first option.
- _Pre-pilot cryptographic-primitives audit_ per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md): scope per Section 8.5 is 1–2 person-weeks of senior auditor time over COSE_Sign1 envelope, Shamir SSS reconstruction, trust-graph envelope, and recovery-flow crypto. Realistic engagement budgets by named route:
  - OTF Secure Audit grant: typical award range $30,000-60,000 covers the engagement with margin; ~6 month cycle; entity-eligibility prerequisite (fiscal-sponsored or incorporated).
  - NLnet NGI Zero Trust: €50,000 small-grant tier (when audit is part of an accepted development grant); NLnet sponsors its own grantees; ~3 month cycle.
  - Cure53 mission-rate engagement: ~$20,000-30,000 at the typical 30–50% civil-society discount; vendor discretion, no application cycle; the project budgets the engagement on its own side.
  - Trail of Bits civic-tech rates: discretionary engagement at ~$25,000-40,000 if granted; narrower scope match than Cure53.
- _Initial infrastructure operational costs_ for the pilot phase: domain registrar (already in Phase A), email/PGP infrastructure for disclosure handling (free), continued static hosting (free); incremental cost approximately $0-500/year.

**Phase B totals — addressable by instrument, not arithmetic.**

Section 10.2 floors and ceilings are most usefully expressed as the addressable grant ranges by named instrument rather than as independent-item arithmetic, because no named instrument writes a $17,000 grant exactly and the project's pursuit posture works against specific instruments. The minimum-grant outcomes that materially advance Phase B by named route:

- OTF Secure Audit at $30,000-60,000 covers the audit with margin; legal consultation absorbed by developer or covered by a smaller flexible grant. Cycle: ~6 months. Entity-eligibility prerequisite gates OTF Secure Audit on fiscal-sponsor arrangement being in place.
- NLnet NGI Zero Trust at €50,000 covers audit-plus-pilot-period-flexible-allocations; legal consultation covered within the development-grant scope. Cycle: ~3 months. NLnet's grantee-sponsorship model avoids the fiscal-sponsor onboarding gap.
- Cure53 mission-rate engagement at $20,000-30,000 covers the audit only; legal consultation absorbed or separately funded. No application cycle, vendor discretion.

The arithmetic-floor figure of approximately $17,000 (legal consultation lower bound + subsidized audit lower bound) is what a sufficient-funding-event size looks like if the components fund independently; the realistic minimum to pursue against a single instrument is $20,000-50,000 depending on instrument. The project's pursuit posture (Section 10.6) reflects this: applications go to instruments at their typical award range, not to instruments at a $17,000 ask.

**Adjacent Phase A-leveraged aspirational item: rolling cryptographic consulting.** Section 8.1 identifies part-time rolling cryptographic consulting at $5,000-10,000/month for 3–6 months of design-and-implementation phase ($15,000-60,000 total) as an aspirational addition. **Phase placement honesty.** The "design-and-implementation phase" the consulting is designed to leverage is Phase A by Section 10.1's definition; rolling consulting reviews design decisions as they are made, which is the leverage point Section 8.5 identifies. If Phase B funding closes after substantial Phase A implementation is complete, the rolling-consulting window has already largely closed and the funding event misses its leverage point. The brief frames this as a Phase A consulting aspiration fundable via an early-closing Phase B-style grant, with the explicit acknowledgment that the value of this funding diminishes as Phase A implementation proceeds. If timing allows, the project pursues both audit and rolling consulting in a combined grant ask; if Phase B funding lands only late in Phase A, the rolling consulting is reframed as "review of completed-but-not-yet-audited cryptographic implementation" — which is a different deliverable than rolling design review. Implied engagement intensity for consulting candidates: 4–8 hours per week at $250-400/hour. Section 9.1 acknowledges the residual engineering risk during the self-audit period.

**What Phase B unlocks.** Pilot deployment with the cryptographic core externally audited. Pilot users receive a build whose primitives have third-party assurance even though the broader integration is reviewed at the integration-boundary granularity per the consent framing in D0011. Fiscal-sponsor arrangement is in place; the project moves from "natural person operating informally" to "natural person operating with fiscal-sponsor grant routing." Foundation incorporation work _may_ begin if Phase B legal consultation surfaces criteria activating the [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) trigger evaluation early, but the default operational state is fiscal-sponsor arrangement as the permanent grant-receipt structure for the duration of the deferral.

**Phase B contingency.** If Phase B funding does not close, Phase A continues. The project does not deploy a pilot without the pre-pilot audit, per D0011's no-skip-the-audit posture. The honest consequence is pilot deferral, not pilot-without-audit.

### 10.3 Phase C — Pre-broader-release funding

Phase C funds the items required between pilot completion and broader-than-pilot release. The largest item is the pre-beta full audit; foundation incorporation (formerly a Phase C item) is now conditional on the [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) trigger evaluation at v1.5 broader-release planning; reviewer honoraria and an optional UX engineer are aspirational Phase C items rather than strict gates per [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md) and D0016.

**Phase C line items.**

- _Pre-beta full audit_ per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md): $60,000-220,000 reflecting market rates at named firms (Trail of Bits, NCC Group, Cure53, Quarkslab). The $60,000-150,000 band fits Cure53 or Quarkslab at the stated 4-6 week engagement; engaging Trail of Bits or NCC Group at the same scope at standard rates extends the upper bound to $180,000-220,000. Lower bound conditional on subsidy programs (OTF Secure Audit at the audit-grant tier, Cure53 mission-org rates extended from the pre-pilot engagement, Trail of Bits civic-tech rates where granted). Firm selection within the band is a Q7 question.
- _Reviewer honoraria (Phase C aspiration per D0015, not a v1.5 ship-gate)._ Per Section 8.2 and D0015, the recruited reviewer pool activates at v1.5 alongside reproducible builds; honoraria are the operational model when partnership or grant funding closes (Q3) but their absence does not block v1.5 ship — v1.5 ships with whatever volunteer-attestation pool has formed through Q5 outreach. If Phase C funding includes honoraria, the operational model lands at approximately $40,000-100,000 per year scaled by the release cadence (quarterly target per [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md)). Honoraria sized to be "fair relative to security-engineering market rates for the equivalent work" per Section 8.2; the brief estimates $2,000-5,000 per reviewer per release at 8-20 hours of review per release, multiplied across the five-reviewer pool. Estimates are structural rather than committed; specific honoraria figures depend on grant scope and are stated in partnership terms when funding closes. The volunteer-to-honoraria-to-volunteer possible trajectory the §8.2 surfacing commitment names is preserved.
- _Foundation incorporation (conditional on [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) trigger activation)_ per [D0010](decisions/D0010-foundation-jurisdiction.md): $5,000-25,000 initial depending on jurisdiction (US 501(c)(3) at the higher end, UK CIC at the lower end, Dutch Stichting and Swiss Verein in the middle), only if the D0016 trigger evaluation at v1.5 broader-release planning activates incorporation. The line item is reserved in Phase C scope but is not a default Phase C commitment — if the trigger does not activate, the project continues operating under fiscal-sponsor arrangement and the foundation-incorporation line item does not apply. **Dependency (when applicable):** if the trigger activates, foundation incorporation requires the Phase B legal consultation (Section 10.2) to have completed — jurisdictional selection, fiscal-sponsor selection, and IP-assignment evaluation produced by the Phase B consultation are the prerequisites the incorporation filing acts on. The sequencing is structural: legal consultation → trigger evaluation → (if trigger activates) foundation filing.
- _Optional: UX-focused engineer_ per Section 8.1: $30,000-60,000 for a 3-6 month engagement focused on the Section 5.6 implementation surface — Signal-familiar UI patterns, trust-badge rendering, recovery flow walkthroughs, calibrated security-communication work. The engineering review identified this surface as 30-50% of v1 implementation effort; engaging a specialist accelerates pre-broader-release work. The framing here is honest about a tension: the work itself is in scope (security-critical UX per Section 5.6's "friction in security software translates to operational mistakes"), and the engagement is optional in the sense that the developer can absorb the work at slower cadence. If Phase C funding closes only at the lower bound and the UX engineer is not engaged, that work returns to the developer's Phase A-cadence work backlog with the quality and timing consequences acknowledged in Section 9.1 rather than rendered invisible.
- _Pilot deployment hardware_: minimal incremental cost under the BYOD pilot model. Pilot users source their own GrapheneOS-capable Pixel devices through the standard channels. The two-to-four-device project pool from Phase A continues as the developer-testing and partner-loaner inventory; no significant Phase C hardware budget is required for the pilot itself.

**Phase C totals.**

- Floor (D0016 trigger does not activate; no foundation incorporation): approximately $80,000 (subsidized full audit lower bound + no honoraria + no foundation incorporation + no UX engineer; assumes Phase B legal consultation already completed).
- Floor (D0016 trigger activates; foundation incorporation proceeds): approximately $105,000 (subsidized full audit lower bound + a partial-year of minimum honoraria + minimum foundation incorporation + no UX engineer).
- Ceiling (D0016 trigger does not activate): approximately $340,000 (Trail of Bits / NCC Group unsubsidized upper-bound audit at $220,000 + full annual honoraria upper bound + UX engineer engagement; no foundation incorporation overhead).
- Ceiling (D0016 trigger activates): approximately $405,000 (Trail of Bits / NCC Group unsubsidized upper-bound audit at $220,000 + full annual honoraria upper bound + maximum foundation incorporation + UX engineer engagement). The earlier ceiling of approximately $335,000 holds for the Cure53 / Quarkslab case at the upper end of their rate band ($150,000 audit ceiling); the project's actual ceiling depends on firm selection per Q7 and on the D0016 trigger outcome.

Phase C totals are stated gross of fiscal-sponsor fees (5–15% per Section 10.2, often 10–15% at established sponsors); the net-to-project receipt is reduced by the routing fee. Under the D0016 deferral, the fiscal-sponsor fee persists indefinitely as permanent operational overhead rather than being replaced by foundation overhead after incorporation — except if the trigger activates and the project incorporates, at which point the fee is replaced by foundation overhead per Section 10.4. NLnet's grantee-sponsorship model is the exception within the fiscal-sponsor framing — it sponsors its own grantees and avoids the routing fee.

**What Phase C unlocks.** Broader-than-pilot release. The project enters a foundation-governed operational posture with the structural mitigations the foundation enables: formalized Safe Harbor per [D0012](decisions/D0012-researcher-safe-harbor.md), board-bound governance per Section 8.4, formal partner advisory authority per [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md), and the reviewer-honoraria operating model per Section 8.2. The conditional commitments in Section 8 become structurally enforceable rather than personally undertaken.

**Phase C contingency.** If Phase C funding closes at the lower bound only, the audit scope narrows per D0011 with explicit acknowledgment, the UX engineer is not engaged, and broader release is deferred until subsequent funding closes. If Phase C funding does not close at all, the pilot continues at the Phase B-funded posture and broader release is deferred indefinitely. The no-skip-the-audit commitment from Section 8.5 means broader release is gated on the pre-beta audit closing, not on a "we tried" disclaimer.

### 10.4 Phase D — Steady-state operations

Phase D is what the project costs annually once the v1 broader release has shipped. The figures are projected ranges rather than committed budget; the brief identifies categories and approximate scale. Phase D operates under one of two structural postures per [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md): _foundation-operating_ (if the D0016 trigger activated and incorporation proceeded) or _fiscal-sponsor-operating_ (the default if the trigger did not activate). The line items differ between the two postures.

**Phase D annual line items (both postures).**

- _Recurring audit cycle_ per [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md): a second external cryptographic audit approximately 18-24 months after the first, amortized to roughly $40,000-100,000 per year depending on the rate of cryptographic-relevant changes and the prior audit's findings.
- _Reviewer honoraria_ at the operational model per Section 8.2: approximately $40,000-100,000 per year at the quarterly cadence target.
- _Infrastructure_: $1,000-3,000 per year for domain, static hosting, log-operator participation (if the project operates its own Sigsum log, the higher end; if the project participates in public logs, the lower end).
- _Localization and translation honoraria_ tracking Q6 in [open-questions.md](open-questions.md): grant-scoped; approximately $5,000-20,000 per year for a small number of target languages with native-speaker security-trainer translators.

**Phase D operational developer time (per architecture-simplification review F19; named as line items but not budgeted).** Several recurring operational commitments compound on the developer's working set across the multi-year Phase D horizon. These are not budgeted dollar amounts in this section because they are absorbed by the developer's time at the volunteer baseline; they are named here so the §10.4 Phase D sustainability arithmetic accounts for them honestly rather than hiding them as "developer absorbs at no cost":

- _Upstream-roadmap tracking._ Tor pluggable-transport changes (per §5.4); SimpleX protocol versioning; Briar maintenance trajectory (per §7.1 Briar dependency); GrapheneOS hardware availability and roadmap; F-Droid policy changes affecting v1 distribution. Estimated load: 40–100 hours/year continuous attention.
- _Witness-pool management (v1+) and reviewer-pool coordination (v1.5+)._ Witness-pool renewal of partner arrangements per Q5 outreach cycles; witness operational support during release cycles. Reviewer-pool recruitment, rotation, and attestation collection per release from v1.5 onward per §8.2. Estimated load: 80–200 hours/year at steady state once recruited pools form.
- _Test-infrastructure maintenance._ CI pipelines for the Rust core and Kotlin UI; reproducible-build infrastructure from v1.5; property-based test framework maintenance from v1.5. Estimated load: 30–60 hours/year at steady state.
- _Partner-organization coordination._ Q5 partnership renewal cycles; partner-mediated D0013 consent and exit channel operations; D0009 partner advisory authority renewal under D0016 deferral. Estimated load: 40–80 hours/year at steady state.

Compounded annual operational developer time at Phase D steady state: approximately 190–440 hours/year independent of release-engineering work, accruing on the developer at the volunteer baseline. This is the structural arithmetic behind the §10.4 sustainability horizon framing below — the operational load that "the foundation pays everyone except the engineer-operator" alludes to is concretely the 190–440 hours/year described here.

**Phase D line items conditional on D0016 trigger outcome.**

- _Foundation overhead (only under foundation-operating posture)_: $10,000-30,000 per year for board governance, accounting, fiscal infrastructure, regulatory compliance, jurisdiction-specific tax filings. Range depends on incorporation jurisdiction per D0010. Under fiscal-sponsor-operating posture, this line item does not apply — operational governance occurs through D0009 partner advisory authority renewal and through the fiscal sponsor's own administrative infrastructure.
- _Fiscal-sponsor fees (only under fiscal-sponsor-operating posture)_: 5-15% of routed grants (often 10-15% at established sponsors per Section 10.2), permanent rather than transitional under the D0016 deferral. NLnet's grantee-sponsorship model avoids this fee where mission fit allows; for grants routed through SFC / OCF / CS&S, the fee is permanent operational overhead. At sustained ~$200,000/year grant intake, fees of ~$20,000-30,000/year; at ~$300,000/year sustained, fees of ~$30,000-45,000/year.

**Phase D annual totals (without maintainer compensation).**

- Floor (fiscal-sponsor-operating posture; the more likely default): approximately $85,000 per year (audit amortization at the subsidized rate + minimum honoraria + infrastructure + minimum localization + fiscal-sponsor fees on the audit/honoraria intake). No foundation overhead.
- Floor (foundation-operating posture): approximately $90,000-100,000 per year (same line items plus foundation overhead at the lower bound; no fiscal-sponsor fees because the foundation receives grants directly).
- Ceiling (fiscal-sponsor-operating posture): approximately $220,000 per year (audit amortization at the unsubsidized rate + maximum honoraria + maximum localization + fiscal-sponsor fees at the ~$300K/year scale). No foundation overhead.
- Ceiling (foundation-operating posture): approximately $250,000 per year (same line items plus foundation overhead at the upper bound; no fiscal-sponsor fees).

The two postures' totals are within $30-40K/year of each other at the same operating scale — the trade between fiscal-sponsor fees and foundation overhead is roughly balanced once Phase D operations reach the scale at which the D0016 trigger criteria would likely have activated. At smaller operating scale, fiscal-sponsor-operating posture is meaningfully cheaper (fees scale with grant intake; foundation overhead is roughly fixed); at larger operating scale, foundation-operating posture is cheaper (fees scale faster than foundation overhead once intake exceeds ~$300K/year sustained).

**Maintainer compensation: aspirational, and the Phase D sustainability horizon.** Section 8.1 identifies that compensation for the developer (and eventual additional engineers and operational roles) is the project's intent when grant funding sustainably supports it. The brief does not budget maintainer compensation as a committed Phase D line item; the funding configuration that sustainably supports paid maintainer time has not closed and is identified here as the project's aspiration rather than its plan.

The honest sizing is that Phase D operational scope per the line items above plus the unenumerated operational load — board interface per Section 8.4, partner coordination per Section 8.6, reviewer-pool management per Section 8.2, release engineering, audit coordination per Section 8.5, [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) dead-man's-switch operation, Section 9.4 trust-roots health reporting, localization coordination, incident response — corresponds to at least 1.0 engineering FTE plus 0.3–0.5 of operations / program-management FTE for a foundation-governed security-critical product at this threat tier. A funded engineering line at security-engineering market rates is approximately $150,000-250,000 per year per FTE (US-equivalent rates; jurisdiction-dependent). Fully loaded with the fractional operations role, the Phase D minimum-viable team composition implies $225,000-500,000 per year of personnel cost the brief does not currently commit to or fund.

The Phase D sustainability horizon without maintainer compensation is finite, not indefinite. Phase D operations are sustainable at the developer's volunteer baseline only for the window the developer's own circumstances support; beyond that window, Phase D either secures multi-year operational engineering-capacity funding (per the new category in Section 10.5) or sunsets per Section 9.4. The brief does not commit to a specific runway figure for that window — Section 9.1's runway-disclosure commitment remains unresolved at this draft, acknowledged in Section 10.8 — but readers should not read Phase D's "steady state" framing as indefinite uncompensated developer time. The most likely Phase D trajectory absent multi-year operational funding is structural collapse into the [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) sudden-unavailability contingency, not graceful steady state. Phase D figures above ($90,000-250,000 per year) describe a foundation paying every contributor in its workflow except the engineer-operator who runs it; that posture is uncommon in open-source security non-profits at this operational scale and the brief acknowledges it explicitly rather than letting it propagate as silent assumption.

### 10.5 Funding sources

The project's funding source roster reflects the named subsidy programs in [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md) and the broader civil-society grant landscape relevant to the project's mission.

- **Self/personal.** Phase A and as long as feasible into Phase B if the developer can absorb the initial legal consultation. Developer time and the small absorbed costs identified in 10.1. The brief does not assume the developer's personal contribution extends indefinitely; the [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) and Section 9.1 framings acknowledge the personal-resource boundary.
- **Audit grant programs** (named in D0011, the first route for audit funding) — application cycles with eligibility prerequisites:
  - Open Tech Fund Secure Audit program — mission-aligned audit grants typically $30,000-150,000 per award, ~6 month cycle, fits Phase B pre-pilot audit (primary route) and Phase C pre-beta audit (primary route). _Eligibility prerequisite: applicant must be a fiscal-sponsored or incorporated entity per OTF's published program criteria. Cairn's pre-incorporation natural-person posture creates a chicken-and-egg with the fiscal-sponsor arrangement that itself is a Phase B item — sequencing is addressed in Section 10.6._
  - Mozilla Open Source Audit Awards — periodic, cycles irregular since 2021; may not run during Cairn's Phase B/C application window. As of brief publication, recent Mozilla open-source security funding has been MOSS-routed rather than OSAA-named.
- **Integrated-dev-grant audit allocations** — audit funded as part of a development grant the project has received, not standalone:
  - NLnet NGI Zero Trust calls — European funding instrument. Small-grant tier typically €50,000, broader development funding at NGI Zero series. NLnet's grantee-sponsorship model also avoids the fiscal-sponsor onboarding gap that affects other grant routes. _Audit allocations are only available as part of a development grant; NLnet does not typically fund standalone audits._
- **Vendor discount mechanisms** — discounts applied by audit firms at their discretion to engagements they accept, not grant programs:
  - Cure53 mission-organization rates — applied at Cure53's discretion to civil-society security tools (~30–50% discount typical). _No application cycle; the project still budgets the engagement on its own side and Cure53's discount changes the size of the engagement budget required._
  - Trail of Bits civic-tech engagement rates — discretionary; comparable structure to Cure53 mission rates but typically narrower scope.
- **Direct grant programs** (Phase C/D funding beyond audit-specific routes):
  - Open Tech Fund main grants (broader than the audit subsidy line).
  - Ford Foundation (civil-society and digital-rights focus).
  - Open Society Foundations (regional foundation network; relevant jurisdictions and program areas vary).
  - Mozilla Open Source Support (MOSS) and successor programs.
  - European democracy and digital-rights instruments (SIDA, GIZ, EIDHR, Dutch Ministry of Foreign Affairs human-rights funds).
  - NLnet main funded calls (NGI series beyond NGI Zero Trust).
  - Knight Foundation (some program alignment for journalist-focused security tooling).
  - Internews and Digital Defenders Partnership — civil-society security-tool grants relevant to Cairn's audience overlap.
- **Multi-year operational engineering-capacity grants** (the Phase D maintainer-compensation route the brief identifies as aspirational; pursuit difficulty is high, cycles long, and these grants rarely cover a full security-engineering FTE at market rates):
  - Sloan Foundation open-source program (where eligibility applies — primarily science/research-aligned open source).
  - Chan Zuckerberg Initiative Essential Open Source Software for Science (where applicable).
  - Internet Society Foundation organizational grants.
  - Sustained engineering grants from European NGI consortia at the project-not-task tier.
  - The category exists in name but is structurally narrower than the audit-funding landscape; the brief acknowledges this is the structural fragility behind Phase D's aspirational framing (Section 10.4) rather than letting it propagate as a silent assumption.
- **Partner co-funding.** Where Cairn integration with a partner organization's tooling benefits both projects, partner organizations may co-fund the integration work. Co-funding arrangements emerge from Section 8.6 partnership conversations and are not assumed in the brief's budget.
- **Individual donations.** Under the fiscal-sponsor-operating posture (the default under [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) deferral): routed through the fiscal sponsor if the sponsor's structure supports individual contributions in addition to grants (SFC, OCF, and CS&S all support individual donations on behalf of sponsored projects). Under the foundation-operating posture (if D0016 trigger activates and incorporation proceeds): a structured donation channel through the foundation.
- **Hardware partnerships** (v3+ aspiration per the roadmap): pre-keyed device distribution, mesh-radio integration kits, established-organization tier as a paid offering subsidizing grassroots use. These are out of scope for v1 funding decisions and are identified for completeness rather than as a budgeted source.

### 10.6 Funding strategy and sequencing

The project's funding pursuit follows a specific sequence consistent with the phase gating:

1. **Partner-organization outreach precedes formal grant applications.** Per Section 8.6, the partnership picture depends on conversations that have not yet been held; partner endorsement strengthens grant applications and partner co-funding arrangements emerge from these conversations. Phase A includes the outreach.
2. **Pre-incorporation legal consultation (Phase B item) gates fiscal-sponsor selection.** The project does not route a first grant intake without the fiscal-sponsor arrangement in place. The legal consultation may be self-absorbed if the developer can sustain the cost, or grant-funded if a smaller flexible grant closes first.
3. **Subsidy programs are pursued first for audit funding.** Audit subsidies have specific mission alignment (the project's threat tier and audience match the subsidy programs' criteria closely) and lower overhead than direct grants. The project applies to multiple programs in parallel; grant cycles vary from 3-9 months across the named programs.
4. **Direct grants are pursued in parallel for broader Phase C and Phase D funding.** Direct grants typically support multi-year operational funding rather than single audit engagements; the application work is more substantial and the cycles longer.
5. **Phase A does not depend on Phase B funding closing.** The developer continues Phase A indefinitely if Phase B funding does not close. The brief does not promise Phase A completion on a Phase-B-funded schedule; the volunteer-baseline cadence in [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md) governs Phase A regardless of grant outcomes.

### 10.7 Funding risks

Section 9.1 frames the project's funding risk as two-horizon: short-horizon risk (Phase B funding does not close, pilot deferred) and long-horizon risk (Phase D sustainability does not materialize, project sunsets per Section 9.4). This subsection enumerates specific failure modes by phase.

- **Subsidy programs do not close for the pre-pilot audit.** Phase A continues; pilot deployment is deferred until the audit lands. The project does not deploy a pilot without the pre-pilot audit. Mitigation: parallel applications to multiple subsidy programs; partner-organization endorsement; iterative resubmission across cycles.
- **All Phase B funding fails for an extended period.** Phase A continues for as long as the developer can sustain it; the [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) sudden-unavailability handling and the Section 9.4 sunset process catch the worst case.
- **Phase C funding closes only at the lower bound.** Per D0011: the audit scope narrows with explicit acknowledgment; the UX engineer is not engaged; broader release is deferred until subsequent funding closes. The conditional-not-unconditional posture in Section 8 means the brief does not promise broader release on a Phase-C-funded timeline.
- **Reviewer honoraria cannot be funded indefinitely (v1.5 onward).** Per Section 8.2, [D0008](decisions/D0008-volunteer-baseline-cadence.md), and [D0015](decisions/D0015-v1-release-security-posture.md), at v1 the recruited pool has not yet activated, so no v1 honoraria reversion path exists. From v1.5 onward, if Phase C honoraria funding does not sustain, the project reverts to the volunteer-attestation baseline with surfaced transparency; release cadence reverts to the median-4-6-month "as-quorum-forms" pattern. Reviewer-pool composition may shift as some volunteer-baseline reviewers depart and the recruitment pool changes; the surfacing commitment in 8.2 lets the pool evaluate this trajectory at recruitment. v1.5+ may also ship under volunteer attestation if Q3 honoraria funding never closes — the architectural commitment is to the verification property (multi-party attestation), not to a specific compensation model.
- **Currency-of-incorporation jurisdiction (D0010) affects donor pool depth.** Some jurisdictions support broader international donor tax-deductibility through tax treaties; some have narrower donor bases. The jurisdictional choice (legal-consultation-mediated) factors this into the selection criteria per D0010.
- **D0016 foundation-incorporation trigger does not activate (expected operational state under several plausible trajectories).** Per [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md), foundation incorporation is deferred with a v1.5 broader-release evaluation trigger; if the trigger does not activate at v1.5 broader-release planning (or at any subsequent re-evaluation cycle), the project continues operating under fiscal-sponsor arrangement indefinitely with [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) partner advisory authority as the permanent governance scaffold. The structural mitigations the foundation would enable (formalized Safe Harbor per [D0012](decisions/D0012-researcher-safe-harbor.md), board-bound governance per 8.4, formal partner advisory authority per D0009) remain at the stated-intent posture indefinitely rather than becoming legally enforceable. Under the D0016 framing, this is not a failure mode — it is the expected outcome for projects of Cairn's operational scale (low to moderate sustained grant intake; partner-organization arrangements that work without formal Safe Harbor; no personal-liability incidents requiring incorporation mitigation). The brief is honest about this throughout Section 8 and Section 10.
- **Maintainer compensation at Phase D scale does not materialize.** The multi-year operational engineering-capacity grant category in Section 10.5 is structurally narrower than the audit-funding landscape, and no source category routinely funds a full security-engineering FTE at market rates multi-year. Failure mode: developer absorbs Phase D operational scope as uncompensated labor beyond the self-funding horizon (Section 9.1's runway boundary), reaching the [D0009](decisions/D0009-sudden-unavailability.md) sudden-unavailability contingency or Section 9.4 sunset on a predictable trajectory. This is the long-horizon failure mode the brief surfaces here so funders evaluating Phase D sustainability see it explicitly rather than discovering it in due diligence. Operational-degradation paths along the same trajectory: foundation administration cadence slows; audit-cycle execution slips against [D0011](decisions/D0011-audit-budget-and-timing.md) timing; partner coordination becomes reactive rather than proactive; release engineering reverts to median 4-6 month volunteer cadence per D0008 even at honoraria-funded reviewer pool.

### 10.8 What this section does not promise

The brief does not promise:

- Specific dollar amounts of funding will close.
- Specific timelines tied to funding events. The phase descriptions are "what unlocks what," not "by when."
- That the project reaches Phase C or D. Reaching them depends on partner-organization outreach, grant cycles, pilot success, and external funding decisions the project does not control.
- A specific maintainer compensation arrangement. The Phase D aspiration is identified; the funding configuration that supports it has not closed. Phase D's sustainability horizon without compensation is finite, not indefinite (Section 10.4).
- A specific fiscal sponsor (per D0010, Q15), audit firm (Q7), or jurisdiction of incorporation (Q4). These are tracked as open questions to resolve through outreach and consultation.
- That the named audit grant programs (OTF Secure Audit, Mozilla OSAA, NLnet NGI Zero Trust, plus Cure53 and Trail of Bits vendor-discount mechanisms) will be open, accepting applications, or operating at the rate tiers cited in Section 10.2 and Section 10.3 when Cairn applies. Subsidy-program landscape and audit-firm rate cards both shift on multi-year horizons (OTF's funding was contested in 2024-2025; Mozilla restructured its open-source funding repeatedly; security-engineering market rates moved materially during 2020-2025). Floors and ceilings are stated against the landscape as of brief publication and require re-baselining at each application cycle.
- That Phase B-D figures are net to the project. The figures are stated gross of fiscal-sponsor fees (5–15% per Section 10.2, often 10–15% at established sponsors). Net-to-project receipt is reduced by the routing fee. Under the [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md) deferral, the fiscal-sponsor fee persists indefinitely as permanent operational overhead rather than being replaced by foundation overhead after incorporation — except if the D0016 trigger activates and the project incorporates, at which point the fee is replaced by foundation overhead per Section 10.4. NLnet's grantee-sponsorship model is the exception — it sponsors its own grantees and avoids the routing fee, making it the lowest-friction first route.
- That foundation incorporation will happen at any specific point. Per [D0016](decisions/D0016-foundation-incorporation-trigger.md), incorporation is deferred with a v1.5 broader-release evaluation trigger; trigger non-activation is the expected outcome under several plausible operational trajectories. The structural mitigations the foundation would enable (formalized Safe Harbor, board-bound governance, formal partner advisory authority) may remain at stated-intent posture indefinitely.
- That hardware availability for the BYOD pilot model will be consistent across all candidate-user jurisdictions. Section 10.1's BYOD posture depends on GrapheneOS-capable Pixel devices remaining available through standard commercial channels in the user's region; the project's small loaner pool (2–4 devices) is the operational mitigation for short-term availability gaps but not for jurisdictional channel closures.
- The §9.1-committed self-funding runway in calendar terms. §9.1 explicitly stated that Section 10 would deliver this figure as "the financial floor of all v1 funding-related risk discussions." This draft of Section 10 does not. Funders evaluating Phase A duration risk and Phase B funding-window timing should request the runway figure directly from the developer; the brief does not currently commit a number, and Section 10.7's "All Phase B funding fails for an extended period" failure mode is therefore bounded against an unstated rather than a stated horizon. Closing this gap is named explicitly so the §9.1-to-§10 commitment is not silently breached.

Funders evaluating the brief should treat Phase A items as the project's unilateral commitment (developer time, no funding required), Phase B as the smallest grant outcome that materially advances the project, Phase C as the funding event that unlocks broader release, and Phase D as the ongoing operational picture once the foundation is operating. The brief's purpose at the funding level is to give partners and funders an honest map of what dollars do for the project; it is not a fundraising target with a deadline attached.

### 10.9 Current funding pursuit posture

The brief is not the application. The brief is the document an application references. The project's current pursuit posture at brief publication, named here so funders evaluating fit can act on it directly:

- **Pre-brief-completion period.** The project is preparing the brief for first sharing with partner organizations (per Section 8.6, Q5 outreach) before submitting any formal grant applications. Phase A continues at the volunteer baseline.
- **First application targets.** When the brief is ready to share, the project's first formal applications target (in approximate order): the OTF Secure Audit programme for the pre-pilot audit (Phase B item, scaled to OTF's $30,000-150,000 award range; ~6 month cycle); NLnet NGI Zero Trust for combined legal-consultation-plus-pilot-period-honoraria coverage if eligible (NLnet's grantee-sponsorship avoids the fiscal-sponsor onboarding gap that affects OTF); Cure53 mission-rate engagement as a parallel route if OTF or NLnet do not close on the audit subsidy.
- **Direct-grant approach.** Direct large-grant applications (Ford, OSF, OTF main, NLnet main calls) are deferred until partner-organization conversations produce co-applicants. The brief's Section 8.6 partnership intent is the prerequisite for direct-grant credibility; pursuing direct grants without partners would weaken the application case.
- **Per-phase deliverables and reporting.** When grant funding closes, the project commits to milestone-based deliverable structures appropriate to the grant agreement. Standard milestones for Phase B (audit firm engagement; audit kickoff; draft report receipt; remediation status; pilot kickoff) match grant administration patterns at OTF, NLnet, and Mozilla. The project commits to quarterly progress reports to funders during active grant periods, with annual financial-and-impact summaries post-incorporation. Public-vs-funder-private boundaries follow the transparency-log architecture in Section 5.5 — what the project publishes publicly and what is shared with funders under grant-agreement confidentiality is drawn cleanly at the report-distribution boundary.
- **Reporting boundaries the project commits to.** Audit reports per Section 8.5 published on the transparency log alongside releases. Trust-roots health reporting per Section 9.4 surfaces dependency-state information funders can use to evaluate ongoing project posture. The project does not commit to funder-private reporting that would conflict with the public transparency commitments elsewhere in the brief.
- **What this section does not promise.** A timeline for first grant award. Specific instrument-by-instrument acceptance probabilities. The project's pursuit prioritization may revise as conversations with partner organizations produce information the brief does not currently have.

---

## Appendix A: Technical Decisions and Rationale

_Purpose: index the project's documented architectural and operational decisions so readers can navigate the rationale, alternatives considered, and references for any specific decision. The decision documents themselves live in `docs/decisions/`; this appendix is the navigation surface over them._

### A.0 The decision-document pattern

Every architecturally or operationally consequential decision in the brief is recorded as a separate ADR-style document in `docs/decisions/` with a uniform structure:

- **Status** (Accepted / Superseded / Withdrawn)
- **Date**
- **Context** (the question and what surfaced it)
- **Decision** (what was chosen and the specifics)
- **Alternatives considered** (each with rationale for rejection or partial adoption)
- **Consequences** (effects on the brief, on operations, on open questions, plus reversibility notes)
- **References** (related decisions, open questions, prior reviews, external sources)

The intent is that reviewers can engage with the choices rather than just the conclusions. When a decision is revised, the original document is preserved with status "Superseded" and a new document records the revision; the chain is auditable. Decisions in this register reflect the project's working understanding at the date stated and may revise as evidence accumulates — particularly D0010 (foundation jurisdiction, explicitly a placeholder pending legal consultation) and decisions interlocked with open questions still under partner conversation.
